# Secondhand Thoughts

# Chapter 1

The man at the turnip stall was going to leave his wife.

Not today. Probably not this year. But Anya Forger could hear him building up to it while she stood three stalls away pretending to examine pears, and the level of rehearsal was impressive in a miserable kind of way. He'd run through the speech four times since she'd come within range. Each version was worse. The current draft included the phrase "I deserve to feel alive," which, no. Nobody delivered that line out loud and left the conversation with any dignity. She almost wanted to help him edit.

*Listen, Gerda, we've had a good run, but I think we both know things have changed.*

She didn't help. She was working.

Berlint Central Market spread through three city blocks between the diplomatic quarter and the government district, a maze of canvas canopies and wooden stalls that thinned in autumn but never fully closed. The afternoon crowd was thick enough to be useful: tourists consulting pocket maps, pensioners comparing vegetable prices with the exhaustive seriousness of arms negotiators, mothers with children who ran at shin height and thought about nothing except wanting things. Anya held her position by the pear display and waited for her contact.

Turnip Man's rehearsal continued in the background, layered under a woman in a brown coat who was composing an indignant letter to her landlord about the mold in the bathroom. *It's been four months, Herr Dietrich. I don't think it's unreasonable to ask.* Two stalls over, a teenager was replaying an argument with his mother and losing, even inside his own head. Behind Anya, a man was thinking about bratwurst with a focus that bordered on worship.

This was the noise. It was always the noise. Sixty, maybe seventy minds within range, roughly twenty meters in every direction, give or take, and every one of them talking at once, none of them aware anyone could hear. Anya couldn't turn it off. Hadn't been able to since she was four, when the first thought she'd ever registered was a nursery teacher's exhausted prayer for nap time. Whatever anyone within range was actively thinking arrived in her head, in their voice, at their volume, whether she wanted it or not.

She mostly didn't want it.

The contact was late. His codename was Kinglet, a mid-level courier, nothing remarkable, just a man who picked things up and left things behind and worried about it the entire time. Anya had been watching the dead-drop bench near the fountain for seventeen minutes, and in that time she'd learned that the pretzel vendor across the way had a sciatica problem he was managing with anger and a philosophy about the proper ratio of coarse salt to fine salt that she would never fully forget.

Somewhere in the crowd, faint and easily missed, a woman was thinking about her son. *If his unit gets redeployed to the eastern sector again, I'll drag him home myself.* Anya let it pass. Background static. Everyone was worrying about something.

*Should've worn the grey jacket. The grey doesn't wrinkle. Why do I never think about that before I leave? Okay. Focus. The newspaper is on the bench. Walk naturally. Am I walking naturally? This doesn't feel natural.*

Kinglet, coming up from the south before she even spotted him. Standard courier anxiety. He was a nervous man doing his nervous job. Anya had worked with worse.

She scanned outward. The crowd noise was a wall of competing voices, but she'd been doing this long enough to know what she was listening for. Trained minds sounded different from the usual churn. Not calmer. Tighter. They snagged on the same object and kept circling back.

There. East side of the market, near the cheese stall. *Bench in sight. Don't look at the bench. Wait for hands. If he rubs his jaw again, look away.* Nobody buying cheese thought in tactical fragments.

A second thread cut in from the south before she finished locating the first. *South lane clear. Let him drift west. Once he moves, once he commits, then go.*

Two of them. One holding the bench, one shaping the exit. Professional enough to think in routes instead of groceries. If Kinglet touched the paper, he would not be taking the walk back alone.

The pears blurred in front of her. Okay. Okay, think.

Kinglet was ten meters from the bench. He was still on the grey jacket. Still nowhere near the right problem. He was going to sit down, take the newspaper, and step straight into the route they were building for him.

Anya put the pear down. She'd been holding it for seventeen minutes and hadn't paid for it, but she also hadn't bitten it. So that was probably fine. It didn't matter. She started walking.

The first problem was communication. She had no earpiece to Kinglet. This was a passive surveillance assignment. She was there to observe the dead-drop and confirm the exchange, not to manage the handoff. She didn't have a signal protocol with him because she wasn't supposed to need one. The second problem was that she couldn't explain to anyone how she knew the contact was tailed, because the answer was *I read their minds* and that was a sentence she had never said out loud to anyone in her life.

So she'd improvise. She was bad at plans. She was genuinely good at the part where the plan was already ruined.

She cut between stalls, moving fast, angling toward the fountain. Behind her, Turnip Man's resolve was collapsing. *Maybe I won't mention the car. The car is a whole thing. If I bring up the car she'll throw something.*

*Hands on the paper. Move.*

That one hit hard enough to kick her into a run.

She reached Kinglet just as he was bending toward the bench. She caught his elbow, laughed too loudly, and said, "Oh my God, I'm so sorry. I thought you were someone else. You look exactly like my friend's cousin. Wow, that's embarrassing." She gripped his arm hard enough to make the point.

His arm locked under her hand. *What? Who is she? Her hand is on my arm. This is not protocol. There is no contact protocol for this.*

"Don't sit down," she said through her smile. "Walk with me right now. You've got two on you."

He made the mistake of glancing toward the bench. Anya pinched the inside of his elbow hard enough to make him flinch and kept laughing like they'd just shared a private joke.

"No, really," she said at full social volume. "I cannot believe I did that."

He went white. Not a little pale. Actually white, the blood dropping out of his face like water through a drain. *This is not my job. This is not my job.* He was a courier. He filed paperwork and left newspapers on benches and occasionally met someone in a parking structure. The panic coming off him was high and thin enough that Anya had to push through it like wading in mud.

She kept the smile, kept the grip, and steered him north into the densest part of the market, where the stalls pressed close and the crowd moved slow.

*Who is she with him? Asset? North side. Go.* So they'd clocked her. Somewhere behind them another mind slammed into the flower stall and lost visual hard.

Anya did not need the full plan. North first, then a wide cut west if they lost visual. She turned left, pulling Kinglet into a narrow gap between a bakery stand and a man selling secondhand books who was thinking, with great bitterness, about a woman named Elke.

"Keep up," she murmured.

"What is happening?" His voice shook. He was twenty years older than her and his hands were trembling and hers were steady and she didn't have time to feel anything about that asymmetry right now.

*Blue canopy. Small woman, courier, north lane. Too tight in here. Take the west mouth. Catch them there.* The thought skimmed across her from somewhere back and left.

West exit. Anya went east.

"Why are we going this way?"

"Because they're expecting west."

"How do you know that?"

"I'm good at reading situations."

She pulled him through a row of vegetable crates. Behind them, the first tail had committed to the west exit, moving on the only clean guess he had left. He was good. He was also going to be standing at the wrong exit in about forty seconds.

The second tail was trying to close from the south, but the crowd had thickened. A knot of tourists had stopped to photograph a flower stall, blocking the aisle like a clot in an artery. Anya used the gap. She steered Kinglet past a display of jarred honey, through a service lane between stalls that smelled like overripe apples and cardboard, and out through a slit in the canvas onto the market's east-side service street.

Kinglet stumbled after her. "You said west."

"I said they expected west."

The service street ran behind the outer line of stalls, narrow and half blocked by crates and delivery carts. Anya turned north first, keeping the bulk of the market between them and the main street. At the next cross-lane she cut west behind a row of produce sheds, the tarps and stacked boxes hiding them from anyone waiting at the obvious exit.

A fruit cart sat crooked in the cut-through, wooden and heavy, loaded with late-season plums. A boy of about sixteen was leaning against it, thinking about a girl named Sofie with a level of romantic anguish that was almost operationally distracting.

Anya bumped the cart with her hip as she passed. Not hard. Just enough to shift it, send a cascade of plums tumbling across the cobblestones behind them, and turn the whole lane into a mess that anyone following would have to fight through.

The boy's panic detonated. *The plums. Oh shit, oh shit, the plums, my uncle is going to kill me.*

Behind them a second mind snagged on the mess. *Obstruction. Lost them. Could be anywhere back here.*

By the time the first tail reached the west exit, he was already too late. *She went east first. Looping. How did she know that?* The thought clipped her just as she and Kinglet came around the market's north-west corner and cut toward the street that fed into the diplomatic quarter.

Once they crossed into the Westalian mission's orbit, pursuit became a political problem no professional tail wanted to create. The men peeled off. They always did at that line.

Anya let go of Kinglet's arm.

Her head was throbbing. She'd been reading forty-plus minds at full intensity for almost twenty minutes, and the noise had been stacking up behind her eyes like pressure from a fever. Fatigue loaded her arms and legs with that wrong, boneless drag she only got from too much listening. She'd need to stop soon. Sit somewhere quiet. Let the noise thin.

But not yet.

Kinglet was leaning against a lamppost, breathing through his mouth. His jacket was wrinkled, the wrong jacket, and his forehead was slick. His thoughts were a wreck of relief and leftover terror. Under both sat one clean fact: he was never doing this again.

"You're fine," Anya told him. She pushed her hair back from her face, fingers brushing the familiar spot near her ears, and said it again. "You're fine. We're clear."

Kinglet dragged air into his lungs like he had to remember how. "The paper."

"Stays there."

"The film-"

"Is gone if you go back for it and worse than gone if you get seen trying." She kept her voice level because his panic was still loud enough to make her teeth hurt. "Walk north two blocks. Buy something useless. Circle once. Report the compromise from a clean phone."

He stared at her. *Who gave her authority to-*

"Move," she said.

He moved. He went north fast, shoulders up, trying to look like a man hurrying home instead of one who had nearly taken a surveillance team back to WISE.

The microfilm was still on the bench. The newspaper was unrecovered. That was a loss, but a recoverable one. The dead-drop site was burned now, but the intelligence could be rerouted through another channel.

Kinglet looked at her with an expression she'd seen before on people she'd pulled out of bad situations: gratitude layered over something less comfortable, the awareness that a woman twenty years his junior had just handled a crisis he'd frozen inside.

"Thank you," he said.

"Don't use that dead-drop site again."

"I won't."

"And next time, wear the grey jacket. It doesn't wrinkle."

His thought flashed once, startled and small. *How does she know that?*

But she was already walking away, and he was already out of range, and she was very, very tired.

---

Forty minutes later, in a room that smelled like institutional carpet and a pot that had outlived any reasonable definition of freshness, Anya sat in the third row of a WISE briefing and tried not to read anyone's mind for five consecutive minutes.

She failed before she finished the thought.

*If this runs past six I'll miss the opening number. She's been practicing the Chopin for three weeks. She's got the dynamics down now. If I'm not there in the front row she'll look for me first.* Two seats to her left, Reinhardt was already halfway to the recital hall.

Somewhere else Brewer was trying to build dinner out of eggs, bread, and whether they were out of laundry soap. Directly behind Anya, because the universe kept making choices, Berg had reached the part of his crush where Sylvia adjusting her glasses counted as a private event. *The line of her jaw when she's making a point.* Anya had endured this since spring. She knew more about what Berg found attractive about Sylvia Sherwood than any person alive, including Berg, and she would have paid real money to stop knowing.

The briefing room was on the third floor of a building in the Westalian diplomatic quarter that, from the outside, looked like a modest commercial property. Inside, it was fluorescent lights and ventilation hum and the particular carpet that government offices shared across every nation and era, beige, resilient, hostile to personality. The pot on the credenza had been brewed that morning by Sylvia, who did not replace it before 1600 hours because wasting coffee offended a principle she had never articulated and that no one in the department had ever been brave enough to question.

Sylvia stood at the front of the room in a grey blazer and a collared shirt that together said *I am here to work and you should be too*. Reading glasses on the chain around her neck. Short grey-blonde hair that she cut, Anya suspected, at the same place on the same schedule every six weeks, because Sylvia did not adjust things that were functioning. Her face showed thirty years of difficult decisions made in rooms like this one. Whatever habit of apology she'd once had was long gone.

"The pattern is consistent with covert operational activity, not standard political maneuvering," Sylvia said. Her diction was precise. She did not use contractions when she briefed, and her sentences arrived pre-structured, situation, assessment, directive, as if she had formatted them before opening her mouth. "We are looking at a structured campaign inside Ostania's security apparatus. The scope suggests an organized effort with specific objectives and an active timeline."

She touched the map pinned to the briefing wall. Red markers clustered near the Ostanian government district, east of center.

"The operative or operatives are unknown. The purpose is unknown. What we do know is this: the activity pattern is consistent with pre-conflict positioning. Weapons procurement channels that had been dormant for several years have been reactivated. Military intelligence sharing between Ostanian departments has been restricted in a manner that suggests compartmentalization for a specific project." She paused, letting it breathe for exactly long enough to register as serious without tipping into theater. "We do not yet have a clear picture of the objective. But the indicators are serious enough to warrant dedicated investigation."

*I wonder if there'll be parking near the recital hall.* Reinhardt again, drifting on schedule.

Behind Anya, Berg had moved on to the way Sylvia held a pen.

From the back row came Falk, quieter than either of them and harder to shake once he got in. *Red pins on a wall, same quarterly panic. Sherwood means it, probably. The numbers still bend around whatever keeps the lights on.*

Falk sat near the wall in a cardigan that could have been brown or grey depending on the light. He was a senior analyst, easy to miss in a room like this if you weren't listening. Anya had heard variations of that complaint from older staff before. She let it pass and turned back to Sylvia.

"Forger." Sylvia's gaze found her with the directness of someone who did not scan a room but targeted a specific person. "You will take point on identification and assessment. Your diplomatic cover provides access to Ostanian state functions. Use it. I want a preliminary assessment within ten days."

Berg was still stuck on the fact that Sylvia always picked Forger for the exposed ones, equal parts admiration and resentment and entirely not Anya's concern.

"Understood," she said.

By then Reinhardt had moved on from parking to flowers and was fully inhabiting the evening ahead of him.

Anya bit the inside of her cheek and did not smile.

The briefing ended with Sylvia collecting her notes in the precise order she always collected them, map last, folded into thirds. People stood, gathered their files, began the small post-meeting exchanges that were half professional and half procrastination. Sylvia left the room without lingering, because Sylvia did not linger.

"Forger. Stay a moment."

Chairs scraped. Berg's attention snagged once more on Sylvia's hands as she stacked her notes; Reinhardt was already calculating recital traffic. When the room emptied, Sylvia set the folded map on the table and slid a thin packet across to Anya.

"Event calendars. Guest lists. Start with the rooms big enough to hide you," she said. "Smaller committees remember faces. State functions remember titles. I want patterns before I want conclusions."

Anya took the packet. "That's your way of making a room sound boring."

"It is my way of keeping you employed." Sylvia hooked her glasses onto the chain at her neck. "Foreign Affairs is hosting a state dinner later this week. Your cover gets you in without strain. Start there."

She tapped the packet with two fingers. "I want who circles whom, who leaves the room together, who stops talking when the wrong person arrives. If the room gives you gossip, bring me gossip. Gossip becomes structure faster than analysts like to admit."

"High floor to open on."

"High noise floor," Sylvia corrected. "If Briar is on security, you will be forgettable."

"And if the room offers something operationally interesting?"

"You remember it," Sylvia said. "You do not improve it. I do not need improvisation at a diplomatic dinner."

"That seems hostile."

"It is practical."

During the briefing she'd caught the private line under Sylvia's public one: *Forger again. Untidy. Fast. Right. Loid had the same bad habit of moving before the forms caught up.* It was as close to affection as Sylvia's professional register permitted.

Anya stood and felt the headache from the market settle more firmly behind her right eye. The fluorescent lights were needles. She wanted, suddenly and badly, to be in a room with no one else in it. But her father was waiting for her at Cafe Linden, and there was nowhere within twenty meters of anywhere in Berlint that didn't have someone thinking in it.

---

Cafe Linden was a narrow room with dark wood tables and a window that hadn't been cleaned since the previous season. It occupied a side street between the diplomatic quarter and the residential neighborhoods south of center, close enough to WISE to be convenient, far enough to feel like somewhere else. Anya and Loid had been meeting here for two years. The waiter knew their order and had long ago stopped pretending he hadn't noticed they were always slightly too alert for a father and daughter meeting in public.

Loid was already seated when she arrived. His coffee sat untouched, a cherry strudel on the plate across from him, her favorite since she was eight. He never ordered one for himself. She'd asked about this once and he'd said he wasn't hungry, which had been a lie, because his thought at the time was *She runs too hard and she doesn't eat enough*, and the lie was so small and so fatherly that she'd let it sit.

He looked up when she came in. Forty-four. Blond hair with grey at the temples that had accelerated over the past two years in a way neither of them mentioned. Reading glasses folded beside his cup. He needed them for menus now, and for small print, and the first time she'd seen him put them on something had shifted her understanding of time in a way she hadn't been ready for. His face was kind in a way that was both genuine and the residue of a lifetime of cover identities, though by now the distinction between the real warmth and the performed warmth had dissolved so thoroughly that even Anya, reading him, couldn't always find the seam.

"How was the market?" he asked.

"Eventful." She sat down and pulled the strudel toward her. "Dead-drop was burned. Two tails on Kinglet, coordinated, somebody professional. I took him out through the east service lane and looped him around to the diplomatic zone."

His face didn't change. The hit of him did. *Two tails in a market. Alone?* Then the rest of him snapped into place around it: east lane was right, use the crowd, use the mission boundary, but alone? The spy's evaluation laid itself over the father's fear, not hiding it so much as giving it somewhere to stand.

"Smart route," he said. "Once you hit the diplomatic line, they were done."

"That was the idea." She took a bite of strudel. It was good. It was always good here.

"We'll reroute the recovery through an alternate channel. The microfilm isn't time-sensitive." He took a careful sip with the unhurried precision of a man who measured his gestures the way other people measured their words. "What about the new assignment?"

"Sylvia wants me in Ostanian rooms for the next ten days," Anya said. "Enough of them that if Yuri works security on the bigger ones, he's going to start seeing the same face too often."

He set the cup down. The gesture was careful, which meant he was interested.

*Yuri logs faces. Guest books. The same attaché twice and someone pulls the file. Start her big. Keep it social. She's twenty.* His thought moved fast through ministry floors, security rotations, reception lines, which rooms were crowded enough to hide her and which would make her stick.

"You'll need sustained access to Ostanian government functions," he said. "Your attaché cover is solid, but repeated presence at high-level events draws pattern recognition. Vary the attendance. Some cultural, some diplomatic, some social. Give them a profile that looks enthusiastic rather than strategic."

"Boring but visible."

"Exactly." A small smile, and then the thought behind it: *Sharp kid. Too young for those rooms. Can't follow her into any of them now.* He cut himself off there, hard enough that the silence of it landed in her.

She looked down at the plate and busied herself with her fork. This was the arrangement, though only one of them knew it existed. He worried about her in the privacy of his own mind, and she let him believe it was private, because telling him otherwise would mean explaining everything, and explaining everything was the one thing she had never been able to do.

"I'll be careful," she said. She was answering a thought he hadn't spoken, and she'd gotten very good at making that look like normal conversation.

He reached for the sugar dispenser, thought better of it, and pulled a paper napkin into the space between them instead. With the pen from his coat pocket he drew three small boxes in a row, then crossed out the first.

"Not the museum lunch," he said.

"Too small?"

"Too still. Ten people in a room that size remember everybody." He tapped the middle box. "Foreign Affairs dinner later this week. That is where you start. Ministers pull attention. Staff drift. A junior cultural attaché can move through a room like that without anyone building a file unless she gives them a reason."

"That's Sylvia's first choice too."

"Then Sylvia is right." He drew an arrow to the third box. "After that, something harmless. A school grant reception. A museum board thing. Break the pattern before you go back into a government room."

Anya tore off a piece of strudel. "So Marta Kramer becomes deeply interested in exchange programs and chamber music."

"In public, yes." He slid the napkin a little closer to her. "Ask boring questions. Arts funding. Youth initiatives. Which orchestra toured where. People dismiss what they think is decorative."

"I hate using that."

"I know. Use it anyway."

"Also eat before you go."

She blinked. "That's operational advice?"

"It's advice." He glanced at the plate between them. "If you're reading half a ballroom, you do not want to do it on sugar and an empty stomach."

"I resent how specific that is."

"Then stop making it accurate."

She looked down at the boxes. "And if Yuri's on the door?"

"Give him one neat answer and no second puzzle." His tone stayed mild. "Invisible is wrong with officers like him. Invisible makes them curious. Boring is safer."

"That's depressing."

"It's useful."

He tapped the third box. "And do not wear the same thing twice in a row to the same cluster of officials. Pattern recognition does not need perfect memory. It needs one clerk with a stubborn face."

"Now you're doing fashion."

"I'm doing surveillance from the other end."

He turned the napkin and wrote three more words under the middle box: arrive late enough, leave early enough.

"Crowded rooms forgive timing if your reason is dull," he said. "Traffic. A prior reception. A headache. Do not be the last person still there when staff starts noticing who remains."

She nodded. "No lurking near ministers like someone obviously lurking near ministers."

"That would help." The corner of his mouth moved. "You get one real read per event. Do not chase a second because it feels available."

"That sounds personal."

"It sounds like a pattern in your work." He recapped the pen and tucked it away. "You do not have to win the room in one night."

They kept going after that, down into smaller details: which coat looked diplomatic instead of memorable, how long to stay before an early exit became suspicious, which parts of Marta Kramer were safest to exaggerate. Eventually the operational talk thinned and he mentioned security protocols at state functions, then Yor trying a new stew recipe and how she should come home for dinner soon, and the sentence was simultaneously a father's invitation and an operative's wellness check and she couldn't untangle the two, and she didn't want to, because the tangled version was the true one.

They sat for a while after that. The operational conversation wound down and something quieter replaced it, the version of them that existed in the gaps between work, where he was just her father and she was just his daughter and neither of them was particularly good at knowing what to do with that.

"How's the apartment?" he asked.

"Fine. The radiator makes a noise."

"What kind of noise?"

"A sort of... ticking? When it comes on."

"That's the pipe expanding. It's normal." He said this with the absolute certainty of a man who had once maintained six separate cover identities across two countries and who now, in semi-retirement, channeled the same operational precision into home repair. "If it starts banging, that's a different issue. Call the building manager."

"Okay."

"Or call me. I can look at it."

"You'd drive forty minutes to look at my radiator."

"I'd drive forty minutes to make sure your radiator isn't going to rupture a pipe in the middle of the night, yes." His face was straight. His thought was not. *Living alone. Fine. Normal. She's twenty. People do this every day.* The thought blurred after that into something less structured, less analytical, just a father's awareness that his daughter was out in the world and he couldn't follow her there.

"I'll call if it bangs," she said.

"Good." He picked up the cup, found it empty, and set it down again. "Your mother's making stew tonight. New recipe. You should come by sometime this week."

The way he said *new recipe* carried a warning she didn't need telepathy to decode. Yor's culinary experiments were acts of love that frequently crossed the line into acts of violence against ingredients. But the stew was real and the invitation was real and the kitchen would smell like burning and hope, and she missed it.

"Maybe Thursday," she said.

She paid because he always forgot to bring cash. He didn't forget. It was part of the routine, one of a hundred small rituals that had accumulated over fourteen years of a cover family that stopped being cover somewhere along the way.

Outside, Berlint was going amber. Early autumn evening, the sun low enough to turn the west-facing windows into sheets of dull gold. The air had a crisp edge, summer's warmth gone, winter still distant, the city held in the kind of cool that made you walk faster without deciding to. Leaves on the cobblestones. A tram rattling down Gartenstraße, its bell sounding twice as it rounded the corner.

Anya walked south toward home.

The thoughts came without effort. She was tired, and tired meant the filter she'd spent twenty years building was thinner. The range didn't shrink, but the noise got louder, less organized, a layered hum of voices she hadn't asked to hear.

A woman closing her shop pulled down the metal shutters in the same rhythm as *Elsa hasn't called. Tuesday, always Tuesday. The news said delays at the eastern checkpoints. If something happened they would call. Somebody would call.* Anya walked past and the worry thinned behind her.

A student with dead headphones came the other direction carrying conscription math like a fever. *If they bring conscription back, do university exemptions still count? Lehmann said yes, but Lehmann failed civics.* He was nineteen, maybe twenty, and already arguing with a letter that did not exist.

The shopkeeper two doors down from him was already in next month. *Another month like this and imported stock is gone. Frau Dittmar wants the order Friday. Korsch from the south maybe. If he can get through.* He rattled the handle twice after he locked it, as if the door could solve any part of it.

An old man on a bench gave her pigeons, cold, and Ilse in no order she would have chosen. Anya did not want that one either. She got it anyway.

She turned onto her own street. Quieter here, a residential block with three or four minds within range. A neighbor with the television too loud. A child on an upper floor, drawing, thinking the colors: *Green. No, the darker green. The one that looks like trees.*

Somewhere nearby, a dog was dreaming in short sensory pulses. Warmth. A hand. Food.

None of them knew she had already been inside their heads.

Anya stood on the sidewalk with her key in her hand. From the next building came a tired, careful thought. *After tea. Pills after tea. Not on an empty stomach this time.* Upstairs, the child settled on the darker green. The key teeth bit into her palm. She crossed to her building, got the door open, and went inside before the next stranger could say anything else in her head.


# Chapter 2

A few days later, the car left her two blocks from the Foreign Affairs Ministry. That was standard practice. A private sedan disappeared into evening traffic, and the district swallowed it without comment. There were no diplomatic plates lingering at the curb and no driver to remember a face.

Anya paid him, stepped onto the sidewalk, and took the city in for one second before the gates.

Berlint at dusk looked almost forgiving. Streetlights were coming up under a flat grey sky. Leaves scraped along the pavement in thin circles. The air carried wet stone, car exhaust, and the sweeter diesel stink from the government lot behind the Ministry. Somewhere back the way she had come, a woman argued with a child about ice cream in October. *He'll get sick. Fine. Maybe one scoop. Damn it.* The thought hit first, then thinned out as distance took it.

Maybe later, for normal people. Right now she had to be fake-diplomat people.

Her credentials sat in a slim wallet inside the clutch: Marta Kramer, junior cultural attache, Westalian mission. The cover had depth: payroll, office keys, a desk on the third floor with two locked drawers and a drooping plant she kept almost killing, a supervisor who thought Marta Kramer did decent work when she remembered deadlines and terrible work when she remembered them too late. Boring was expensive to build. WISE had built her boring properly.

Two SSS officers stood at the entrance under the Ministry crest. The taller one checked her papers against the guest list. The shorter one watched her face with professional boredom and a bad lower back.

*Division with remainders again. Mina's going to cry and I won't get home before bedtime and Nora will be furious.*

"Attache Kramer." The taller man handed the papers back. "Main gallery. Second doors on the left."

"Thank you."

Heat met her in the foyer. So did the event.

It wasn't just the general fact of a crowd. It was this crowd. Diplomatic minds came lacquered over: full sentences rehearsed into shape, talking points laid in rows, grudges dressed for dinner. Under that sat the real mess: an aide counting chairs and enemies, a deputy minister thinking about his mistress and a fisheries vote in the same breath, security officers running faces, doors, and distances, spouses sorting one another into triumphs and mistakes by fabric alone. Champagne loosened the stitching. Warmth loosened it further. By the time she crossed the marble, people's thoughts were already slipping out through the seams.

Breathe. Don't snatch at everything. Don't be stupid.

She smoothed the front of her dress, dark blue and respectable, the sort of dress a junior cultural attache wore when she wanted to look serious and forgettable, and walked into the ballroom.

The Ministry did state dinners the way it did everything else: with marble, chandeliers, and the steady suggestion that everyone present ought to feel honored. Warm gold light sat on the floor. White linens glowed under crystal. A quartet near the windows played a Baroque piece nobody cared about. Waiters moved through the room with champagne, wine, and a pale nonalcoholic drink nobody wanted. Ministry catering meant the food would look official and taste tired.

Warm rooms made sloppy thinking. So did alcohol. So did people standing too close to one another and pretending not to notice. Pressure gathered behind her left eye almost at once.

Rule one: keep moving.
Rule two: let the useful thought trip over you by accident.
Rule three: do not puke on foreign policy.

A waiter passed. Anya took a glass of champagne and let herself drift just slowly enough to look social.

Deputy Minister Werfel saw her first and brightened in a way that had nothing to do with diplomacy. "Attache Kramer. Good to see you again."

"Deputy Minister Werfel. How was the recital?"

His face opened. *She remembered. Elena will be ridiculous about this.*

"Wonderful. One missed note in the Handel, but she recovered beautifully."

"I missed six notes the last time I touched a piano, so that sounds heroic."

Werfel laughed, and the exchange stayed easy. He kept talking about his daughter's bow hold and the National Gallery exhibit and whether the ministry had overfunded a restoration nobody would admit liking. Anya nodded in the right places and let the room keep muttering around him.

*Schiller can sign it himself if he wants the appropriation that badly.*

*That is the Trade Minister's wife's dress. No, worse, it's almost the same dress.*

*The pork is dry. How is pork dry in gravy?*

Then a sharper line caught.

*If Defense punts again, south gate sits another week.*

Anya did not look at the source yet. Looking was how you told on yourself.

Werfel was still talking. "I always think modernists improve when they're slightly underfunded. Desperation helps."

"That's comforting. I've been underfunded for years."

He laughed again, took another sip of wine while she took champagne, and another slip came from somewhere near the windows.

*Three weeks. Four if Kleist keeps hiding behind procedure.*

A checkpoint, a timeline, and a name. It wasn't much, but it was something.

She let her attention widen by inches instead of jerking toward it. The woman wore a charcoal suit, looked to be in her mid-fifties, held white wine in her left hand, and never actually drank it. Her thumb tapped once against the stem each time somebody crossed between her and the annex tables. Anya kept her face on Werfel and stored the picture.

"You Westalians are all pretending to like abstraction," he said. "It's a national vice."

"Not all of us. Some of us are pretending to understand it."

That got her another pleased burst from him and a second, more dangerous current from two bodies over.

*Civilian line item on top this time, idiot. Do you want Muller asking why concrete costs like ammunition?*

It was General Brennan, border infrastructure command, thick-necked and excellently tailored, with a mind full of columns and irritation.

*Liaison allocation. Keep it in liaison. Don't put uniforms anywhere near paper.*

It wasn't clean and it wasn't enough. But now it rhymed: south gate, Kleist, civilian line, money pushed sideways.

A cultural secretary from the Ostanian side drifted up before she could peel away. He had a damp upper lip and a folder tucked under one arm because some people would rather die than arrive anywhere without paperwork.

"Attache Kramer. The film week proposal."

Of course it was.

"Yes?"

"Your mission still wants the Murnau posters in the smaller gallery, not the foyer?"

*Please say yes. Please say yes so Culture stops calling me at home.*

"Smaller gallery," Anya said at once. "The foyer eats anything delicate."

Relief showed at once. "Good. Excellent."

"Though if you give us the foyer instead," she added, because Marta Kramer would complain just a little, "I'll still write a beautiful thank-you note."

He almost smiled. "I'll keep that in mind."

He moved on. Werfel got claimed by a trade delegate. Anya finally had room to breathe, which lasted about six seconds before two new thoughts clipped past each other so close they practically sparked.

*Why is Brennan pretending he doesn't know the number?*

*If Griesler folds early, half the room starts forgetting who invited whom.*

She shifted toward the long side of the ballroom and picked up a plate from a passing tray because empty hands invited questions. She ended up with a cheese puff, anchovy toast, and something with a mushroom cap pretending to be elegant. She ate the cheese puff on the move. Salt helped.

Near the center of the room Brennan stood with three men who all looked as if they enjoyed using the word strategic. Numbers rolled through his mind in hard clean blocks until his sciatica barged in over them.

*Hotel chair tonight and I swear to God I'll set the place on fire.*

Apparently the state ran on hidden accounts and bad backs.

So Brennan wasn't the whole answer. Maybe he wasn't even the main one.

She circled wide, letting the event carry her. A deputy's wife mentally drafted a letter to a school headmistress. An undersecretary tried not to think about the ulcer starting under his ribs. A young diplomat composed an apology text to a woman he had definitely lied to. Anya yanked away from that one so fast she nearly clipped a waiter with her shoulder.

*God. No. Not that one.*

The room would not become useful just because she needed it to. It stayed full of everybody else's garbage while she hunted for the one thread that mattered.

At the far end of the ballroom, just beyond the first line of tables, the charcoal-suited woman had turned enough for Anya to catch her profile. She had an administrative face, the kind nobody remembered and everybody regretted underestimating.

*If he asks for the guest log again, tell him I never had it.*

It still wasn't a who and it still wasn't enough. But now there was a he.

Anya took another half-step. The woman's gaze flicked, not to Brennan this time, but to the annex doorway where three retired party men in black suits were pretending not to huddle. One of them laughed with his mouth and not his eyes.

*Desmond should know better than to ask in the open.*

There it was.

But which one? Demetrius, Damian, Donovan himself by proxy? The Desmond name covered too much ground to be useful alone.

A low chime rang from the doors. Staff began steering the room toward tables with the gentle aggression only government hospitality perfected.

Fine, then. She would sit down and watch what rose when people got trapped with food and protocol.

Her place card put her one rank too far from importance. That was good for invisibility and bad for access. Table fourteen sat off to the left of the central aisle, close enough to see the dais and the annex doors if she angled her chair, far enough that nobody important would stay long.

The seating mix looked deliberate in the ugly Ministry way. Anya between a deputy from cultural preservation and the widow of a deceased rail minister, across from a deputy legal adviser, one SSS liaison in formal dress, and Colonel Dreher, retired, who wore his age the way some men wore medals: as proof that time had failed to beat them properly.

The widow spoke first. "Westalis sends its young women to do all the work now, does it?"

Anya smiled. "Only the women who can survive committee minutes."

That got a wheezing laugh, and just enough approval to buy silence for a minute.

Soup arrived. It tasted like warm apology. The quartet kept playing. A vice minister rose to welcome everyone and think resentfully about his tailor while saying peace and dialogue and mutual respect into a microphone.

At the next table, a man whose hair looked shellacked into patriotism checked the annex doors every thirty seconds.

*He was in there twice already. God forbid the boy ever just eat his soup.*

Anya lowered her eyes to the bowl and listened harder.

*You want an attendance list, go ask scheduling. You want April fourteen, ask someone else.*

Across from her, the deputy legal adviser thought in dense little knots.

*No military signatures. That's the entire point. Civilian board, civilian purchase, civilian exposure if it bursts.*

He lifted his spoon and said aloud, "The saffron is subtle."

Anya nearly laughed in his face.

Colonel Dreher had gone from politely indifferent to mildly annoyed at something across the room.

*Another circuit. Christ. He has Donovan's jaw and none of Donovan's patience.*

It still wasn't a name. But now there was a shape in the air: a younger Desmond making circuits, not eating, asking retired men about schedules and lists and things they didn't want to say in the ballroom.

The widow leaned toward Anya. "You know, I always think cultural affairs sounds restful, and then I remember artists."

"They're usually less dangerous than ministers," Anya said.

"Usually?"

*She's prettier than the last one. Shame about the hair.*

"Only usually," Anya said, because that was safer than responding to the rest.

Bread came. Fish came. The pork came later and proved the earlier complaint right even before she tasted it. Around the table people discussed restoration grants, the opening of a museum wing, fuel import negotiations they pretended were not fuel import negotiations, and a concert series nobody wanted to fund but everybody wanted their name on.

Underneath, the room kept leaking.

*South route. Not checkpoint. Stop calling it the checkpoint where anyone can hear you.*

*If Griesler takes the committee copy instead of the cleaned one, we're done.*

*He said forum, not committee. Forum. Why is he still on the forum?*

Useful and useless came through the same door: a philanderer worrying about lipstick on his collar, a woman silently reviewing whether she had over-salted tomorrow's roast, one security officer cataloguing exits, another thinking about hemorrhoids with enough misery to light up the whole room. By the time the main course landed, pain was beating behind both Anya's eyes.

She touched two fingers lightly to the side of her head, then dropped the hand before it became a tell.

The SSS liaison at the table noticed anyway. *Headache. Too much perfume in here.*

"You all right, Attache Kramer?"

"Perfectly diplomatic," she said.

He snorted into his wine.

At the far edge of the ballroom, a dark-haired man moved out from one retired cluster and into another with the calm of somebody pretending not to work very hard. Not tall enough to be Demetrius, she thought first. Then she checked that against what she actually knew and stopped being lazy. Height meant nothing from here. The angle was wrong. All she had was a dark suit, good posture, and the fact that older men who ignored everybody else kept letting him into the corners of their conversations.

The younger Desmond, Dreher had thought.

Anya cut a piece of pork and forced it down.

The vice minister moved from welcome remarks into a toast. Crystal rose around the room. So did the thoughts.

*Smile, smile, smile, then ask him who approved the reallocation.*

*If he says liaison again I'm leaving him to die.*

*Why is Donovan's son talking to Kleist's clerk?*

There it was. It wasn't just retired party fossils; administrative staff were in it too.

Anya drank. The champagne had gone flat.

Dreher followed her line of sight without moving his head. "A dreary breed, diplomats," he said aloud.

"Some of them," Anya said.

"No sense of timing."

*If the boy corners me after dessert, I'll tell him the same thing I told his father twenty years ago. Put it in writing or stop whining.*

That one hit hard enough that her hand tightened on the stem.

So it ran through his father: old Desmond channels, administrative nerves, south gate, civilian paperwork, a forum guest log. None of it was clean, but it was enough for her to smell the shape of something ugly.

Dessert came and went under speeches about heritage, trade, and the spiritual value of exchange programs. The widow of the rail minister confessed aloud that she hated custard. The deputy from cultural preservation admitted, after his third drink, that he thought most public sculpture should be quietly buried. The quartet changed tempo. Chairs loosened. Tables broke apart in waves.

If she waited much longer the room would dissolve into too many conversations at once and she'd lose the thread in the sludge.

She excused herself on the strength of needing air and stepped out into the crush between tables.

A minister's aide near the wall was composing the resignation letter he had been writing in his head for three years and would never send. An SSS officer near the annex doors was on shift change and hated the replacement's shoes. A woman in green had gotten sauce on her sleeve and wanted the earth to open. Then, under all of it, the cleaner line pulled again.

It wasn't dinner chatter or drunk vanity. It was focused, ordered, and running too fast.

Names and days kept flashing by, attached to a list that refused to stay in one place.

Anya turned toward the French doors at the far end and almost made it before Colonel Dreher cut across her path with a fresh drink and the stubbornness of a man who believed public space reorganized itself around him.

"You're with the Westalian mission, yes?"

"Cultural affairs." Her smile held. "Marta Kramer."

"Dreher. Retired, allegedly." He gave her a practiced look that had probably unsettled younger women for decades. "Enjoying the evening?"

*Westalian. Young. Alone. If they sent her to fish, they're getting lazy.*

"Very much."

"Good."

He did not move.

All right, fine. She could run two tracks.

"You seem skeptical," Anya said.

"I distrust any evening where they serve pork under gravy and call it generosity."

That almost got her. "Then we're aligned."

He studied her another second, then huffed once through his nose.

*If Desmond asks me about April again, he can freeze with the smokers for all I care.*

April again. That gave her something firmer.

Aloud he said, "Your people still trying to borrow our print collection?"

"Borrow is a harsh word. We prefer 'temporarily admire from abroad.'"

"Predatory."

He was enjoying himself now, which made escape possible. Anya used the opening the second he gave it, drifting past with a soft apology and a lift of her empty glass.

Cold hit the minute she stepped through the doors. Iron sconces threw weak light across the terrace stones. A couple argued in hushed vicious voices near the balustrade. An older man checked his watch with naked hatred. A woman smoked like she was stealing time from God. The air smelled of rain, tobacco, and wet stone cooling too fast.

And near the far railing, alone, stood the mind she had been following through half the room.

He wore a dark suit and held himself very straight, one hand around a drink he had barely touched, the other flat on the stone as if he had taught it calm by force. Even from a distance she could see he wasn't looking at the view. He was looking at the glass doors, the annex corner reflected in them, the route back in. He was working, not brooding.

The signal sharpened so fast it made her teeth hurt.

*Griesler first. Before month-end. If he panics and runs to committee, it stops being a review and starts being troops at the fence.*

Anya slowed without meaning to.

*Kleist tomorrow if his secretary lies again. Wednesday if I have to go through the father-name door.*

Another fragment, clipped and mean with fatigue.

*Need the forum list. Need the civilian names on the draft, not the cleaned copy.*

It still wasn't enough to hand anybody, but it was enough to turn her.

He shifted, just slightly, and she saw more. His knuckles were white against the glass stem. The drink sat almost full. Whatever expression he wore toward the room had dropped off out here; all that remained was concentration stretched thin over anger.

One more pulse came off him, fast and ugly.

*If they see me before I'm ready, everyone I leaned on burns.*

That did it.

He wasn't feeding the scheme. He was trying to document it before someone could sell it as policy and turn the border red.

Her stomach dropped anyway. Tomorrow's report changed shape on the spot. Sylvia would want the path, the logic, the confidence level. WISE would want sources she could not name because she had stolen them straight out of people's heads. And Damian Desmond, if that was who this was, had not chosen to tell her any of it. He had stepped out for air and she had gone through him like a lock pick.

The answer brought no comfort. It only gave her a worse problem.

He moved to set the glass down, and the terrace light caught his face.

It was Damian Desmond.

He was older, obviously, sharper through the jaw and darker under the eyes, with the soft roundness of Eden sanded out by adulthood and bad sleep. But it was Damian. He was the same age as her, with the same mouth when it went flat with irritation and the same line between the brows when he thought too hard. WISE kept a file on him because WISE kept a file on every Desmond worth breathing near. The paperwork called him a junior diplomat with moderate potential and uncertain loyalties. It had not mentioned the look of somebody holding a roomful of inherited wolves apart with his bare hands.

He scrubbed a thumb once over the edge of the stone.

*No more delays. No more waiting for them to hand me the harmless version.*

The thoughts kept coming, but now she stopped treating them like answers and started sorting them against what she had seen inside: the administrative woman by the windows not drinking, Brennan burying money in liaison lines, Dreher thinking about April and old arguments with Damian's father, the way retired men kept letting him into their corners because the family face still opened doors, the almost untouched drink, the full body stillness of a man using every inch of himself not to pace.

This was what she had come for: not a clean confession, but a pattern under pressure.

And she had it because she had taken it.

Anya stayed where she was, arms crossed against the cold because it looked natural and because the cold had finally reached her skin. The sensible move was obvious: mark him, go back inside, pull one more confirming thread from the room, leave. The actual move was harder. She had enough to ruin him with the wrong framing and not enough to protect him with the right one.

The terrace door opened behind her for a smoking attaché and banged once in the wind. Warm light spilled across the stone, brief and gold. The gust caught the loose front strand of her hair and blew it over her mouth. Annoyed, she set her glass on the railing with a sharp crystal click and shoved the pink strand back behind her ear.

Damian's thoughts broke stride.

His head turned just enough to catch her in the edge of his vision.

*Pink?*

Then the present tore.

*Courtyard. Spring. Bell late again.*

Her fingers dug into her own sleeves.

Sun on paving stones. A white shirt half untucked from running. A schoolbag dropped too hard. Pain and humiliation arriving together.

*You hit me right in the face. God.*

Not spoken, only remembered. He was thirteen again, nose bleeding down the front of his shirt, furious because it hurt and because half the yard had seen it.

The memory jerked, found another scrap.

A wet paper towel shoved at him.
A girl's face gone wide with horror.
That same girl's mouth twitching because the hit had been an accident and also, unfortunately, impressive.

Anya stopped breathing for one beat.

He kept turning the fragments over, not on purpose anymore. The way memories got handled when they leapt out before a person had decided whether they wanted them.

*Same tilt.*

*Green eyes.*

*No, that's insane.*

His attention snagged harder.

*Pink hair. Of course it was pink.*

For one second the terrace, the state dinner, the border, all of it thinned under the stupid bright violence of childhood: Eden uniforms, brick warmed by sun, his own outrage, her own mortified silence before she'd tried to apologize and somehow made it worse.

Then the first edge of a name pushed up.

*An...*

The terrace door opened again.

"Desmond," a man in a grey suit called from inside. "The deputy minister wants you."

Damian straightened at once. The schoolyard vanished. His mind snapped back into neat political lines so fast it felt violent.

"Of course."

He picked up his glass and turned toward the doors. As he crossed past her, six feet away at most, his thoughts were already on appropriations, border posture, who hated whom at the deputy minister's table, whether Kleist would dodge him again if the minister kept him late. His public face was back on. He was a diplomat again.

One brief glance brushed over her. It was automatic, polite, almost dismissive. There wasn't enough light, enough time, or enough reason in the middle of a state dinner to connect a pink-haired Westalian cultural attache to a girl from school who had once punched him in the face by accident and then failed to apologize correctly.

*Westalian. Freezing. Why don't any of them ever bring coats?*

Then he was through the doors and gone into the big hot noise of the ballroom.

He had not recognized her fully. All he had was pink hair, a courtyard, a punch, the front edge of her name.

Anya stayed on the terrace until the cold bit through the dress and into muscle. The flat champagne sweated against her palm. From inside came the muffled churn of the room closing around itself again, ministers reclaiming ministers, aides scooping up bosses, security shifting for departures. Her heartbeat had gone too fast and stayed there.

The bad part wasn't the mission result. The mission result should have simplified tomorrow, and instead it had split it open. She had enough to change WISE's read on the sector and no clean way to use it without dragging Damian, his access, and whoever he had leaned on into the light before he was ready. If she'd been anybody else, it would've been easy.

The bad part was how she had gotten it.

She'd let herself lean in past the point she would have called necessary in anybody else's report. She had taken surface thoughts because she had to, then she had stayed for fear, then for strategy, then for a memory that had nothing to do with the border and everything to do with the small ugly fact that she had no doors in her head and everybody else did.

The woman smoking two meters away crushed her cigarette out and thought, *If he leaves me I'm taking the apartment.* That, at least, was somebody else's problem.

Anya pushed off the railing and went back inside before the terrace became conspicuous.

The ballroom had begun its slow collapse into coats, handshakes, and strategic lingering. She did not look for Damian again. Looking now would have been stupid. She made one clean circuit of the room, caught only static and leftovers, let Brennan's thoughts confirm that he was still hiding money in the wrong place, let the charcoal-suited woman think miserably about a missing guest log, and then took the first exit that did not require another speech.

The SSS officer at the doors glanced at her pass and thought about his bus home. That was good enough.

Outside, the air felt cleaner even with traffic in it.

She walked the first six blocks instead of taking a car. She'd had too many minds in her head already that evening. The government district thinned into broader streets, then apartments, then shops shutting under metal grilles. Her heels bit at the backs of her feet. Wet leaves stuck to the curb. By the time she got to a main road, her headache had turned from sharp to deep and ugly.

She took a tram for three stops because she was tired and immediately regretted it.

*Did I turn the stove off?*

*No, no, no, don't cry here.*

*If he asks where I was, I say paperwork.*

Anya got off one stop early and walked the rest.

Her apartment building smelled like boiled cabbage and radiator dust. Mrs. Voller on the second floor was thinking about game shows loud enough to hear before Anya reached her own landing. By the time she got her door open, the key teeth had left fresh half-moons in her palm.

She kicked off her shoes in the hallway, made it to the sink, and threw up ministry pork, too much acid, and almost no champagne.

So much for not puking on foreign policy. At least the foreign policy had survived.

She ran water until the basin cleared, stood there with both hands braced against porcelain, and waited for the room to stop shifting under her feet. The headache thudded behind her eyes with slow professional patience. Her earrings were suddenly unbearable. She stripped them out, dropped them on the counter, and pressed the heels of her hands against her sockets until color burst under her lids.

Damian's near-formed *An...* came back immediately.

"Great," she muttered to the sink. "Love that."

The apartment offered no comfort beyond privacy. It was one room and a kitchenette, with a desk by the window, a radiator that clanked when it felt philosophical, two chairs, one of them mostly decorative, and a narrow bed under a bookshelf holding equal parts tradecraft manuals and cheap novels she kept buying at kiosks because pretending not to be a spy required hobbies. Tonight the room felt staged for somebody else: somebody who came home from the ministry and wrote harmless notes about cultural exchange schedules instead of deciding whether to lie to her own service.

She changed out of the dress without turning on the bright overhead, traded stockings for old pajama pants and a sweater with a hole by one wrist, tied her hair back badly, and sat at the desk with a legal pad, the evening's seating card, and a blank WISE report form she should not have been looking at until morning.

The pad got the fragments first, raw and out of order.

South gate.
Kleist.
Griesler.
Civilian line item.
Liaison allocation.
Forum list.
April fourteen.
Desmond.
Old files.

She stared at the names until they stopped being words and went back to sounds.

The first version came out too fast because truth usually did when it had nowhere to go.

She rolled paper into the machine and started typing.

Subject identified as Damian Desmond, Foreign Affairs. Off-book activity suggests he is conducting a covert inquiry into procurement irregularities connected to border review channels and Renewal Front-adjacent actors. Access appears to derive from old Desmond political relationships with retired party and committee figures.

Anya stopped with her fingers still on the keys.

There was no source path and no way to explain the confidence level. There was no way to prove why Damian mattered without handing Sylvia a person to squeeze, watch, or burn. WISE would not torture him. That was not the service she worked for. But they would turn him into an operation immediately. They would open him up like a file and map every contact they could reach through him: every older man at dinner who had let him into a corner, every clerk who had given him one more paper than protocol allowed, every frightened administrative woman guarding guest logs with a wineglass in her hand.

*If they see me before I'm ready, everyone I leaned on burns.*

She had taken that straight out of him. She could not unknow it now.

Also, and this part mattered more than she wanted it to, the report was impossible on its own terms. Anya had observed a young diplomat moving between tables. She had observed older officials accepting him. She had observed him take air on the terrace and think like a man trying to keep a border incident from turning into something worse. Everything that made the report true also made it unusable. Sylvia would ask how Anya knew the difference between internal rivalry and a real counter-operation. She would ask why she felt confident about intent. She would ask for the chain.

Anya could lie by omission around telepathy in the field. She did it every day. But this was not omission. This was a report built from the center out on information nobody had chosen to give.

She yanked the page free hard enough to wrinkle it and dropped it on the floor.

A second version came out cleaner, smaller, safer.

Damian Desmond remains a person of interest due to unusual access patterns across ministry and party-adjacent personnel. Current behavior suggests freelance positioning around internal security chatter rather than direct operational control. Continued watch recommended. Renewal Front indicators present but fragmentary.

The sourcing posture was better. The consequences were worse.

This one still put Damian under a lamp. It still told WISE to keep a close enough eye that he would eventually feel it, maybe not tomorrow and maybe not next week, but soon. And if he was using old family channels against Ostanian hardliners, close watch from Westalis would squeeze him from the wrong side at exactly the wrong moment.

She read the page again and felt the problem move under her hands instead of resolving.

Because there was a second lie buried under the operational one. She wasn't only trying to protect a useful line. She was trying to protect the private ugliness of what she had done: the terrace, the stolen fear, the childhood memory that had nothing to do with the assignment until it did, because once Damian almost remembered her, the entire mission ceased to be about a target and became about a person she had known before either of them were old enough to choose any of this.

She had heard him bleed in memory.

That thought sat in her throat like something physical.

Anya pushed back from the desk, stood too fast, and paced once to the window. Across the street a man in shirtsleeves smoked under an awning and thought blankly about football. That much, at least, was merciful. The radiator knocked twice. A car passed. Somewhere down the block a dog barked and got answered by another dog and then another. It was ordinary city noise, ordinary strangers with their ordinary private heads.

And then there was her, sitting in the middle of it.

By the time she sat back down, the second report might as well have been a confession, not to telepathy but to wanting to be the kind of person who could split the difference cleanly, tell enough truth to stay useful, and hold enough back to keep Damian alive, or free, or simply unexamined. That person probably existed in spy novels written by men who had never had to hear a target remember being thirteen.

She tore the second page in half, then in half again.

The room smelled faintly of sick and paper dust. Anya rubbed the heel of one hand over her sternum like that might knock the discomfort loose.

What did she actually know if she forced herself to use only what could live on paper?

Damian Desmond had access.
Damian Desmond had interest.
Renewal Front chatter was real.
Procurement chatter was real.
The room at the ministry was more frightened than it should have been for routine nationalist noise.

She also knew, with the kind of certainty nobody sane could cite in a report, that Damian was working against it.

That certainty would stay in her body whether she filed it or not.

She fed a third page into the machine and sat for a long time without typing. Her fingers rested on the keys, did nothing, moved, and stopped again.

At last she began with the version that could survive questions.

Mid-level diplomat with family visibility. Politically useful name, inconsistent direct authority. Activity at the ministry event suggested internal positioning and information gathering around security-adjacent issues, but present evidence does not support categorizing him as the primary operational threat.

It was still a lie, just neater.

She kept going because stopping there would have been honest in a way the rest of it wasn't.

Renewal Front references overheard at the event remain incomplete and likely include opportunistic posturing by multiple domestic actors. Recommend monitoring as elevated nationalist chatter rather than imminent action pending corroboration.

That line hurt more than the first one had.

Because now she was doing it on purpose.

She wasn't just hiding telepathy or protecting a source path that had never been a source at all. She was steering. She was building a document designed to make Sylvia look away half a degree, a bureaucratic nudge that might buy Damian space and maybe buy herself one more chance to figure out what the hell he was actually doing before WISE stepped on it.

It was the ugliest kind of lie because most of it was professionally legible. Nobody reading it tomorrow would hear a crack in the sentence. Nobody would think she had gone home sick from ministry pork and typed her way into a betrayal with a childhood memory still stuck under her ribs.

Anya reached for the legal pad and added one more line to the raw notes in pen.

He almost had my name.

She stared at that, then drew a box around it as if containment on paper could do anything useful.

This part did not belong in the report at all. It did not belong anywhere. Damian remembering her wasn't an intelligence datum she could file. It was a timer.

The whole problem reduced itself to four bright stupid scraps: pink hair, a punch, a courtyard, An.

By chapter standards of her own life, maybe that still qualified as "probably fine." By any sane standard, it was a problem moving toward her with its head down.

She thought about the look that had not quite become recognition when he passed her on the terrace. It had been automatic and dismissive, then gone. He had not placed her, not yet. But he had reached for it without meaning to, and that meant the memory was close enough to surface again the next time some stupid detail lined up: a laugh, a gesture, Becky saying the wrong thing at the wrong time if the universe felt theatrical.

The report sat in the machine half-finished.

Anya read it from the top, adjusted one phrase, cut another, and added a softer uncertainty where the truth pressed too hard against the edges. By the end it looked like good analytical caution. That was the part she hated most.

She slid the page out and set it beside the torn remains of the first two versions. The desk was a mess of paper and bad choices: one true and unusable, one cautious and still too dangerous, one smooth enough to survive daylight.

She gathered the third version, clipped it into a plain folder, and left the raw notes loose under her palm for a second too long.

Tomorrow morning she would walk into Sylvia's office with neat paper, controlled voice, and a professional assessment built on scraps, observation, and a lie she had now told enough times inside her own head to make it sound trained.

The first two drafts went into the ashtray on the windowsill. She struck a match, touched flame to one corner, and watched the paper blacken inward. The fire ate Damian's name first. Then Griesler. Then April. The room smelled briefly of smoke and chemical ink.

When the ash cooled, she crushed it flat with the matchbook and rinsed the tray clean.

The folder stayed on the desk.

Anya turned out the lamp, stood in the dark apartment with the ministry still humming at the back of her skull, and understood that nothing about tonight had ended on the terrace. The discovery was the easy part. The easy part had already happened.

Tomorrow the lie would stop being a draft and become a record.


# Chapter 3

Anya put the folder on the table and kept her hand on it a second too long.

Burned coffee. Paper. The metal taste in the back of her mouth that showed up when she was about to do fieldwork or lie or both. Sylvia sat across from her with a legal pad and the calm face she wore when she already expected competence.

*On time. Prepared. Good.*

Three versions of the report had existed last night. The first had been true and unusable: Damian Desmond running a quiet war inside Foreign Affairs, Renewal Front chatter sharper than WISE knew, too many lines pointing toward a real operation. The second had cut the telepathy out and still pointed too close to him. She had deleted that one too.

The folder in front of her held the third version. Clean formatting. Correct stamps. It was a lie.

"Your initial assessment of the Ostanian operative," Sylvia said. "Walk me through it."

"The operative is a mid-level diplomat. Damian Desmond, yes, Donovan's son, but he's kept visible distance from the family's political apparatus." Anya opened the folder, though she knew every line already. "His activity looks like internal party positioning. I don't read him as a direct security threat to Westalian interests."

*Matches the secondary indicators.*

No suspicion. Not even friction. Sylvia just made a note.

"The Renewal Front intelligence."

"Fragmentary." Anya turned a page she did not need. The paper nicked the pad of her thumb. Good. Pain was useful. "Mentions of an initiative. Some nerves around timing. It tracks with the usual nationalist noise. I'd log it as elevated chatter, not active preparation."

Sylvia's pen kept moving.

*Sound judgment. She doesn't inflate.*

Anya kept her eyes on the folder because looking up would make it worse. Sylvia trusted her enough to write quickly. Trusted her enough not to stop and ask the next question. Two years of clean work bought that kind of silence.

"The Desmond name usually gets escalated," Sylvia said.

"I know." Anya heard herself answer too fast and slowed down. "I know. But the son isn't the father. At dinner he came off careful, ambitious, politically housebroken. Not dangerous in a way that needs immediate weight."

That was not even the clean lie. It carried too much truth inside it. Damian was careful. He was ambitious. He was also hiding an operation big enough to tear the sector open if WISE leaned on it.

Sylvia turned to the last page and rotated the folder back toward her.

"If you stand by the assessment," she said, "sign it."

There was a blue line waiting at the bottom of the page. Her name typed underneath. Her thumb still stung where the paper had cut it.

Anya picked up Sylvia's pen.

*No hesitation. Good.*

The lie became a signature in her own hand.

Sylvia took the folder back at once, capped the pen, and stamped the corner with practiced force.

"Watch and report, then. Passive observation through your cover. Monthly unless his profile changes."

"Understood."

That should have been it. File closed. Morning gone. Sylvia looked up anyway.

"Your work at the dinner was clean," she said. "Positioning was good. You kept the read despite the crowd. I noticed."

*Tell her clearly. Loid doesn't.*

The praise hit low and ugly, right under Anya's ribs. She felt her grip tighten on her own knee under the table.

"Thanks."

"You've earned trust here," Sylvia said, matter-of-fact. "Don't start doubting your instincts because the target's name is loud."

Anya nodded because her throat had gone tight in a way that would not help anything.

At the door Sylvia said, "There is loose-leaf in the break room. I keep it behind the mugs. Henderson drinks anything that sits still."

*He will find it. He always does.*

"Yes, Handler."

The small joke almost got a smile out of her. That made it worse.

In the corridor she kept walking until she hit the women's bathroom. Empty. She locked herself in a stall and sat with the folder on her knees.

Her hands were shaking. Not dramatically. Just enough to rattle the paper.

The top sheet still showed the header and time stamp.

FORGER, ANYA  
08:14  
Sector assessment: low priority monitoring.

Her signature sat at the bottom in dark blue, ordinary and obedient.

If another desk laid it beside the real sector traffic later, the hole would show. Not today. Maybe not soon. But it was there now, stamped and filed.

She flattened the page with both palms until the tremor eased.

Nobody trained you for the paperwork. The ordinary font. The clean stamps. Her stomach turned at how steady the letters of her name looked.

She flushed the unused toilet, washed her hands too long, and stared at her face in the mirror until it looked like her again.

Then she made tea in the break room because Sylvia had told her to.

The break room smelled like dish soap, scorched kettle metal, and the stale sweetness of sugar spilled days ago and never fully wiped up. Sylvia had been right. The loose-leaf sat behind the mugs in an unmarked tin, hidden with the optimism of a woman who believed at least one man in the building could be trained.

Anya spooned too much into a strainer and let the kettle shriek itself empty while thoughts passed the half-open door in fragments.

*...if Henderson took my yogurt again I swear to God...*

*...meeting at eleven, why is everything always at eleven...*

*...good tea? no, impossible...*

That last one was almost certainly Henderson. She nearly laughed. The sound came up wrong in her throat and turned into a cough.

She drank half the mug standing at the counter, because sitting still felt dangerous, then carried the rest into the corridor and took the long way back to her cover desk. The long way cut through records and analysis. She knew that. That was why she took it.

The concealed side of the station looked especially ugly in daylight. Fluorescents. Grey cabinets. Coat hooks bowed under rain jackets. Someone had pinned a cartoon of a sinking ship beside the routing board weeks ago and no one had taken it down. WISE liked to think of itself as invisible. Most days it looked overworked instead.

Her folder was already open on the records counter.

Anya stopped without meaning to. The top page had been turned just enough that her signature showed at the bottom. Above it, Sylvia's routing stamp had dried into blunt black certainty.

LOW PRIORITY MONITORING.

No one around the counter treated it like anything. A records clerk in sleeve garters fed carbon copies through a scanner one page at a time, thinking about train delays and whether her son had remembered his gym shoes. A junior analyst clipped cable summaries into distribution bundles and wondered if the cafeteria soup had crossed the line from old to dangerous.

The folder sat open between the scanner and the distribution bundles, her signature drying in plain view.

"Morning, Forger."

Gerald Falk had a stack of briefs under one arm and a mug in the other hand that smelled like burnt chicory and surrender. He was reading as he walked, tie slightly off center, not even pretending the day had improved him.

"Morning."

He nodded at the open folder. "Sherwood cleared this already?"

"She did."

He set his mug down, skimmed the first page, and made a face that meant numbers were happening somewhere in his head.

"Good," he said. "If I had to watch another desk escalate a Desmond just because of the surname, I'd start billing for theater."

*One less aristocrat burning bandwidth.*

His thought was cynical, ordinary, not malicious enough to warn anybody about anything. That almost made it worse.

Anya said, "Try billing the aristocrats directly. Maybe we all retire."

Falk snorted. "Now there's a funding model."

He initialed the routing sheet and dropped the file into the blue tray. The tray already held six other assessments, all thinner than hers. The motion took less than a second. That was all it took to move Damian from suspicion to queue.

Across the lane, a young analyst in shirtsleeves uncapped a grease pencil, leaned over the glass tracking board, and erased DESMOND from the red column marked PENDING REVIEW. He rewrote it in blue under PASSIVE / MONTHLY.

The pencil squeaked on the glass.

Anya took a sip of tea she could no longer taste.

The analyst caught sight of her and gave a quick, tired smile. "If it's your read, I can stop losing sleep over that file."

*She's usually right. Thank God.*

"You shouldn't lose sleep over anyone before lunch," Anya said.

"That's not how this place works."

"No," she said. "It really isn't."

He went back to the board. Falk picked his mug up and drifted away toward the inner offices, already thinking about a procurement cable and whether the minister attached to it was stupid or merely greedy. Around Anya the station kept doing what it always did: sorting, stamping, ranking. The scanner took another page. Someone laughed two desks over. Her tea hit the back of her tongue bitter as pennies.

She wanted to hit something.

She had lied to Sylvia. She had lied to the analysts who would work downstream from the file. She had lied while reading their surface trust as casually as other people read clock faces. The asymmetry of it sat ugly under her sternum. Everyone else in the room was blind in the normal human way. She was the one walking around with a knife nobody could see.

On the legitimate side of the building, her overt desk looked almost cheerful by comparison. Mission letterhead. Cultural calendars. A framed print of a Westalian museum no one in the office had ever visited. Someone had left a cream envelope on top of her keyboard with the Ostanian Academy of Arts seal stamped in gold.

Attached to it was a note from the deputy chief's secretary.

Need mission representation tonight. Academy opening reception. I am still dying of whatever plague Kessler brought back from the trade office. Please attend in my place and look diplomatic. Guest list enclosed.

Anya put the tea down and opened the envelope.
