The guest list was thick paper and black ink and too many titles. She skimmed ambassadors, patrons, ministry deputies, donors, composers, one television critic with an inflated self-assessment, and then found the line she had been looking for.

DESMOND, DAMIAN  
Ministry of Foreign Affairs

Not keynote. Not host. Just present.

Which somehow felt more dangerous.

She read the list again, slower. The reception was for a reconstruction retrospective funded by a joint cultural grant, which meant speeches about memory, photographs in front of damaged-looking canvases, and at least one man pretending a budget line counted as healing. The chamber quartet would play in the main hall. There was a side gallery with the larger pieces. Foreign Affairs had sent Damian as part of the Ostanian delegation because somebody in his office had decided culture could still be useful if properly photographed.

Passive observation through your cover.

Sylvia had said it like routine. Routine had become permission the second Anya signed the report.

She pulled the program closer and started marking the margins with a pencil. Names of curators. Donors. The title of the lead painting. The exact phrasing from the grant summary so she could recognize it later when someone repeated it in a speech as if they'd invented it. She circled three safe opening lines and crossed out two of them immediately.

The first sounded too eager.

The second sounded like a woman trying to flirt on purpose, which was somehow worse.

The third was about the quartet and at least had the advantage of being true if they were bad.

At noon she ate half a sandwich over her desk and read the program again. At one she drafted a short mission note about attending in the deputy chief's place and filed it through the correct cover channel. At two she told herself she would keep the contact clean: twenty minutes, public room, no volunteering of off-book details, no letting him set the pace. At two-fifteen she rewrote the plan in her head and admitted the real problem was not protocol.

The real problem was that she wanted to go.

Strategic need, yes. He was a live line into Foreign Affairs and whatever he was doing against the Renewal Front. She needed contact. She needed something more than stolen thoughts and formal smiles across big rooms.

When she pictured the terrace again, heat climbed her neck before she could call it tradecraft.

He had looked at her on the terrace like a memory trying to fight through fog. He had thought her face, her laugh, the front edge of her name. She should have wanted distance from that. What she wanted instead was another chance to stand close enough to hear him think.

It would have been cleaner if she had simply wanted the intel.

By late afternoon the station had settled into the dull roar of paper moving and phones being answered with institutional patience. Her file on Damian was no longer visible from her desk. That should have helped. It did not. Somewhere behind two secure doors and one scanner queue, her signature was drying into archive.

At six she left the mission, stopped by her apartment long enough to change out of office grey and into something the cultural desk could plausibly have chosen on purpose, and stood in front of her bathroom mirror trying out the three marked opening lines.

The first still sounded false.

The second sounded thirsty.

The third sounded like her if she was being careful.

She went with careful.

On the tram into the government district she kept the folded guest list in her coat pocket and watched Berlint slide by in streaks of rain-dim glass and lit shop fronts. The carriage was crowded enough to hum, not crowded enough to drown her. She got surface noise in bursts: grocery lists, sex, a blister on someone's heel, a woman silently workshopping a resignation she would never send. Human spill. Easy. Familiar.

Every time the tram lurched she felt the paper in her pocket tap against her hip like a reminder.

Her lie had bought her room. Tonight she was going to spend it.


The Ostanian Academy of Arts was bright, crowded, and blessedly smaller than the state dinner. Thoughts moved through the hall in manageable bands instead of storm fronts: complaints about funding, annoyance about the quartet, one man's desperate hope that the salmon puffs had not run out yet.

Anya stood near the refreshments table with a glass of white wine she did not want and found Damian by the far wall.

He was talking to a woman in a grey suit. He looked lighter here, one cuff unbuttoned, his wine glass loose in his hand. When the woman said something and he laughed, the sound came out before the diplomatic version of it could catch up.

*The attaché from dinner. Don't stare. Listen.*

His eyes came back to her anyway.

Anya moved on him in stages. Brief stop with a Westalian press officer. Nod to an Ostanian academic. One useless canapé for camouflage. By the time the woman in grey stepped away, Anya was already in the opening.

"The quartet's ambitious tonight," she said. "I think that's Schumann."

"It is," Damian said, turning toward her. "Though ambitious is fair."

Up close he looked less like the boy in the old memory and more like a man who had come straight from work and stayed anyway. Slight dampness in his palm when they shook hands.

"Anya," she said. "Westalian mission."

"Damian Desmond. Foreign Affairs. We met at the dinner. Sort of."

"Sort of is generous. I was mostly trying to find the bar."

That got the small laugh again.

*She's funny. Be normal.*

Normal did not last. His gaze caught on her face a beat too long, moved to her hair, came back. The pause was tiny. Anya felt every second of it.

*Her face. Where...*

She smiled like she had not heard that and kept the conversation moving.

"So you've been in..." She stopped. Berlint was wrong. Schools were wrong. Anything with a timeline was wrong. "Sorry. Foreign Affairs. Is it as glamorous as people say?"

The redirect was visible. Damian paused half a beat before answering.

"It depends who says it." He loosened a little. "Mostly it's position papers and meetings about meetings."

Then, because he could not help himself, "The interesting work lands higher up the chain. For now."

His mouth tightened almost immediately. He looked into his wine like it had betrayed him.

*Too much.*

"You don't have to..." Anya heard herself start and caught it. He had not said anything that needed saving. "That's honest," she said instead.

He glanced at her, held there half a beat, then looked down into his wine. "That wasn't supposed to be. I mean, it was. Just not that soon."

Still awkward. Still better. His ears had gone slightly pink. Anya looked at her wine so he would not catch what her face was doing.

Music shifted at their backs. Someone near the donor wall started talking too loudly about regional healing. Damian turned his head toward the sound and then back to her with a look that was not quite apology and not quite conspiracy.

"You're attached to the exhibition?" she asked.

"Only in the sense that Foreign Affairs gets blamed for any evening involving flags."

"That does explain the string quartet."

"Does it?"

"Everything bad has a committee behind it."

His mouth moved before he caught it. "That may be the most professional thing anyone's said to me all week."

She tipped her glass toward the nearest wall card. "'Resilience Through Shared Tenderness,'" she read. "That sounds like a committee wrote it while one of them held the others hostage."

Damian followed her gaze and then laughed outright. Not the dinner laugh. This one was less careful.

"Three deputy directors and a translator lost that fight," he said.

"You know this for a fact?"

"I sat through part of the process by accident."

"How do you sit through that by accident?"

"My brother's office wanted me visible. Culture was considered harmless."

"Was it?"

"Not to the translator."

He stepped closer to the wall card and lowered his voice like he was sharing classified material. "It originally said 'mutual care in reconstruction.' Then someone decided care sounded soft. Then someone else decided reconstruction sounded punitive. This is where we landed."

"Shared tenderness."

"An atrocity."

"I kind of love it now."

"That reflects badly on you."

"It reflects accurately on me."

He looked at her for a second too long again, but this time the look had something warmer in it.

*Don't grin. You are grinning. Stop.*

Anya had to look away before her own face did something unhelpful. She used the movement to inspect the donor board, which was almost as tall as the painting beside it.

"That plaque is bigger than the art."

"The plaque paid for the art."

"That doesn't mean it has to win."

"That's not how donors think."

Anya snorted. "Westalia had a visiting pianist last spring. We printed six hundred brochures promising selections from our nation's mournful digestive tradition."

Damian blinked. "What was it supposed to be?"

"Folk heritage."

He put the side of his glass against his mouth and failed to hide the laugh. "That's worse. I respect it, but it's worse."

"People showed up on purpose."

"For the music?"

"For the digestive tradition. The hall sold out."

His shoulders loosened another inch. He didn't look back for the woman in grey or scan the room for rescue. He stayed with her.

The quartet hit a phrase badly enough that three people near the front flinched in sequence.

Damian murmured, "Our cellist is trying to escape the violinist."

"I knew it."

"You looked pleased with yourself before I said anything."

"I was."

"That's unnerving."

"Then don't volunteer supporting evidence."

He laughed again. Two guests squeezed past behind him, and he shifted with them without breaking off. Bureaucratic fraud. Bad translation. Committee language bad enough to count as a crime. Stupid material. Enough to keep him there.

Then Damian's attention snagged.

Not on her face this time. On her hands.

She had started talking with both of them without noticing, sketching the shape of the donor plaque in the air. His eyes dropped, tracked the motion, and slowed. Something moved behind them.

*Courtyard bench. Paint on cuffs. Both hands when she argues.*

"Did you..." he started, then stopped. His eyes came back to her face. "Sorry. Did you study in Ber..."

Too close.

Anya cut across him so fast it bordered on rude. "Hold on. Is that plaque actually blocking the signature?"

She pointed at the bottom corner of the painting. A passing donor in pearls was already thinking furiously about the placement.

*If my name is under that awful man from customs I want the whole board redone.*

Damian blinked, question still hanging in the air behind his eyes, and followed Anya's finger.

"It is," he said.

"That's criminal."

"It's donor relations."

"Same thing."

He turned back to her slowly. Not fooled. Not pushing yet.

*That was a dodge.*

Anya lifted one shoulder. "You were about to ask a geography question at a reception. I panicked on your behalf."

His mouth twitched. "I might have made it interesting."

"Regional vowels are never interesting."

"That's a very strong position."

"It's a correct one."

He looked at her another beat, then let the question slide. "You sound local sometimes," he said. "And then not."

Tangential honesty. Bad evasion. The only kind she was any good at.

"I had tutors who cared too much," she said. "Everyone wanted my language corrected into their version of civilized."

"That sounds exhausting."

"It was boring first. Then exhausting."

Something in his face eased. Not because he fully bought it. Because it was enough for now.

They drifted away from the donor board toward a cluster of smaller canvases, following the space that opened naturally between guests. A cultural secretary from the Ostanian side nodded to Damian and then glanced at Anya's mouth before remembering manners. A critic with an expensive scarf thought, *He's in a better mood than he was at the ministry,* and kept moving.

"Do you actually do much of the arts portfolio?" Anya asked.

"Enough to complain about it convincingly."

"That wasn't the question."

"No," he said. "It wasn't."

He took a sip of wine he clearly disliked. "I get pulled in when the ministry wants someone young enough to make funding cuts look like modernization."

"That's bleak."

"It's also true."

"Westalia sends me when they want someone who can smile at a sculpture and make it sound bilateral."

"Can you?"

"Depends on the sculpture."

He glanced at the nearest canvas, which looked like wet ash with aspirations. "And this one?"

"This one wants me dead."

"Finally. An honest critic."

He should have sounded smoother than he did. Public Damian probably did. This version kept letting little honest parts break through and then watching them like they might need to be recalled.

"You hate these things," Anya said.

"I hate what people do in rooms like this."

That came out too fast. He stopped, jaw tightening around the mistake.

*Careful.*

Anya waited.

He corrected, but not all the way. "Everyone talks around the art. Over it. They say culture when they mean image, or continuity, or budget camouflage. By the end of the night you learn more about procurement than painting."

That was closer to the bone than his public face ought to have allowed. She kept hers neutral.

"That sounds useful."

"Sometimes."

"Sometimes sounds like yes."

He looked at her sidelong. "Do you always interrogate people this politely?"

"Only if they start talking like they regret their job."

"I don't regret it."

"No?"

"I regret large parts of the institution attached to it."

He said institution like it had edges.

Anya let herself smile. "That's more specific than most diplomats get on first meeting."

"Most diplomats don't compare grant language to hostage situations."

"That's because most diplomats are weak."

"That's a terrible thing to say at an arts reception."

"Then it's the right venue for it."

His next laugh came with a shake of his head. For a second he looked young in a way the dinner had not allowed.

*She's impossible. Keep talking to her.*

There it was. Not just recognition danger anymore. Not just that old schoolyard static pressing at the edges. He wanted the conversation. He was stepping into it on purpose.

That changed the temperature under her skin.

They stopped in front of a bronze sculpture with an explanatory placard so vague it could have described weather.

"Do you know what's funniest?" Damian asked.

"I'm worried already."

"This grant came out of a policy fight over industrial repair funds."

"That's not funny."

"Wait." He lowered his voice again. "The arts lobby won because one committee member thought public restoration sounded less expensive than infrastructure."

Anya stared at him. "You are joking."

"I wish I was."

"So this whole exhibition exists because someone heard the word restoration and pictured a mural instead of a bridge."

"Essentially."

"That is the dumbest thing I've heard this month."

"It's only the twenty-ninth."

"Exactly. Strong competition."

He looked like he wanted to say more and should not. Surface thoughts broke against the leash of his public training in brief clean flashes.

*Budget committee.*

*Trade office.*

*If she'd seen the numbers... no.*

Anya kept her expression easy and did not chase the scraps harder than she already was just by standing there. Surface thoughts were not consent. They were just open doors she walked through anyway. The guilt came and went in quick ugly bursts, never strong enough to stop her.

"So why are you really here?" she asked.

He didn't answer immediately.

Good. He kept watching the spill being cleaned instead of reaching for the easy answer.

Around them the room thickened and thinned. Someone somewhere dropped a glass and then spent six full seconds thinking *Don't turn around* while everyone turned around. The quartet staggered into recovery. Damian watched the spill being cleaned and said, without looking at her, "Because people treat rooms like this as off-stage."

Anya waited again.

"They aren't," he said.

That was all.

She rolled the stem of the glass against her palm. No names. No intel. Just him showing her where he stood in the room, and that got under her skin worse.

"Fair," she said.

He turned back to her. "And you?"

Too direct. Too soon.

Her first answer was the true one: because she had lied to her own side this morning and wanted to know if it had bought her something worth the damage. Her second answer was also true: because his thoughts felt different from everyone else's and she kept wanting to hear the next one. Neither was usable.

She took a sip of wine she still hated. "I go where people underestimate the portfolio," she said. "They get lazy when they think the room doesn't matter."

His gaze sharpened. Not alarm. Interest.

*Not just sculpture and canapes. I knew it.*

He said, "That sounds less like culture and more like work."

"Those aren't opposites."

"No," he said quietly. "They aren't."

For a second neither of them spoke. The nearest thought in the room belonged to a donor trying not to stare at Damian's mouth. The second-nearest belonged to a waiter thinking about cigarette break timing. Normal noise. Harmless noise. Between it, the silence held.

Damian glanced past her shoulder toward the smaller gallery and then back at her.

"Have you seen the next room?"

"No."

"There's a painting in there that's supposed to justify the whole exhibition." He shifted his glass from one hand to the other, then back again. "Or embarrass it. I honestly can't tell."

*Too eager.*

Protocol said not to let the target set the pace on first contact. Protocol was already losing.

"Show me."

The gallery was cooler and quiet enough that Damian's surface thoughts rose cleanly. Fifteen people, maybe fewer. Soft shoes on polished floor. One guard thinking about cigarettes. A woman near the back wondering if she could leave without greeting the deputy minister's wife.

They stopped in front of a huge dark canvas that looked like a wall after a fire.

"This one?" Anya said.

Damian folded his free hand behind his back and put on the face of a man preparing to say an intelligent thing about art.

*Negative space. Say negative space.*

Anya bit back a grin. "Go on. Skip the gallery answer. What do you actually think?"

Damian looked at her. "The gallery answer?"

"The polite one. Everyone has one."

He studied her a beat longer than the moment needed, then turned back to the painting.

"The use of negative space is..." He stopped hard enough that she saw the decision happen in his jaw. "Honestly, I hate it. It looks like the painter got furious at a door."

The laugh got out of her before the attaché version could catch it. It bounced off the gallery wall, too loud, too familiar, entirely hers.

Damian went still.

*That laugh.*

His eyes narrowed. The flirtation went out of his face. Search took its place.

"Sorry," Anya said, still smiling. "That was mean."

"No." He kept looking at her. "No, it was accurate."

He kept hold of it.

"This is a strange question," he said. "Have we met before?"

There it was. The half-memory from the terrace had crossed into the open and turned into a question.

Anya felt the old school courtyard flare up in her own head so fast it was almost comic. Mud on shoes. Becky yelling. Damian with a red mark on his cheek and righteous fury. She shoved it down.

"I don't think so," she said.

Too quick.

She added, "Unless you've been trapped at a lot of exchange receptions. They blur after a while."

He watched her for another second. Long enough that she had to work not to fill the silence.

*That isn't it.*

"Right," he said finally. "Sorry. You just seem familiar."

"That can happen when everyone in diplomacy starts wearing the same face after eight p.m."

That got a breath of a laugh out of him, but not full relief. Good. Or bad. Hard to tell.

They moved on from the painting, but not back to where they had been. The field had changed. He was still listening to her words. He was also checking them against the question he had finally said aloud.

So she stopped pretending the conversation was harmless.

"That wasn't the gallery answer either," she said.

"What wasn't?"

"The question."

He looked at the next canvas and said, "No."

"Should I answer it again?"

"Would I get a different one?"

"Probably not."

"Then we can spare ourselves."

He sounded dry. Not offended. Not convinced.

Anya said, "You notice people in inconvenient patterns, don't you?"

"Occupational hazard."

"Mine too."

That made him turn back to her fully.

"And what exactly is your occupation hazard?" he asked.

There was the dangerous version again. Not where are you from. What do you do when the room isn't looking.

She should have smoothed it over. She heard the proper, polished line and let it die.

"Listening past what people mean to show me," she said.

His eyes held hers.

*Jesus.*

Then, almost in the same breath, *Too much again. Stop doing that.*

Anya glanced at the next placard because if she looked at him another second she was going to make the wrong face. "You really are bad at harmless conversation."

"So are you."

"I know."

He huffed a laugh. The tension didn't break, exactly. It resettled into something thinner and stranger.

"Most people in these rooms want the easy version," he said.

"Of what?"

"Of everyone." He took a breath and let it out through his nose. "The harmless attaché. The grateful donor. The minister who cares deeply about access to the arts and not at all about the front page. The version where no one is using anything for leverage."

"And you're tired of performing yours."

"Tonight?" He considered that. "A little."

"That's honest again."

"You keep rewarding it."

"Maybe stop offering it."

"That doesn't seem to be happening."

They had reached the far end of the gallery where the windows looked out on black courtyard glass. Their reflections floated faintly over the city lights: her pink hair toned down by the dark, his suit turned almost charcoal. For one ugly second the reflected shapes looked young enough to belong to the wrong decade.

Damian saw it too. Not the same thing, probably, but something. His shoulders drew tight and then eased by force.

"Did I make this odd?" he asked.

"It was already odd."

"That's not reassuring."

"I wasn't aiming for reassuring."

He smiled despite himself. "No. I noticed."

A server came through with fresh drinks. Anya took sparkling water and Damian, after a visible argument with himself, did the same.

"Wise choice," she said.

"I need a clearer head."

*Around you, apparently.*

That one came through with enough heat behind it that she nearly inhaled her water wrong.

"You okay?" he asked at once.

"Fine. Bubbles."

He waited one beat, like he could tell there was more and had decided not to embarrass her by asking for it.

The pause caught under her ribs.

He said, "For what it's worth, I'm not trying to interrogate you."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"If you were interrogating me, you'd be smoother."

"That's offensive."

"It's accurate."

"You really enjoy telling me accurate things that sound insulting."

"You keep setting them up."

He shook his head and looked down into the water like he expected it to improve him. "I don't usually do this badly."

"Do what?"

"Meet people."

Her pulse stumbled once anyway.

"You're not doing badly," she said.

"I asked if we'd met before."

"We might have met before. That's objectively possible."

"You answered that too quickly."

"You noticed that too."

"Yes."

"Then maybe you aren't bad at this. Maybe you're just annoying."

That made him laugh properly again. The sound bounced softer here than her own had. Less dangerous. More dangerous. Hard to tell.

"That's unfair," he said.

"No, that's accurate."

"Right. Your favorite category."

"One of them."

He leaned one shoulder against the wall, closer to relaxed than she'd seen him yet. "All right. Since we're apparently done pretending to be normal diplomats for five minutes. What do you actually think this exhibition is doing?"

The question was serious enough that she answered it seriously.

"Trying to make austerity look humane," she said. "Trying to tell everybody that if the state funds damaged-looking paintings it still has a soul."

He stared at her.

*Exactly.*

"That's a grim read."

"It's not wrong."

"No," he said. "It isn't."

He looked back toward the big room beyond the gallery threshold, where speeches and donors and strings were still grinding away. "The ministry loves culture when culture can stand in for repair. It's cheaper. Cleaner. No one has to admit what isn't getting fixed."

He said it while looking out at the donors, not at her, like the room could take the blame for him.

Anya said, "You really shouldn't say things like that to foreign attachés."

"You really shouldn't say things like that to diplomats either, but here we are."

"Fair."

He rubbed a thumb once against the rim of the glass. "There's a forum at the ministry on Thursday afternoon. Trade and cultural appropriations."

Anya looked at him.

He kept going before embarrassment could stop him. "Public enough to justify foreign attendance. Miserable enough that no one bothers performing for long. The deputy minister says tariff reform at least three times before anyone stops him."

"That is a terrible invitation."

"It's an honest one."

"You already established that's your problem."

"Apparently." He exhaled. "If you're actually covering East-West exchange work, it's more useful than tonight. People start telling the truth by accident somewhere around the third repetition."

She stopped turning the empty glass in her hand. Fluorescent ministry room. Tariff reform. People bored enough to tell the truth by accident. Not a polite brush-off.

Anya heard the correct professional answer and stepped around it.

"Yeah," she said. "Send me the details."

His surprise was tiny but real. He took out his phone at once and then made himself slow down.

"I mean tomorrow," he said. "I'll send them tomorrow."

*Not tonight.*

"Tomorrow's fine."

She gave him the official cultural attaché address, the one WISE monitored, on a mission card. He read it once, entered it carefully, then slid the card into his inner pocket as if he did not trust himself to remember it later.

"That really is your work address," he said.

"You sound disappointed."

"I sound cautious."

"That too."

He put the phone away. "For the record, the forum may still be terrible."

"I believe in consistency."

"Good."

"Do you go to all of these?"

"No."

"Then why this one?"

He looked toward the doorway again, where the sound from the quartet came in thin and overcommitted. "Because this is where the budget language gets cleaned up before it starts doing damage somewhere else."

That was closer to the wire than anything he'd said yet. He seemed to hear it after it left his mouth. His jaw tightened. The old diplomatic reflex came down a second late.

"Sorry," he said. "That was vague and bleak."

"It was specific enough."

"Was it?"

"Yeah."

He studied her face the way he had at the start of the night, but this time the question in it was not only memory. It was whether she understood more than she was supposed to. She let him sit with that without helping him.

Eventually he said, "You don't sound much like a cultural attaché."

"You don't sound much like a man enjoying his reception."

"I didn't say I was enjoying it."

"Exactly."

That won another small, unwilling smile.

When they finally circled back toward the main hall, the quartet was packing up and half the room had already defected to coats and exits. A donor with lacquered hair caught Damian's sleeve and thanked him for his ministry's commitment to national healing. He delivered the required answer with flawless public warmth, and the whiplash of it almost made Anya laugh. By the time he got free again, he looked faintly embarrassed to have been overheard being competent.

"I assume that's the version you're better at," she said.

"Unfortunately, yes."

"Shame."

"On what grounds?"

"I liked the other one."

He went very still for half a second.

*Don't react to that. Absolutely react to that.*

Anya looked away fast enough to make it look like she was watching someone fetch a coat.

At the entrance he offered his hand again.

"It was good to meet you, Anya."

"You too."

He held on half a beat longer than protocol required. Then, quieter, "If I remember where from, I'll tell you."

Anya kept her smile in place by force. "Please do."

He nodded and headed out into the cold with the look of a man carrying home a question he did not intend to drop.

Outside, the boulevard had emptied into the usual government-district dark. Streetlights on. Trees half-turned. Her wine buzz had never arrived. All that was left was the cold and the day.

She had blown past the twenty-minute mark, let the recognition question live, and accepted a second meeting in a ministry room because he offered it and because she wanted it. The lie to Sylvia had bought her room. She had used all of it and already started spending tomorrow's.

City thoughts brushed past as she walked toward the diplomatic quarter. Dinner. Rent. Wet shoes. A child reciting spelling words to herself. A man rehearsing an apology he was never going to make. The usual human spill, unasked for and easy.

At a red light she pulled one hand from her pocket and held it under the signal glow.

Steady.

She curled it back into a fist and waited for the light to change, with Sylvia's quiet trust still in one ear and Damian's not-quite-confession about off-stage rooms in the other.

The report was in WISE's system now. Her cover address was in Damian's pocket. By tomorrow there would be a ministry forum in her inbox, and her own signature was already sliding deeper into archive behind two secure doors.

When the signal changed, she crossed with everyone else, hand steady in her pocket, feeling the file disappear into one system while the line she'd opened to Damian pulled taut in the other direction.


# Chapter 4

Eight days after Damian walked out of the arts reception with the Westalian mission address in his pocket, Anya was back in the Foreign Affairs Ministry under another name. Tonight the badge on her jacket said ANNA KESSLER, because this room liked full names and department lines and last names that matched the guest list. A week ago he'd gotten Anya, easy and informal, because the cultural exchange people treated first names like a policy instrument. The state dinner had been a third card for a third room. If anybody from either government ever laid the badges side by side, they'd stop laughing at the bureaucracy and start asking harder questions.

Not tonight. Later was how damage liked to arrive.

The deputy minister had been murdering tariff reform for forty straight minutes when Brandt slid into the empty seat beside Damian and bent his head in a way that looked polite from a distance and predatory from where Anya sat. Most of the audience had the dead-eyed patience of people being held hostage by their own careers, and the abandoned translation headsets on the back table had already knotted themselves into a little plastic nest of surrender. Damian still had his diplomatic posture on, one ankle hooked back under the chair, folder balanced on one knee, but the line of his shoulders had gone half an inch stiffer the second Brandt sat down.

*...if Dreher says framework again I'm going to bite somebody...*

*...don't forget potatoes. He'll complain all through dinner if there aren't potatoes...*

*...who put dill on salmon in October...*

Then Brandt's surface thoughts came through hard enough to lift gooseflesh on Anya's arms.

*...security meeting again. Not on the invite list. Naval attaché Monday, depot office Wednesday, now this. Not random.*

So that was how the night was going.

She kept her eyes on the podium and listened sideways. Brandt was tall, blond, thinning up top, one of those men who dressed as if every room were a chance to be taken for slightly more important than he was. His badge read BRANDT, K. Ministry of the Interior, Policy Liaison. She already knew that from the seating packet. Knowing it from his own head felt worse.

Damian didn't turn fully toward him. Publicly he could make indifference look courteous.

"If this is about the revised border brief," he murmured, "it can wait until the minister finishes."

*...make him say it twice. Make him commit to the lie in public.*

Brandt smiled with only one side of his mouth. "This isn't about the brief. I was curious about the smaller meeting upstairs. The one that didn't make it onto the calendar."

Damian let half a beat pass. "A lot of meetings don't make it onto public calendars."

"Not all of them include a naval attaché."

Two rows ahead, somebody coughed into a handkerchief. The deputy minister droned on about harmonized customs language. Nobody was listening. The room had thinned into private miseries and dinner plans and one man trying very hard to remember whether he had actually signed a school permission slip or merely thought about signing it. Anya caught all of it in smears. Brandt cut through anyway.

*...say procurement. See if he flinches.*

"Then perhaps I should be more direct," Brandt said softly. "You seem interested in procurement questions that sit a little outside your remit."

Damian moved one thumb along the edge of his folder. That was all. His face stayed easy.

"I work in Foreign Affairs. Everything is outside my remit until it isn't."

A couple of people near the aisle laughed, because they thought he was making a joke about ministries. Brandt did not.

*...there. Smooth little bastard.*

Anya looked down at her notes as if tariff codes deserved the full devotion of her pen. Her pulse had already climbed. She could let Damian handle it. He had handled worse. He was better at this than she was. He had grown up in rooms full of people trying to peel information off him with their teeth.

Then Brandt thought Gessler.

*...take it upstairs tonight. Gessler will want the access log and every room Desmond's touched this week.*

That made the problem bigger.

If Gessler pulled access logs, he'd get Damian's closed-door meetings. That alone was bad. Stack enough odd rooms into one week and anybody who loved paper more than people started seeing pattern. Anya didn't know the full shape of what Damian was doing. She knew it got harder fast once somebody upstairs started drawing lines between doors.

Anya closed her notebook.

A ministry aide was moving down the side wall with a tray of sweating water glasses and the blank expression of a man dissociating professionally. The minister finally stopped speaking and invited questions. Chairs shifted. Brandt put one hand on the back of Damian's seat as if he meant to keep him there through the break.

That was enough of that.

Anya stood, gathered her packet, and crossed the room before common sense could stage an intervention. If she took two minutes to think about it, she might take five. Five was Brandt walking Damian into a smaller conversation with fewer witnesses.

She stopped beside them with her mission smile already in place.

"Mr. Desmond. Hello again."

Brandt's hand came off the chair at once. Damian looked up. For a second the surprise on his face was unhidden enough to be human before the public version came back over it.

"Ms." He glanced at her badge. "Kessler."

"Anna Kessler, formally." She gave a little apology to the air. "Last week was much more civilised."

*Anna now. Last week it was Anya. Fine. Different room.*

Good. At least he was tracking the same problem.

Brandt's eyes moved over her face, badge, folder, and back again.

*...Westalian mission. Convenient.*

"I don't think we've met," Anya said, turning to him before he could ask the question that would hurt. "Anna Kessler. Cultural office at the Westalian mission."

"Brandt." His handshake was dry and brief. "Interior."

"I'm so glad you're interior and not commerce." She shifted her packet into her left arm and made herself sound relieved enough to be annoying. "I've been trying to find the person responsible for the Velden freight corrections and Commerce keeps acting like the corridor numbers are folklore. Do you know who handles the cross-posted manifests when exhibition cargo gets counted once at the border and again when customs bounces it for documentation?"

The trick was not cleverness. Cleverness got remembered. The trick was paperwork dense enough that people wanted out.

Brandt blinked.

Damian looked at her like he realized she was either saving him or digging a second trap under the first one.

"Commerce handles trade numbers," Brandt said.

"I know, but then they said methodology and sent me to transport, and transport said if the trucks originated under cultural waiver then foreign liaison had to sign off, and foreign liaison is apparently you people tonight? Or someone adjacent to you?" She opened the packet as if it contained proof of injury. "We've got a touring exhibition sitting on three separate inventories and every office says the duplicate line is somebody else's duplicate line."

That part was almost true. Some poor exhibit probably was sitting in a warehouse somewhere because governments loved paper more than paintings.

Brandt did not take the bait so much as get webbed by it.

*...Velden. Public problem. Could still be coincidence. Could still be cover.*

"I don't handle inventories," he said.

"Right, but you know who does."

"No."

"You don't know or you don't want to tell me?"

He gave her the look bureaucrats reserved for women they considered mild inconveniences. Anya had worn worse looks to worse rooms.

Behind him Damian got slowly, beautifully blank.

*...she's doing this on purpose.*

Good. Keep up.

A cluster of ministry staff started filing toward the coffee urns at the back. People rose around them. Brandt could have walked away. Anya moved half a step to follow before he made up his mind.

"Everyone keeps saying the word audit at me like that's a magic spell," she said. "I don't need magic. I need one person with a stamp who'll admit the numbers are wrong."

Brandt tried to angle his shoulder past her. She pivoted with him.

"Was it transport or procurement that changed the routing code last spring?" she asked. "Because if it was procurement then I need a different complaint entirely."

There it was: procurement.

His expression barely changed, but the thought landed hard.

*...Jesus Christ. Even Westalian arts girls are saying procurement now.*

He did not like that. Good.

Damian took pity on him then, or on himself. It was hard to tell.

"Ms. Kessler," he said, smooth as polished brass, "surely this can wait until the break is over."

"No, because if I don't pin down the office tonight then tomorrow I'll get three new answers and one of them will be wrong in a more official font."

That got a real noise out of a man by the coffee urn. Not a laugh exactly. Close enough.

Brandt's patience frayed a little at the edges.

*...Desmond comfortable with her. Not good. Noted.*

He looked back at Damian. "You're unusually popular."

"With cultural administration?" Damian said. "Always."

"If only." Anya offered Brandt a page from the packet before he could leave. It was the printed agenda. "Would you mind circling whoever I should annoy next?"

He did not take the paper.

"Commerce," he said again.

"Commerce is hiding from me."

"Then perhaps that's instructive."

"It isn't, though. It's rude."

Brandt actually closed his eyes for one brief second. When he opened them, his smile had teeth in it.

"I have another meeting."

"Of course you do," Anya said brightly. "Would tomorrow be better if I sent a note?"

"No."

"Great. Then I'll keep guessing."

He walked off with the stiff-backed speed of a man who knew that if he stayed another five seconds he'd say something professionally regrettable.

Anya let him go three steps before she brushed his thoughts again.

*...Dreher first. Gessler after. Pull the access log. Check Kessler against the calendar. Then Desmond. Tuesday should be enough.*

Enough for what on Tuesday?

There wasn't enough room to grab it cleanly. There was too much other noise. He was already threading into the coffee crowd and the room crashed back over him in salt, boredom, bad coffee, school pickups, a blister on someone's heel, three separate resentments about the deputy minister, and one woman privately wondering if divorce papers counted as formal wear if they were still in her handbag. Anya's temple pulsed.

It could wait. It wasn't gone. It had only been shoved half a step down the line.

She turned back. Damian was on his feet now. Up close he looked composed in the usual way and furious in the smaller ones. One hand had gone to his folder hard enough to crease the paper inside.

"Ms. Kessler," he said. "You have very strong feelings about freight accounting."

"I'm developing them."

"At speed."

"That's one of my gifts."

His mouth almost moved. Not a smile. The outline of one aborted on principle.

*...not only cultural office. Not even close.*

He angled himself so the room couldn't read the conversation off his face.

"There's a service corridor behind the east stairwell," he said softly. "Five minutes."

"That sounds ominous."

"It isn't a suggestion."

Then he walked away before she could answer, threading back toward the front with the easy, trained indifference of a man who had not been publicly rescued by a woman using exhibition inventories as a weapon. By the time he reached the podium, he had already tilted himself toward a deputy minister. Public Damian had zipped back up.

Anya stood where he left her long enough to look like somebody deciding between water and bad coffee. Her fingers had started the tiny post-adrenaline shake they always threatened after a move she had not fully thought through.

Tuesday should be enough.

Whatever enough was, Brandt thought he could prove it by Tuesday.

She took a glass off the passing tray and drank because her mouth had gone dry. The water was warm.

*...Marta will kill me if I don't text before six...*

*...if Desmond asks for another annex I am transferring departments...*

*...that girl's badge says Kessler. Wasn't it Keller on the seating chart? No, Kessler. Read properly, idiot.*

Anya set the glass down and waited out the five minutes without looking at Damian once.

---

The corridor behind the east stairwell smelled like cleanser, old metal, and the wet rubber lip of the mop bucket abandoned against the far wall. One fluorescent strip buzzed overhead. Pipes ran along the ceiling with brown paint flaking off at the joints. Somewhere deeper in the building a door banged and footsteps faded.

Damian was already there.

His tie was loosened. His jacket was still on. His hands were in his pockets, which with him meant he wanted them anywhere else.

*...don't start angry. Figure her out first. Then be angry.*

Too late for the second part.

"So," he said.

"So."

The fluorescent strip hissed.

He looked at her badge. "Is it always something different?"

"Only when the room demands it."

"That's not an answer."

"It is. It's not the whole answer."

His gaze stayed on her face a fraction too long.

*...same eyes. Stop that.*

That hit weirdly low in her stomach and she shoved it aside. That was not the emergency currently in progress.

"Try again," he said. "Who are you really?"

That was the dangerous version of the question because the truthful answer had too many parts. WISE operative. Cultural attaché. Liar. Telepath. Girl from Eden he almost remembered. None of them fit cleanly beside a mop bucket.

"I am Anna Kessler in rooms that need Anna Kessler," she said. "I'm Anya in rooms that don't. And I work for more than the cultural office."

Damian's jaw flexed once.

"Intelligence."

"Yes."

"WISE?"

There was no point pretending not to understand. "Westalian intelligence," she said. "That's as specific as I'm going."

He absorbed that with the stillness he used when he wanted someone else to fill the silence for him. She did not. Barely.

*...WISE. Or she wants me to think WISE. Why step in. Why now.*

"Brandt," he said at last. "How did you clock him?"

"I got close enough."

"To what?"

"To him. To the conversation. To the room."

"That is three different evasions."

"Yes."

His stare sharpened. "You're not very good at this part, are you."

"Evasion?"

He waited.

"No," she said. "I'm not."

That slowed him for half a beat. Good. True answers in the wrong places made people reset.

He took one step closer, not aggressive exactly, more like he was closing the corridor down to the part they occupied.

"He mentioned a meeting upstairs that wasn't on the calendar," Damian said. "He mentioned procurement without saying the word until you said it for him. He has a superior named Gessler who apparently gets access logs. You knew all of that fast. Too fast."

"I heard enough."

"How."

Her mouth almost outran her. She could feel it line up wrong.

"Because he was practically shouting it in his head." The sentence was out before she could stop it.

She bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to get copper. That was a terrible answer.

"Because he was obvious," she said instead, the swerve ugly and late. "His face. His tone. The questions. Come on."

Damian's expression changed in the smallest possible way. Not because he believed her. Because he had watched the correction happen.

He didn't say that. He didn't need to.

"You approached me last week under one name," he said. "Tonight you appear under another. You knew enough to keep Brandt pinned. You also knew the name Gessler mattered." His voice stayed quiet, which made it worse. "So let's save time. Did your people put you on me after the gallery?"

That was almost the truth. The actual truth was she had put herself on him, and then falsified the report that should have done the opposite.

"My people were already watching some of the same mess," she said. True at the edges. Not enough in the middle. "When Brandt started leaning, I moved."

Damian looked at her for a long moment.

*...too fast. Still doesn't account for Gessler.*

No, it didn't.

He breathed out through his nose. "All right. Then let's work from the part I can use."

That was not trust. It was triage.

He leaned one shoulder back against the pipe behind him, as if giving himself something cold and ugly to push against.

"Gessler is deputy director on the internal review side," he said. "He isn't Front leadership. He's worse for me than that. He likes paper. If Brandt takes him a pattern and Gessler starts checking my room entries against closed committee minutes, every quiet conversation I've had in this building gets louder."

That was specific enough to hurt.

"So you were right to interrupt."

A beat.

"I don't enjoy saying that."

"You're welcome."

His eyes flicked up. That might have been the wrong level of lightness. Might have been fine. Hard to tell with him.

"What exactly do you think you saved me from?" he asked.

"Tonight?" Anya folded her arms because if she didn't, her hands would start doing too much. "Brandt wanted your meetings lined up in a neat row for Gessler. He was already halfway to thinking you weren't only curious. He was thinking pattern. Once bureaucrats get to pattern, they never stop until they've built a shrine to it."

Damian's gaze dropped briefly to her left hand where it was tucked under her right elbow. His own hands stayed in his pockets.

*...she talks like field, not desk.*

Good. Fine. Whatever kept him from asking the one question she couldn't survive.

"I don't use the name you're about to say," he said.

She tipped her head. "Renewal Front."

His mouth flattened.

"There it is."

"If we're pretending that's not what this is, then we both wasted the walk."

Something tightened in his face and then gave a little, like she had finally hit the right locked door instead of all the decorative ones around it.

"My father left—" He stopped. Started again from a different angle. "There's a standing procurement authorization that runs through the Defense Liaison Office. My father's signature is on the original. He's been dead for years and it's still disbursing because nobody in this building wants to be the person who admits the contract was never about border security." His hand came out of his pocket, made a vague gesture at the pipes, went back. "The Front didn't build anything new. They moved into what was already running. That authorization. The committee seats at the Gesellschaft Club. Procurement desks that only exist because my family's name was on the appointment memo."

"Brandt?" she asked.

"I don't know yet." His jaw worked. "Useful to somebody. Gessler definitely knows which doors still open with the old keys." He looked past her shoulder at nothing. "The point is it's not—" He caught himself, started over. "It's not ideology. Or it is, on the surface, but underneath that it's invoices. Checkpoint staffing contracts. Emergency appropriations where somebody has to supply the emergency first. People who got comfortable when the border was hot and need it hot again because cold borders don't pay."

"And you're—"

"Trying to shut it down," he said, cutting across her before she finished. "Or slow it down. I don't know." He scrubbed a hand over his mouth and caught himself doing it. "Something."

The pipe behind him knocked once, sharp enough to make both of them glance at it. A secretary passed the far end of the corridor without looking in. Her thoughts brushed Anya for a second and vanished into grocery lists.

Damian waited until the footsteps were gone.

"So yeah," he said, quieter. "That's what I'm doing here."

"Okay."

His eyebrows went up. "That's it?"

"What do you want me to say? I got between you and a ministry liaison. I'm not going to pretend I don't get it."

He breathed out through his nose. Not quite a laugh.

"That isn't the part that should be easy for you."

No. It wasn't. The easy part was him. The impossible part was her side of the wall.

She touched two fingers to the side of her head, then dropped her hand when she realized what she was doing.

"The easy part is knowing Brandt is a problem," she said. "The hard part is being in this building at all."

"Because?"

Because Sylvia had believed her. Because the report was in the system. Because she was now in a mop-smelling corridor cutting a deal with the man she'd told WISE not to treat as urgent. Because every correct move she made from here deepened the wrong one already on paper.

"Because the room has ears," she said instead. "Because yours does. Because mine does. Pick one."

He watched her closely enough that she could feel the weight of it on her skin.

*...too much how. Too much from where. God, what are you actually hiding.*

Everything.

"What do you want?" he asked.

"Brandt off your back, first." She uncrossed her arms. "After that? Shared intelligence. Limited. Specific. Enough to make it harder for the Front to move without somebody noticing."

"You say that as if we're already on the same side."

"I'm not—" She stopped herself. "We're both in this hallway. That's all I'm saying."

He went quiet. In the silence his surface thoughts sharpened instead of disappearing.

*...recruitment. Leverage. Maybe bait.*

*...Father let one Westalian inside. Three dead. Don't be stupid.*

There it was again. It wasn't only a reflex. It was a wound.

Anya kept her face still.

"If this is recruitment," he said, "say it plainly."

"It isn't." She heard how thin that sounded and corrected. "Or it is, badly. I'm not here with a folder and a speech. I'm trying to make a small offer before Brandt hands your week to Gessler."

"Limited how."

"Names. Dates. Meetings. Movement if either of us gets it. No sources."

"My people stay mine."

"Mine too."

"No questions about networks."

"Fine."

"If you think you've bought yourself access to every quiet conversation I have with anyone who ever served my father, you haven't."

"I didn't think that."

"Good. Because you'd be disappointed."

The corridor hummed. Anya kept guessing off the scraps she caught and the parts of his face he couldn't control, which was not the same thing as certainty no matter how much she wanted it to be. It still helped more than it should, and she hated that too.

He seemed to be pulling back now, testing the width of the offer.

"Regular channels only," Damian said.

"Meaning?"

He tipped his chin toward the building around them. "The cultural exchange calendar. RSVPs. Invitations. Notes about panel seating and youth orchestras and whatever other useless things your office can plausibly send my office without lighting up anybody's desk."

"So the boring stuff."

"The boring stuff keeps people alive."

Fair.

"If Brandt is already looking," he went on, "we don't invent some dramatic secret trail for him to trip over. If you need to contact me, it goes through paper that can survive daylight."

There was something bleakly funny about that. Their first alliance term was basically commit espionage like civil servants.

"Okay," she said. "Calendar. RSVPs. I can do boring."

He looked dubious. Justified.

"And if one of us decides the other is lying," he said, "it stops."

"No penalties," Anya said. "No revenge tour. No dragging each other down on the way out. We stop."

His attention sharpened on that phrase.

*...she cares about the exit.*

Because she already lived there.

"I mean it," she said before he could press. "If this goes bad, it ends clean."

"Nothing like this ends clean."

"Cleaner, then."

He considered her. The fluorescent light made the shadows under his eyes look older than the rest of him.

"My side of this isn't abstract," he said. "If Gessler gets enough paper to justify it, he'll pull my staff records, my scheduling history, everything. He'll sit on it until he can make a loyalty question out of any name that repeats too often. One wrong inference and people who never asked for any of this get questioned because they happened to have coffee with me twice in one month."

That cost something to admit. She could see the cost more than hear it. His voice lost structure when it got close to people who mattered.

"Okay," she said, softer now. "Same on mine."

He frowned. "You said your people were already on the same mess."

"They are."

"Then why does helping me sound like self-harm."

Because it was.

She looked at the mop bucket instead of him. Dirty water. One drowned paper label floating near the rim. It was an easy object. Easier than his face.

"Because I've already put skin in it," she said.

His silence changed.

"How."

Anya licked the copper taste off her teeth. Too close.

"I can't answer that."

"Convenient."

"Yeah." She looked back at him. "You think I don't know that?"

He held her gaze. Something in his expression nearly softened and didn't.

"What happens if Brandt checks the calendar tonight?" she asked, because leaving his last question open was safer than trying to fill it. "Not in general. Specifically."

He let her get away with it for the moment, probably because he wanted the answer himself.

"He sees you approached me in public at a ministry forum," Damian said. "He sees whether the Westalian mission requested extra seating near Foreign Affairs. He sees whether my office approved anything unusual around your attendance. If he's thorough, he checks whether your name showed up near me anywhere else this week."

"This week only?"

"If Gessler tells him to go back, farther."

And if somebody on her side ever decided to do the same, they'd find the gallery address, the lie in her report, now this.

"Good," she said, not because it was good but because naming the shape helped.

"It isn't."

"I know."

A sound from the stairwell made both of them turn. Two junior staffers came through arguing about a translation memo. They barely glanced down the corridor. Damian waited until the door shut behind them again.

"Say I believe you enough for tonight," he said. "What then."

"Then we start with something small enough to kill if it stinks."

He folded his arms now, mirroring her earlier without noticing.

"Such as."

"Such as if I hear a name tied to Brandt's side of this, I pass the name. If you hear movement that would put me or my office in front of the same people twice, you warn me before I walk into it."

"You expect me to protect your cover."

"I expect you not to let the Front use me to get to you if we're doing this."

He stared at her.

"That's almost the same thing."

"Almost."

Another beat. Another tiny shift.

"All right," he said. "One warning each. No more than that until something proves useful."

"That's fair."

He looked away first, which surprised her. His gaze went to the pipe above her shoulder, fixed there while he made himself ask the next question.

"Why were you at the gallery last week before I spoke to you?"

It wasn't a trap exactly. It was worse. It was personal.

Because she had known his name before he knew hers. Because she'd been sent to identify an unknown operative and found a boy from school with his father's face and a mind she couldn't stop listening to. Because she had used his own thoughts to walk herself into his orbit and kept going when she should have gone back to Sylvia with the full truth.

"Work," she said.

"That's the answer your badge would give."

"It's still true."

His jaw tightened. "Do you always answer like this when you're cornered?"

"No. Sometimes I panic."

That pulled his eyes back to her. Honest again. Wrong place again.

He huffed a little laugh in spite of himself.

"I'll make note of that."

"Please don't."

He pushed off the wall. Decision time.

"Shared intelligence on the Front," he said. "No questions about networks. No meetings off ordinary channels unless it becomes unavoidable. Either of us can end it."

"Yes."

"And if Brandt or Gessler touches your office because of tonight, you tell me."

That wasn't about protecting her. It was about knowing how much damage the evening had already done. Still.

"I tell you," she said.

He nodded once. Almost formal again. The public shell climbing back over the private one because the conversation had reached the part where he needed armor.

Then he stopped.

"How did you know about Brandt?" he asked. "The truth this time."

There it was. The corridor had finally found the one loose wire she had been stepping around.

She could lie cleaner. She should lie cleaner. She heard that and did it badly anyway.

"My side has been hearing his name around the same people you're watching," she said. "When he started leaning on you, I moved."

Damian didn't blink. Didn't help her. Didn't call it false either.

The silence held long enough for the fluorescent buzz to turn mean.

"That's what you're giving me," he said.

"For now."

He considered that, then straightened his tie with two quick, irritated motions.

"All right," he said. "Then here's what I'm giving you. If your first note through that calendar smells wrong, I burn it and we're done."

Fair.

"If yours smells wrong, same."

His mouth pulled tight. "You really aren't afraid of sounding rude."

"I'm afraid of lots of things. That's not in the top ten."

That did it. A real smile almost surfaced and got strangled on the way up.

"Good night, Ms. Kessler."

"Good night, Mr. Desmond."

He took two steps, then turned back once more.

"If Tuesday comes up tonight," he said, voice low, "pay attention."

Then he left.

That was new.

Anya stayed where she was until the stairwell door shut behind him and the corridor felt empty again. Tuesday. So he had heard it too, or guessed the same pressure point. Either way she had something to look for besides her own pulse.

The tremble started in her hands around second twenty.

She shoved them under her arms and counted backward from one hundred because it gave panic something stupid to chew on.

Seventy-three.

Her teeth hurt. She had bitten the inside of her cheek hard enough to split skin.

Fifty-nine.

The sequence kept arranging itself the same way in her head: the report in the system, Damian in the corridor, Brandt upstairs with Gessler's name in his head. One face for the forum, another for the hall, a third for the truth she still had not said out loud once, not even alone.

Thirty-eight.

She had made an alliance with a man who did not know the ugliest thing about it.

Not the telepathy. That was its own rot, and older. The other thing was the report.

She had lied about Damian to WISE after the gallery and bought herself breathing room with the lie. Now she was spending that room on him. If anybody ever laid tonight's attendance beside the assessment she had filed, the contradiction would light up hard enough to hurt.

Sylvia would not miss a contradiction like that for long.

Her stomach clenched hard enough to hurt.

No moral speech arrived after it. No clean verdict. There was only consequence.

When she could trust her legs again, she went back into the forum.

---

Somebody had found coffee while she was gone. That made the air smell burnt and punitive. The deputy minister was back at the podium grinding through a new page of notes with the glazed persistence of a man who believed data could become charisma if forced long enough.

Damian stood near the front beside a vice minister, all public edges restored. If she had not been in a mop corridor with him she might have believed the reset. He did not look at her.

Brandt did.

He was three rows back now, notebook open on one knee, writing with short annoyed strokes.

*...Kessler. Westalian mission. Check whether she was at the gallery. Check whether she was at dinner. Desmond doesn't get three coincidences.*

Anya kept walking as if she had only come back from the toilets. No flinch. No glance. She crossed to the back wall, took another warm sparkling water off a tray, and stopped where she could watch the room through the glass.

Brandt kept writing.

*...Tuesday. Bretz. If Desmond turns up there too, Gessler gets the whole packet.*

There it was.

Bretz.

Something small and vicious skipped through her pulse. It wasn't relief. It was relief's uglier cousin. A usable name.

Colonel Bretz. Procurement review Tuesday. Enough for Gessler if Damian showed up. Enough for Damian to get there first if he moved fast.

Brandt flipped a page.

*...and Kessler. Why was Kessler near him twice.*

Twice. So he already had the gallery in mind or some other room. Good to know. Bad to know.

Anya kept the water glass steady by gripping it harder than necessary. Across the room Damian turned a page in his folder and did not once let his gaze drag toward her. Smart. If anybody had been timing how long it took people to betray themselves, they were currently failing the test in the boring direction.

The minister called on a man from trade compliance. Somebody near Anya muttered, *kill me now*. Another mind supplied an itemized dinner plan involving sausage, mustard, and a fight with a brother-in-law about football. Telepathy never respected tone.

Brandt stood. Notebook in hand.

He moved toward the side door that led deeper into the ministry offices. Gessler. Or Dreher first, the way he had thought before. Either way, his paperwork night had started.

Anya put the water glass down untouched.

The new channel Damian had named came back to her in practical pieces: the cultural exchange calendar, invitations, RSVPs, idiotic official notes nobody could flag without admitting they had read something too carefully.

She could wait until morning. Morning would be safer on the face of it. More office-like. Less suspicious than a night note.

Morning also gave Brandt the whole night with his access logs.

No.

She left by the diplomatic side exit instead of the main hall, signing her name on the ministry clipboard in the round careful hand she used for cover work. ANNA KESSLER. Time out: 18:14. The clipboard already held five other neat lies about where the people on it had gone and why.

Outside, Berlint had gone to iron. The sky over the government district was the color of dishwater and the sidewalks shone with a damp sheen that made every streetlamp look doubled. Cars hissed past the curb. Somebody down the block was selling roasted chestnuts from a cart and the sweet scorched smell hit her all at once, hard enough to make her realize she had forgotten to eat.

She bought a paper cone because standing still without purpose made her look wrong. The vendor's thoughts were a practical flood of small concerns.

*...charcoal low. Need change. Girl looks too thin. Charge extra for diplomats. No, too obvious...*

Anya gave him exact coins and walked toward the mission with the chestnuts burning her fingertips through the paper.

Every few steps she caught stray thought-shards in the crowd. Wet socks. Rent. Call mother back. Idiot in the blue coat. She let them pass without trying to hold them. Too full already.

By the time she reached the Westalian mission annex, night staff had settled into their preferred rhythm of paperwork, quiet resentment, and tea gone cold. Her badge got her past the front desk without comment. The guard on rotation was thinking about a television detective and whether shaving in the evening counted as self-respect. Safe enough.

Her office on the third floor smelled faintly of radiator heat, toner, and the lemon soap the cleaner used on Fridays whether the carpet wanted it or not. One lamp was on. Filing trays were stacked. The cultural calendar binder lay open where she'd left it that morning on a page about youth orchestra exchange dates.

It was perfect, ridiculous, and useful.

Anya shut the door halfway, not enough to look secretive, enough to keep hallway chatter out. Then she sat, pulled the exchange calendar toward her, and stared at the ministry contacts sheet until the names stopped blurring.

Bretz, Colonel Emil. Gesellschaft Club patron committee. Tuesday dinner rotation.

It was there, printed in the kind of directory that passed for innocent because culture liked rich men in uniform if they donated to the right concerts.

So Brandt's Tuesday was real. Bretz had a reason to exist in the same social ecosystem as Damian's headache.

Anya took out the mission note cards.

Her first instinct was to write too much: Tuesday, Gesellschaft, Brandt's watching, Gessler pulling logs, don't be seen twice. That was not a cultural invitation. That was a confession with letterhead.

She tore the first card in half.

On the second she wrote, in the dead banal mission language she hated most:

The Westalian Mission Cultural Office is finalizing guest coordination for Tuesday's Gesellschaft Club dinner. We understand Colonel Bretz remains on the patron committee. Please advise whether your office requires updated seating notes for any ministry attendees with corridor trade interests.

Hideous, and perfect.

Anyone skimming it would see bureaucratic static. Damian would see Tuesday, Gesellschaft, Bretz, corridor trade interests. If he wanted the lead, he had it. If he didn't, he could ignore it and nothing on the card explicitly begged to be burned.

She read it twice.

Then a third time, because now came the other decision. Official channel meant record. Record meant trace.

The WISE report sat in another building under her name and Sylvia's trust. The new note would sit in this one under her cover and the Foreign Affairs ministry's letter stamp. Put together, they did not say alliance in neat language. They said she had buried a live line and then built one anyway.

Her right hand had started to shake. Not much. Enough to make the pen tap the desk in a stupid bright rhythm.

She put the pen down.

This was the actual point, not the corridor or the terms or even Brandt. This was the moment where the thing became paper.

If she sent nothing, Damian lost time and could lose the Tuesday lead with it. If she sent it, there was now a document trail between them that both Brandt and WISE could eventually find. It wasn't romance or abstract guilt. It was a trail. A clerk could retrieve it. A supervisor could initial it. A hostile audit could staple it to her false assessment and ask why.

She thought of Damian in the corridor saying people who never asked for any of this got pulled in because they had coffee with him twice. That was true on both sides. Once the records started talking to one another, nobody stayed bystander.

Anya pressed her thumbnail into the scar on her left palm until the pain steadied the rest of her.

There was no more waiting for a cleaner version of bad.

She fed the card into the mission envelope, marked it for Foreign Affairs cultural liaison, and added the tiny routing notation that would put it on the next internal pouch instead of tomorrow's public stack. A little abuse of office routine. Not enough to ring bells. Enough to buy hours.

Before she sealed it, she copied the text into the outgoing log.

Name of correspondent: D. Desmond, Foreign Affairs.  
Subject: Tuesday guest coordination.  
Action: advisory / no response required.

The logbook line looked harmless. That was almost the worst part. Harm should look like harm. Instead it looked like stationery.

She sanded the ink, closed the book, and sealed the envelope.

When she carried it downstairs to the dispatch tray, the night clerk barely glanced up.

"Late addition?" he asked.

"Club dinner nonsense," Anya said.

He grimaced with instant bureaucratic solidarity. "Tragic."

"If only culture were less important."

"One can dream."

He stamped the corner of the envelope and dropped it into the ministry pouch without another thought.

That was it. There went the paper.

Anya watched the pouch swallow it and had the sudden animal urge to grab it back, laugh, say wrong envelope, something stupid, anything. She kept her hands at her sides until the urge passed or turned into something less embarrassing.

It was too late now. Good.

Back upstairs, her office felt smaller.

She sat again because standing invited pacing and pacing invited thoughts she didn't have room for. The mission radiator ticked. Somewhere down the hall a typewriter started up in ugly bursts. Her chestnuts had gone cold on the desk. She ate one anyway and it tasted like ash and sugar and the inside of a burned pan.

She opened her notebook and wrote down what could be written safely for herself:

Brandt - access logs - Gessler  
Tuesday / Bretz / Gesellschaft  
No new channel outside calendar  
If either lies, stop

The third line made her snort once, too sharp to be amusement. If either lies. Right.

She wasn't lying about the Front. She wasn't lying about Brandt. She wasn't lying about wanting to keep Damian from getting folded into Gessler's files until the paper cut him to pieces.

She was lying about the foundation of the entire arrangement.

The alliance existed because she had falsified Damian downward in WISE's system. That wasn't an emotional distinction. It was operational fact. If she'd written the true report, Sylvia would have escalated him, somebody more senior and cleaner would have taken the contact question away from her, and tonight she would not have been free to improvise in a ministry forum under a diplomatic badge. She had bought herself room with institutional fraud and then spent the room on him.

There it was in plain language at last. She had lied in the report. She had written Damian as lower risk and lower value than he was, left out his active line into Renewal Front-adjacent procurement channels, and let WISE settle into the false calm of that version. Tonight's alliance stood on that lie. If WISE had seen the real assessment, they would have put other hands on him before she ever got another chance.

Her stomach rolled again.

If Sylvia reopened the assessment and asked for source justification, Anya had nothing that survived scrutiny. If liaison ever shared attendance data back through the intergovernmental reporting lane and somebody diligent noticed Anna Kessler's repeated proximity to a supposedly low-priority Ostanian diplomat, that contradiction went straight into her file. If Brandt handed Gessler enough paper and Gessler got curious enough to push the question outward, WISE might hear it from the wrong side first. That would be worst. Not the reprimand. Not losing the field badge. The wrong side telling her own service where to look.

She imagined the sequence because her brain insisted on practical horror. Sylvia's office. Door shut. The assessment printout. The calendar note. The neutral tone that meant she was already past being disappointed and into damage control. Her cover apartment gone by morning. Her mission access frozen. Damian's name flagged through channels he had never agreed to expose. Somebody on his side hauled in because a Westalian operative with a false report had touched the wrong paper trail.

Anya got up too fast, shoved her chair back, and crossed to the window before the nausea made a decision for her.

Outside, the district moved in cold strips of light. Government cars, umbrellas, steam from a sewer grate, a man arguing into a payphone with all the wounded dignity of someone losing to mathematics. Ordinary city. Nobody looking up.

She braced both hands on the sill until the wave passed.

No one had forced this on her in the passive voice. She had done it. First the report. Then the corridor. Then the note in the pouch. Three choices. Three lines.

The chestnut paper cone crackled when she crushed it without meaning to.

A thought brushed through the office wall from some clerk on the other side.

*...Tuesday dinner, Tuesday dinner, if I forget the seating chart she'll kill me...*

Tuesday again.

Fine.

She went back to the desk, opened the cultural contacts binder one more time, and copied the rest of Bretz's public details into her notebook. Wife: Margarethe Bretz. Patron committee. Regular cognac reception before dinner. Never stays past eleven, according to a penciled note from some prior event coordinator with the neat irritated handwriting of a person who had once planned around him. Useful. Damian could probably get more through his own side. Still useful.

She did not send a second note. One was enough to damn herself with.

Instead she drafted a harmless follow-up event brief for the mission files in case anybody later asked why she cared about the Gesellschaft dinner at all. Cover maintenance. Not elegant. Necessary.

By the time she finished, the typewriter down the hall had stopped. The clerk thoughts had thinned. Somewhere in the building a cleaner's bucket rattled over tile. Same sound as the ministry corridor, somehow.

Anya checked the wall clock. Past eight.

No reply would come tonight through official channels. Good. It was safer that way. She didn't need proof Damian had taken the bait. The envelope existing was already enough.

She closed the binder, locked the notebook in her desk, and sat with both hands flat on the blotter until the tremor settled to something livable.

At the front desk the guard glanced up from his crossword.

"Long night?"

"Apparently culture never sleeps," Anya said.

He groaned in sympathy without really hearing her and went back to his puzzle.

Through the dispatch room window she looked once more at the ministry pouch, sealed for the morning run, squat and ordinary on the shelf, with her envelope somewhere inside it among seating charts, routing slips, and other harmless-looking paperwork.

That was the shape of it now. Not a feeling. Not a line pulling taut in the dark. A sealed pouch, Colonel Bretz's dinner, and Tuesday already moving toward both of them whether that turned out to be clever or suicidal or far too late.


# Chapter 5

Anya kept the Gesellschaft Club roster open across her knees while Damian idled half a block past the entrance instead of pulling in. The note that got her here had come through the cultural exchange calendar at noon under a tedious chain about seating cards and missing RSVPs. Forty minutes later the same thread had picked up a question from Brandt's office asking whether Anna Kessler would be attending the club reception on behalf of the Westalian mission. Damian had answered that too, with the exact amount of bureaucratic boredom the occasion required.

If Brandt wanted the calendar, he had it.

If Gessler pulled Kessler, he would find the harmless cultural attaché file the cover was supposed to produce.

None of that made Damian visible outside a private club with her any less loud. It only made it a risk they had chosen instead of one chosen for them.

Soap still clung to his collar over whatever cedar thing he'd put on and then tried to wash off before picking her up.

He checked the side mirror again.

*No gray Volker. No ministry sedan. Bus, florist van, taxi. Fine.*

He had already taken two unnecessary turns around the square before easing back toward the curb. The third pass finally seemed to satisfy him. He parked under a streetlamp that turned the windshield into pale gold and killed the engine without taking his hands off the wheel.

"Did Brandt answer?" Anya asked.

"Only once." Damian kept looking ahead at the club entrance. "He wanted to know whether Kessler was attending in an official capacity."

"And?"

"I told him Cultural had sent a representative because the club had a Westalian quartet playing after dinner and somebody needed to pretend that mattered."

"That sounds insulting to quartets."

"It worked."

He turned then, just enough to meet her eyes.

"Brandt is active," he said. "Contained, but active. Gessler has your name, which means he has your file. If either of them gets curious enough to move from paper to feet, tonight becomes a problem quickly."

"You came anyway."

"Bretz signs on Tuesday."

Tuesday dropped between them hard enough to turn her stomach once.

She looked back at the roster. Getting it had cost him something. She could tell from the way his thoughts kept catching and restarting. *Bretz? Over the phone? Absolutely not.* *Why do you need that list, Desmond?* *Forget it.* He had cut the calls off before curiosity turned into a report. He had not wanted another message sitting in the same calendar Brandt was already checking. He had wanted to bring her in person even less than he wanted to burn a favor.

If the lead was real, it was moving too fast to hand off. If she was lying about something fundamental, he wanted her close enough to watch it happen.

"Tell me again why we're escalating from fake bureaucracy to actual public embarrassment," she said.

"Because if Bretz signs what I think he's signing, whatever Hartig is building won't wait for either of our institutions to organize themselves." Damian took the folder from her lap, opened it, and tapped the first page with one finger. "He arrives around eight. Cognac in the salon, dinner at nine, never stays past eleven. Tuesday is his procurement review. If he's angry about anything, it'll be tonight. If he's frightened about anything, it'll also be tonight."

"And you couldn't send somebody you actually like."

"I don't actually like anyone suitable for this."

"Comforting."

"Useful."

"His wife is Margarethe," he said. "She likes arranging people. If she decides you're decorative, you'll spend the night getting handed from one conversation to another. If she decides you're attached, you stay where she put you."

"So we go in attached."

"We go in as a couple because that's the only version of this room that gets close to Bretz without setting off alarms."
