Fragments hit in bursts. *Real. Her mouth. Don't stop.* A key turning in an apartment too quiet when he came home. Cardboard boxes still unpacked beside a chair with one jacket over it. His own voice at a meeting, saying things he didn't believe in the tone required to make everyone else believe he did. *Don't do the polished version now. Don't make this another performance.* Then, smaller and sharper because it was happening now instead of earlier: *Please. Let this be real.*

Those weren't confessions. Strong feeling had ripped them loose before he could stop it, turning private spill into broadcast.

Her whole body reacted before she had a usable thought. Her hand jerked in his hair. Her mouth went slack. The kiss lost its rhythm so abruptly that Damian felt it.

He drew back at once. Not far. Just enough to look at her. His grip loosened on her hip. *Too fast. I hurt her. Stupid.*

The room changed in the space of one breath. A moment earlier it had been hunger, relief, the shock of still being alive. Now information contaminated it too. She could still taste his want. She knew about the empty apartment, the lonely key, the plea he hadn't meant to say anywhere except inside his own head.

She should've let him go.

Instead she caught the front of his shirt and kissed him again.

Not careful this time. Not clean. She went back in with her eyes open for a second and one hand braced hard on his shoulder, as if force could turn the act back into something simple. It couldn't. Her ribs complained when he pulled her closer. He made a soft sound against her mouth and then another when pain tugged at the split in his brow. Nothing about it felt like the first kiss anymore. This one kept breaking on breath, on pain, on the extra current she couldn't shut off.

Then memory flashed through him, bright because the same nerves were doing too much at once. Gym lights. Soap water skidding across varnished floor. Poster paint ground into the rubber edge of a sneaker. Pink hair shoved back with impatient fingers. Laughter. The class photo waiting on a folding table by the wall. The thought that chased it came closer than the others had.

*Anya. Anya F--*

His mouth stalled against hers. A hiss broke out of him as pain spiked behind the wounded brow and shattered the rest.

She heard even that much. Heard the name start. Heard how near it had gotten. And still, after one suspended second that could've become stopping, she kissed the corner of his mouth and kept going.

He drew back just far enough to breathe. His forehead rested against hers. One hand stayed spread between her shoulder blades, warm even through the fabric of her shirt. The other still held her at the hip.

"I don't know what this does to the operation," he said.

"I don't either."

A breath of laughter touched her mouth. "Probably nothing good."

"Probably not."

He didn't move away. Neither did she. For a few seconds the only thing that existed was the fact of his hand between her shoulders and the way both of them were still breathing like they'd run to get here.

Eventually he leaned back against the wall. Contact thinned but didn't break. Their knees still touched. Her shoulder stayed against his. His fingers, without seeming to decide it, caught in the fabric at her sleeve and remained there.

Exhaustion hit him fast after that. Adrenaline had been doing impossible work and now it was done. The eyelids got heavy. The line of his mouth softened. Thought slowed, then drifted.

What came back as he slid toward sleep wasn't just the gym floor this time. It was syllables.

*Anya.*

A pause long enough to make her own pulse kick.

*For--*

His mouth moved once against the wool at her shoulder and stopped there. The name didn't stay in him. Pain broke it every time it got close to locking. But it kept coming back closer.

Anya stayed very still.

The bare bulb buzzed. His blood had dried brown at the edges of her nails from the butterfly strips. In her pocket, the folded note pressed against her thigh: `18:12 / west exit revision / WS-7`. The prepaid phone sat in the closet with its battery beside it, both of them waiting for morning. Dawn would be for moving him, reporting from somewhere cleaner, and going north on the one corridor she'd managed to narrow.

For now he slept in brief, uneven drifts with his shoulder against hers and his fingers still slack in her sleeve. Her jaw had been locked so long it hurt, and the bruise under her ribs was only part of the reason. She had no clean way to separate what belonged to her from what she'd taken. Under the scratchy blanket, with his near-name still catching and failing in her head, staying felt like one more theft. She stayed anyway.


# Chapter 10

Under the kitchen light, the milk was going to be a problem. The next evening, Yor knew only that the milk was going to be a problem.

She held the carton at an angle and read the date stamp. Tomorrow. Back into the refrigerator it went, behind the leftover stew, where Loid was less likely to discover it. He had opinions about expiration dates that she considered disproportionate to the actual science of dairy spoilage, but she'd never found a way to say that without sounding like she was defending spoiled milk, which she wasn't. What she was defending was the principle that milk didn't become poison at midnight on the printed date. She was fairly sure about this.

Bond had taken over the middle of the living room rug again, chin pressed into the worn border, sleeping with the calm certainty that everyone else would step around him. His breathing came deep and even, with a faint whistle at the end of each exhale, and one back leg twitched with whatever passed through a precognitive dog's sleeping mind. Yor sometimes caught Anya watching Bond with an expression that suggested she was listening to something Yor couldn't hear, but the habit was so old and so gentle that Yor had filed it years ago among the things about her daughter that she loved without understanding.

Across the hall, Anya's old bedroom door stood half open. The bedspread was smooth. At noon a message had come from an embassy number Yor didn't recognize: *paperwork ran long don't wait up love you*. Too neat. Too bright at the end. Yor had answered with *Eat something hot if you can*, then, because that sounded too much like an instruction, *Only if you want to!* Anya had replied with a peanut sticker and nothing else. That had not helped.

A bento box sat open on the counter. This was not strictly necessary. Loid was at the university for a consulting session, one of the occasional appointments that maintained a cover identity that had long since stopped feeling like one, and he wouldn't be back until evening. But Yor liked making them. The routine was grounding. Rice balls, three, with pickled plum. Tamagoyaki, two slices, trimmed where the browning had become something closer to charring. A thermos of mushroom miso soup from a recipe she'd clipped from a magazine in the city hall break room.

The soup tasted too salty. Not catastrophically, but enough that she could hear Anya's polite silence if she ever served it, the careful way Anya said "It's good, Mama" when what she meant was *This is mostly salt*.

The bento went into the refrigerator with a note: *The soup is maybe a little salty. Sorry! Please don't feel like you have to drink it. But I think it's okay? Yor*

As she dotted the question mark, her phone buzzed.

Not a ring. Just one hard pulse against the countertop, the pattern reserved for numbers that didn't exist in any directory. She set down the pen and picked up the phone.

An address on the northern outskirts. A name: Karl Dominic. A timeframe: tonight. Beneath it, the standard return instruction blinked in smaller text. Confirm completion within forty-five minutes of execution. And at the bottom, the six-digit code that confirmed it was real.

Twice was enough. The message would erase itself in ninety seconds. She set the phone face-down on the counter and went back to the bento box, adjusting the thermos so it wouldn't leak during refrigeration. Once the lid was closed, the knife she'd used for the tamagoyaki got washed, dried, and returned to the block.

Bond opened one eye from the rug, then closed it.

"I'm going out tonight," Yor told him. "Don't eat Papa's dinner. It's in the fridge."

She considered sending Anya another message about coats and late trams, decided she was already hovering in a way twenty-year-olds hated, and didn't. Instead she packed the leftover stew into a smaller container in case Anya came by after midnight hungry enough to stop pretending she wasn't.

After a shower, she dressed in a fitted dark jacket, pants that didn't restrict movement at the hip, and flat shoes with grip soles she'd ordered from a work-supply catalogue under the reasoning that icy sidewalks were dangerous and practical footwear was just common sense. Which was true. The shoes were also excellent for moving quietly across concrete and gravel, which was the actual reason she'd bought them.

In the closet, behind the shoe rack and a box of seasonal clothes she rotated every April and October with a conscientiousness that Loid found endearing and Anya found baffling, sat a locked case. The key lived on a chain around her neck alongside a small enamel peanut charm, a prize from an Eden Academy festival booth, won by a six-year-old Anya and presented with the solemnity of a military decoration. The charm's paint was chipping at the edges, and she'd never replaced it.

Inside the case: two blades, a garrote wire, a compact medical kit, and lockpicks that had been Garden standard issue since before Anya was born. From it she selected the longer blade and the picks. The garrote she left. It was a single target in a compound setting. The blade was cleaner, faster, something she could sheathe when the job was done. The garrote required sustained proximity she didn't enjoy. That wasn't out of squeamishness. It was because the garrote was slow, and slow meant time spent close enough to hear the sound the body made, and that was a sound she preferred to keep brief.

With the blade strapped to the inside of her left forearm, under the jacket sleeve, Yor checked the draw. The motion was smooth. The lockpicks went into her inner pocket. The phone went into the other one, screen down over the return code she was not going to think about yet.

In the hallway mirror, she looked like a woman heading out for the evening. It was a bit severe. She tried the small reception-desk smile she used at city hall when she arrived for her shift. It softened things.

On her way out, she stopped at the hall table where Loid had left a shopping list in his careful handwriting: rice, laundry soap, Bond's joint medication, milk. That reminder had been on her list all day. He would buy a new carton, find the old one hiding behind the stew, pour it out, and give her the expression. Not disapproval exactly. More the gentle bewilderment of a man who had spent decades in intelligence operations and could not fathom his wife's relationship with dairy.

At the bottom she added *milk, please check fridge first!* in her rounder, larger handwriting, and beneath it: *Back late. Don't wait up? There's stew.*

The door locked behind her. The landing smelled of the neighbor's floor wax and the building's old radiators kicking against the October chill.

Then she went to kill Karl Dominic.

---

The compound was forty minutes north of Berlint proper, where the city's residential blocks thinned into industrial lots and gated estates belonging to people who valued privacy over proximity. Yor took the tram to the northern terminal and walked the remaining two kilometers along a service road paralleling a drainage canal. The air smelled of wet concrete, diesel from a freight depot she'd passed a kilometer back, and the faintly vegetal rot of standing water in the ditch.

The tram had been half full. Shift workers folded into coats. A student asleep against the window with ink on two fingers. A woman with grocery sacks and a face so tired it looked arranged that way. Yor stood the whole ride, one hand on the rail, and read the briefing file once before it wiped itself. Karl Dominic. Fifty-three. Arms broker. His supply chain had been linked to the recent military buildup along the Ostanian border: weapons transfers routed through shell companies, warehouse leases, and stamped invoices that made rifles travel dressed as something else. The Garden's assessment classified him as a destabilizing agent. Eliminate. Standard contract.

Standard. The word belonged to the paper trail around every job: assignment number, return code, the deposit arriving on the fifteenth. Take the name. Do the work. Wipe the blade clean before it dried. Be home in time to pack leftovers and answer Loid when he asked how city hall had been. If she stopped in the middle and asked who had checked the file, who had signed off, whose daughter was waiting in some apartment with the light on, the whole mechanism would seize. So she had not asked. She had called that trust.

So had the shipping clerk. Three months ago. A man in the port district whose briefing profile listed arms connections that turned out, when she'd checked afterward, to be invoices for industrial machine parts. Legitimate equipment for a legitimate factory. The man had begged, not for himself, which she could manage, but for his daughter, who was waiting at home. They sometimes begged. She'd trained herself to close that channel years ago. But the invoices. The gap between the profile and the evidence. She'd reported the discrepancy to her handler. The handler had said: *Thank you, Yor. Don't worry about it. These things are reviewed.* And she'd stopped worrying.

Mostly, she'd stopped worrying.

The landscape changed as she walked. Berlint's streetlights gave way to intermittent industrial security lamps, and the sounds of the city, tram bells, traffic, a bar's music leaking from somewhere, faded into the low drone of freight machinery and then, past the last depot, into nothing much at all. The service road was unpaved beyond the terminal's reach: packed dirt and gravel that crunched under her soles. Past a shuttered machine shop came a fenced lot full of construction aggregate, then a drainage pump station where someone had left a folding chair and an empty thermos. The only other living thing she'd encountered in the last kilometer was a cat that crossed the road, evaluated her with the professional indifference of an animal that knew it couldn't be caught, and vanished into a culvert pipe.

Half past ten found her at the compound. The perimeter was a three-meter concrete wall topped with razor wire and motion sensors at the corners. Beyond it: the roofline of the main building, interior lights, the muted geometry of an estate that had been a manufacturing facility before Dominic repurposed it. Sealed loading docks on the east side. The main gate faced south, connected to the highway by a paved access road.

Surveillance settled into place. Three men visible at first glance: one stationed at the gate, shifting his weight foot to foot with the restlessness of a man assigned a post he found boring; one near a cargo van parked in the loading area, its rear doors open; one walking the interior perimeter on a patrol route. She timed the patrol. Four minutes per circuit. Consistent rhythm. The pace of routine rather than alertness. A second look caught more: office lights burning in the southwest corner of the main building, the intermittent shadow of men crossing a conference room farther in, the bright square of paperwork being moved from one hand to another. A live deal, not storage.

The north wall had a vulnerability. A motion sensor housing mounted at an angle that covered the airspace above the wall rather than the wall face itself, a factory installation never corrected. Exactly the mistake that stayed in place when security was maintained by men who filled out forms instead of climbing walls.

When the patrol rounded the south wall, she went up the north wall at the sensor gap and dropped into the compound's northeast corner behind a maintenance shed. The shed smelled of machine oil and dried grass clippings. Thirty still seconds passed while the patrol guard's boots crunched by on the other side, steady and unhurried, the steps of a man completing a task he'd completed a thousand times without incident.

The crunch faded toward the south. She moved.

The man at the cargo van was leaning against the rear bumper, smoking. His sidearm sat on his right hip, holstered, the retention snap undone. It was structural carelessness. He'd never encountered trouble here, and the boredom of guard work had eroded whatever discipline he'd once maintained. Yor came around the van's blind side. The last three meters took less than a second. She put the blade through the base of his skull at the junction where the brainstem lived, and he dropped. She caught his weight against the van's bumper and lowered him to the pavement without noise. His cigarette rolled under the tire.

Inside the van: long wooden crates, unmarked, secured with military-grade packaging straps. The chemical smell of preservative oil seeped through the wood grain. Rifles. She counted twelve crates. Enough for a company.

From there she moved toward the main building.

The loading dock's sealed entrance had a secondary door, service access, a standard pin-tumbler lock that the picks handled in nine seconds. A corridor inside, fluorescent tubes overhead, half of them dead and the survivors flickering with a buzz that set her teeth on edge. An office door on the left, closed. Voices from deeper in the building, male, three or four of them, the measured rhythm of negotiation. The private sale. Buyers in the building. A steel fire door marked MAINTENANCE STAIR sat at the corridor branch, paint bubbled near the bottom from old flooding.

That changed the approach. Buyers meant witnesses and potentially more armed men. She needed the principal and the exit, nothing else.

Two guards in the corridor, stationed outside a conference room. The first noticed her at six meters and reached for his radio. Wrong decision. Yor closed the gap in a stride and drove the heel of her palm into his trachea. He folded with a wet, shocked gasp. The second guard was faster. He'd drawn his sidearm by the time she reached him. But his partner was between them, and the half-second he spent calculating a clear firing line was a half-second he didn't have. Her blade opened the inside of his forearm from wrist to elbow, deep enough to ruin the arm, and the gun clattered to the floor tiles. An elbow below his ear dropped him after that.

The first guard's radio came with her. The channel stayed quiet long enough for a fragment from the conference room to carry through the door.

"Not until Karl says," one man said. "He wants the papers in order."

Dominic liked control. No one would burst into his office uninvited while buyers were waiting to be told they could touch what they'd paid for. That would buy her minutes. Not many.

The corridor branched. Left toward the offices. Right toward the voices. She went left. The buyers were not her contract.

Karl Dominic was alone in a corner office at the corridor's end, behind a desk buried in working stacks of paperwork, speaking into a phone. He was shorter than his file photo suggested, stocky, balding, in a dress shirt with the sleeves pushed past his elbows. The office smelled of cigar smoke layered over cheap instant coffee from the tins stacked in freight depot commissaries. A framed photograph on the desk showed Dominic with a woman on a boat, somewhere sunny, both of them squinting at the camera with the particular resignation of people who'd been told to smile.

He looked up when the door opened.

"No," he said into the phone, already irritated, already mid-argument. "I want the confirmations in front of me before they touch the second shipment. If Voss wants deniability, he can pay for it twice."

Then his face processed confusion, recognition, fear.

He dropped the phone and lunged for the desk drawer.

Yor was faster. She had been faster than every person she'd ever been sent to kill, and twenty years had not changed that. The speed lived below thought, below decision, in the architecture of muscle and nerve that the Garden had trained and she had maintained through thousands of hours of practice she told Loid was yoga. Her blade caught his reaching wrist before his hand found the drawer, and the cut was deep enough to part tendons. He screamed, short and bitten off, with no room left in it for dignity.

He tried to stand. She put the blade through the left side of his neck, angled for the carotid, and held it for the two seconds it took. His legs buckled. He went down against the desk, scattering papers, and slid to the floor trailing a red line down the laminate front.

The phone was still connected. A tinny voice on the other end asked, "Karl? Karl, what happened?"

Yor disconnected the call.

The desk intercom buzzed almost at once. Once. Then again. She looked down. Beside the blotter lay a cream index card in Dominic's thick handwriting.

22:35 buyers seated  
22:40 signatures  
22:45 yard release

The wall clock above the filing cabinet read 22:38.

Wiping the blade on the hem of Dominic's shirt, she sheathed it. Her hands were steady. They were always steady during the work. They didn't shake or hesitate or feel the weight of what they'd done until afterward, when the adrenaline burned off and she was just Yor again in a kitchen with a knife and a cutting board, pausing over carrots long enough to blame the tremor on the cold.

The papers on the desk were organized in working stacks, not abandoned clutter. Tonight's crates. Tonight's buyers. Tonight's payments.

Garden training had been explicit on this point. The Garden provided the briefing; the operative executed the contract. Investigation was a different role, different people, a different level of the organization. For twenty years, that division had been enough.

But the shipping clerk. The invoices that hadn't matched. The handler's voice: *Don't worry about it.* And the daughter, waiting at home.

Yor looked at the papers scattered across Dominic's desk and the ones that had fallen to the floor, some of them soaking up the blood pooling from his neck, and she didn't leave.

The top stack held shipping manifests and transfer orders for military hardware: rifles, communications equipment, explosive ordnance, all routed through holding companies whose names meant nothing but whose addresses landed in Berlint's government district, near the defense ministry. The shipment codes repeated. So did a name. Werner Voss.

The intercom buzzed a third time. In the conference room, chairs moved. Yor set her phone to silent, braced it over the desk, and took the fastest images first: cover sheet, buyer list, Voss transfer page, the order for encrypted field communications equipment that had moved six weeks earlier to an anonymous warehouse near the ministry. The quantities suggested equipping a field unit, not filling a depot. An active operation, being assembled through Karl Dominic's desk.

Under the manifests sat a second packet, clipped to the first on heavier cream stock. Those were Dominic's confirmations.

The radio in her hand hissed. "Gate to corridor," a man said. "We need Karl in two."

These were not freight receipts or customs clearances. They were completion records, the back-office proof Dominic would use to show a liability had been handled and demand payment for it. One look told her why they felt familiar. The forms used the same sequence as the Garden's own closeout notices: assignment number, compensation release, executing party.

The contracting entity was coded. She didn't recognize the designator. The executing entity, on the summary sheet clipped to the front, appeared nine times across four years under a numerical code Yor knew as well as her own address. She'd seen it on her payment records for twenty years, on the deposits that arrived on the fifteenth of each month, on the brief confirmations after each completed assignment, on the secure documents she signed when she accepted new contracts.

The Garden.

The code sat in the same place she usually looked for her own fee. Her fingertips slipped on the cream paper. For the first time that night the room gave a small, ugly tilt under her shoes.

Faster now, she read. There were nine assignments: liability removal and witness management. They were not border threats or coup planners. They were cleanup for bad shipments and men who had seen too much. She photographed the summary page, then the payment table behind it, the release dates landing on the fifteenth like the deposits in her own account. A door latch clicked in the corridor. Someone said something annoyed and too close to make out.

One entry carried a date from three months ago and a district marker from the port. She found the corresponding page halfway down the packet and snapped it before the words stopped blurring. There were the machine-parts invoices again. There was the clerk's name. She saw the cramped office, the stack of stamped forms, the moment he started begging only when he understood she wasn't there to threaten him. He had said his daughter was waiting at home.

The shipping clerk had not been an exception. Or if he had been, this dead man had eight others filed beside him.

There were still pages under her thumb. She did not get them. The hall outside had changed shape. A buyer's voice carried clear now, no longer muffled by conference-room walls. "Where is he?"

Yor took one last image of the page headers still buried in the packet, enough to keep the assignment numbers and dates, then laid the cream stock back where she'd found it. Taking the whole packet would announce the search before she reached the yard. Leaving it meant Dominic's people might not know for minutes which pages had been looked at and which hadn't.

Karl Dominic had not hidden a confession in a lucky drawer. He had been working through tonight's sale with the paperwork laid out exactly as he needed it, shipment on top and cleanup underneath, because men like him believed paper was insurance.

Twenty years. The number landed like a deposit date. For twenty years the Garden had handed her a name, a district, a return code, and she had sharpened blades, wiped sinks, hung up her coat, and told herself Loid's shoes by the door and Anya's bed across the hall stayed safe because she did what was necessary.

Now an arms broker had kept proof that the Garden did cleanup for his supply chain in the same neat stacks as freight paperwork.

What if the shipping clerk had not been a mistake. What if the mistake was every time she'd folded a closeout slip and put it away without looking harder.

No clear next step followed that thought. The blank space frightened her. She had always known what came next.

The first shout went up as she reached the corridor. Not a full alarm, not yet. Just the shocked bark of a man finding the guard on the floor outside the conference room. The clean route was gone. She hit the maintenance stair instead, took the steps in silence, and came out through a lower service bay that stank of solvent and wet cardboard. By the time she crossed the yard, the perimeter patrol was already turning north twenty seconds ahead of schedule.

Back over the wall she went, using the same sensor gap and not enough time. Her left hand caught the razor wire on the descent. She felt the resistance and the tug but not the pain. The pain arrived thirty seconds later on the service road, a line of hot sting across her palm that resolved into a shallow cut running along the old scar from when Anya was eight and learning to cut carrots and the knife had slipped from her small grip and caught Yor's hand instead. Anya had cried for twenty minutes. Yor hadn't minded the cut. She'd held her bleeding palm behind her back so Anya wouldn't feel bad and said, "See? It's nothing. Let's try again."

With the cut wrapped in her jacket sleeve, she kept walking.

Then she saw the ditch.

At the base of the wall, where the service road curved toward the canal, the weeds beside the industrial waste containers had been pressed flat into a human hollow. A cheap thermos lay on its side with the cap screwed back on tight. Beside it sat compact field glasses wrapped in dark tape to kill reflection, a pencil worn down to the metal, and a folded municipal survey map pinned under a broken brick. The compound perimeter had been sketched on the back in quick pencil strokes: south gate, loading dock, patrol route, the north sensor gap she'd used. In the upper corner someone had written `WS-7`.

Somebody had been here long enough to count.

Movement broke beside the containers.

A figure rose. One shoulder lower under a light pack. Weight on the back foot. Small enough to be underestimated.

Yor's mind measured the distance, the angle, the quickest path to the throat.

Then the figure stepped into the spill of security light and it was Anya.

Yor stopped. Her daughter was in dark clothes, not the diplomatic wardrobe, not the outfits of a cultural attaché at Westalian embassy events, but cut from the same logic as Yor's own: cloth that wouldn't shine, snag, or hold the light. Anya's hair was pulled back under a dark knit cap. Mud darkened the knees of her trousers. A compact camera strap crossed her ribs under the jacket. The field glasses on the ground were hers. So was the map.

Anya looked too thin. She always looked thin. Milk and soup were small problems with practical answers. This belonged to the other pile, the ache that stayed lodged behind Yor's ribs because Anya was twenty and an adult and there was no decent way to make an adult eat.

Neither of them spoke for a moment. The canal water moved in the ditch. The compound's lights threw a pale halo against the low October clouds.

"You should eat more," Yor said. "You look thin."

Anya's mouth opened. Closed. Her eyes dropped to Yor's left hand, the jacket sleeve dark and stiff with blood, the makeshift wrapping already soaking through.

"Your hands are bleeding, Mama."

"Oh." Yor looked at her hand as if the blood were a social inconvenience, like noticing a stain on her shirt at a work dinner. "It's, um. It's not bad. I scraped it on a fence. It's really not deep. I just didn't have a, um. I'll clean it when I get home."

Her own voice sounded absurd in the canal dark. Fence. As if Anya were twelve and had found her after grocery shopping instead of outside a weapons broker's wall with surveillance notes pinned under a brick.

Yor looked at the map again. "What are you doing here?"

Anya followed her glance and, for a second, seemed to consider lying. Yor watched the thought pass through her face and go.

"I had a line from the west side," Anya said. "One corridor. It ended here."

The words were plain. The meaning inside them was not.

"I was supposed to watch the gate," Anya said. "See who came out after Dominic. Get a plate number, maybe a face. Something I could use."

She swallowed. "I didn't think it was going to be you."

Yor's eyes went to the taped field glasses, the notes, the careful little pencil marks counting patrol time. Not an accident, then. Not curiosity. Her daughter had come north from some other piece of trouble and laid herself flat in canal weeds to watch a killer's house breathe.

"You were going to follow them?" Yor asked.

"If I had to."

The answer came too fast. Anya heard it too and made a face at herself, small and irritated.

"I mean," she said, "I was going to decide that when I knew who it was. I hadn't really finished the plan."

That was so recognizably Anya that Yor almost smiled. It vanished before it could settle.

Behind the wall, someone shouted again. This time the word *body* carried clearly.

Anya's gaze flicked to the compound, then to the phone-shaped weight in Yor's pocket, then back to her face. A change passed over her there, quick and impossible to name. Not surprise exactly. Not fear. Something like recognition, but pointed in a direction Yor could not place and could not safely ignore.

"They were waiting on signatures," Anya said. "If anything's missing, they'll know fast."

Yor said nothing.

Anya held her eyes. "Don't make whatever call comes next tonight. Just go home."

The night seemed to thin around that sentence.

Yor didn't ask how Anya knew there was a call. She didn't ask what the west side had been, or who had sent her here, or why her daughter now sounded like someone who understood reporting windows and search patterns and the cost of using a burned line one stop too soon. Anya didn't ask why Yor had Dominic's blood on her sleeve, or what was in the pocket she'd glanced at twice, or whether the look on Yor's face belonged to injury or something worse.

Those questions stayed where they always stayed between them, large and untouched. But the space around them had changed shape. It was no longer ignorance. It was arrangement.

"I need to get back," Yor said, gesturing toward the road to the tram station. The old line came to her out of habit and sounded childish now. "It's late."

"Yeah." Anya nodded once. "I'm not coming with you."

"Right. Of course."

Another silence sat down between them. The canal gurgled. A light snapped on above the compound's loading dock and threw a harder glare across the road.

"Were you here alone?" Yor asked.

"Yes. It was just watching. I wasn't going in."

That answer she believed. It did not reassure her.

"Be careful getting home," Yor said. "It's cold tonight. You have a proper coat? Not just that one?"

"I know the route. I've got layers."

"There's stew in the fridge. From yesterday. If you want to stop by. You don't have to. But it's there."

"I'll come by. Tomorrow, maybe."

"And the soup. The mushroom one. It's new. It might be..." Yor glanced helplessly at her bleeding hand as if it had personally interrupted her sentence. "I think I used too much miso. But it's fine. Probably. It's an experiment."

"It's probably not that bad. I'll try it."

Yor wanted to cross the six paces between them. She wanted to put a hand on the back of Anya's head and say something ordinary enough to get them safely through the night. Instead she kept both hands to herself. One was bleeding. The other had touched Dominic's paperwork and carried photographs of the Garden's code in an arms broker's office.

"Goodnight, Anya."

"Night, Mama."

Yor turned toward the tram road. Looking back felt impossible. Even so, before the bend took the canal out of sight, she heard the small sounds of Anya repacking the observation post: thermos cap, brick against gravel, paper folding once and then again.

By the time she reached the dead streetlamp near the service-road junction, the domestic thoughts were trying to reassemble themselves: stew in the fridge, soup too salty, whether Anya had lied about the coat, whether there was enough rice left for breakfast. Under them sat the other things, flat and impossible. Nine contracts. Four years. The Garden's code on Dominic's papers. WS-7 on Anya's map. Her daughter saying *don't make whatever call comes next tonight* in a voice that assumed Yor would understand her.

She tried, uselessly, to fit the pieces together in an order that made sense. Anya had come north from a west-side line with a map and a camera. Dominic had kept Garden work under his shipment papers. Somewhere between those two facts lay a shape large enough to hold her marriage, her daughter, twenty years of contracts, and whatever she had been telling herself all that time. She could not hold the whole thing at once. She could only hold the next practical fact: if Garden touched the canal before dawn, the hollow in the weeds would stop being a question and start being a trail.

The phone in her pocket vibrated once: not a call, just the automated reminder that the confirmation window was closing.

Yor took it out under the dead streetlamp. The screen showed the unentered completion field and eleven minutes remaining. If she answered, Karl Dominic would become just another completed line item. Garden would know she had stayed long enough to touch his desk. They would audit the breach, reconstruct the yard, sweep the north wall, the ditch, the flattened weeds where someone had lain with field glasses and a map marked `WS-7`.

Her thumb rested over the keypad. For twenty years she had never missed a confirmation window. Not once. Completion, code, silence. Then home.

Tonight she slid the battery free instead.

She put the phone in one pocket and the battery in the other. The dark screen reflected nothing back at her. By the time the tram came, the window would die on its own.

Then she kept walking toward home.


# Chapter 11

After the canal, Anya spent three days inside WISE headquarters, and by now the fluorescent buzz felt lodged behind her eyes.

Not from the reception itself. Crowds gave her the bright-light headache and then let go. This was three days of precision work: engineered elevator rides, timed coffee runs, standing beside colleagues with a dead phone in her hand while their thoughts scraped past hers in fragments, taking the long way to the archive because two analysts from signals usually argued there at 10:15 and one of them had clearance she needed to hear from up close. The most invasive thing she had ever done on purpose.

Yor kept breaking through it anyway. Dominic's records. Garden's code in Dominic's paperwork. The crack that had opened in her mother's head on the service road. But Anya had nothing she could carry into a briefing folder, no way to ask Yor what she'd found without admitting she'd stolen it out of her mind, and no reason to think Yor would tell anyone before she tried to think her way back into obedience. That was the bet Anya had made when she turned away instead of following Yor home. Maybe Yor would go quiet for a few days. Maybe, in those same few days, Garden would clean the trail and Yor would let them. Damian's warning about the WISE leak felt shorter than that. So Anya came to headquarters and stayed.

In her fourth-floor cubicle, coffee went cold beside an access-log spreadsheet. The fluorescents buzzed. Reheated fish from the break room was making the whole floor smell like punishment. Outside, two analysts argued about sourcing methodology while one of them worried about his dead dog and the other about an overdue credit-card bill.

Reading WISE all day got her the same junk everybody else carried around: errands, resentment, money, lunch, whether anyone had replaced the toilet paper on the third floor, whether a mole on somebody's shoulder looked worse than last week, whether the vending machine was still eating coins. Intelligence came in bursts. Between bursts, life kept being stupid and ordinary.

It had started with a single fragment from the diplomatic reception: *the asset inside WISE*. A mole, somewhere in a crowd too dense to pin to one skull. She had tried replaying the room afterward, flat on her bed with a pillow over her face to block the morning light, but telepathy wasn't a recording. It happened once and then it was memory like any other, full of holes and false certainty.

So she'd started the other way. Paper first, the way Loid had taught her since she was sixteen: before you use the cheat, do the work. Access logs. Intercepts. Who sat in which briefing, then somehow the Renewal Front knew the same thing a day later. Grosz had helped by cutting the whole giant bureaucratic mess down to the parts that actually mattered: army planning, contract lanes, the places where decisions turned into parts and shipments. If somebody inside WISE was feeding that machine, the access pattern should touch those lanes.

By Monday noon she had a private grid open across three windows: names down the left, compartments across the top, then print releases, late badge swipes, leave blocks, briefing attendance, routing authority, and a last column she hadn't labeled because the label would have been *things I only know because I stood next to people and stole them.* She let herself write in it anyway. Berger: hates Vienna posting. Dreher: hiding medical thing? Falk: gives nothing. Then she stared at the words until the shame stopped being dramatic and turned useful.

Eleven analysts had the clearance and the opportunity. Two weeks of paperwork got that down to seven. Two had been out of Berlint during key leak windows. Two more never touched the right compartments. One had the right access but spent every suspicious evening in a gambling room loud enough that even telepathy would have had to fight the slot machines. Another was having an affair so obvious in his thoughts that Anya wanted to apologize to every person who had ever accidentally stood near her. Brief reads of colleagues whose surface minds were banal, frightened, horny, or furious in ways that had nothing to do with procurement channels dropped the list again. By Monday evening she had three names.

Anna-Lise Berger had been one of them. Berger's file looked bad in the abstract: access to the right summaries, two after-hours printer releases, a stretch of bitterness sharp enough to read from across a conference table. An elevator ride killed that. Berger's head was full of Vienna resentment, her mother's slipping memory, a dentist appointment she'd been avoiding for three months, the dull ache of being forty-three and professionally overlooked, and a panicked internal reminder to buy incontinence pads before visiting her mother again because the last time she'd forgotten and spent two hours pretending not to notice the wet chair. When the doors opened, Berger smiled at Anya and stepped out still innocent, and Anya stepped out carrying things Berger had never offered her.

She ducked into the stairwell afterward and stood with one hand on the rail until the elevator started moving again. Protect your own secret by prying under everybody else's. Good work. Real principled stuff.

That left two. Martin Dreher and Gerald Falk.

Dreher worked signals analysis on the third floor. He was fifty-one. He drank black coffee with two sugars in the cheap styrofoam cups everybody hated. A desk photo showed his wife, two sons, and a Golden Retriever who looked disappointed to be there. He had been in the building fifteen years and brought Friday pastries from a bakery near his apartment. He was the kind of colleague people described by habit because the habits were what you noticed first.

His file looked worse than Berger's had. Dreher's desk touched the Baltic readiness brief, border-positioning summaries, and the procurement annexes Grosz had effectively put a red circle around. Over the previous month he'd pulled the same bands of material the Renewal Front kept staying a step ahead of. Three times he had released hard-copy packets from the third-floor secure printer and then vanished from the building for somewhere between ninety minutes and two hours. Three times the Renewal Front had later anticipated deployments or inspections that should have stayed inside WISE. On paper it looked clean in the ugliest way.

Anya spent most of Tuesday morning making herself prove it twice.

She checked the access times again. 13:08 on the procurement annexes. 13:14 on the border summaries. Secure print release at 13:23, user tag unresolved. Dreher badged out at 13:41. Leak window opened that evening.

Then another. 14:02, 14:05, 14:11. Print release. Blank calendar block. Dreher gone for an hour and forty-eight minutes. By the next morning the Renewal Front had already shifted material off a route WISE had planned to watch.

Then the third. Same lane. Same disappearing act.

It was enough to make her go downstairs instead of forwarding the pattern immediately. If she was going to light a fire under internal security, she wanted his face in the room with the numbers.

Tuesday morning put him alone in the break room with an empty mug in his hand, staring at the motivational poster above the microwave as if the eagle might tell him what to do next. Anya filled her own cup and stopped within range.

His thoughts hit like cold water.

*Tuesday. He said Tuesday. If the second panel is worse than the first one, if Kessler was wrong, if I've got three weeks, maybe four before it shows, before Eva looks at me and knows. Nine months with her mother and I can't do that to her. I can't.*

The panic broke, re-formed, and tried to pass as work.

*Budget meeting at two. Baltic assessment. Focus.*

Then it slipped again.

*Second referral first. Referral means paperwork. Paperwork means the WISE medical plan. HR sees the claim and I'm done. Cash for one more visit. Kessler at four. Tell Eva it's Wilkinson. Tell her I'm staying late. Please just believe that one more time.*

By the time the thought-stream collapsed into raw fear, Anya's hand had tightened hard enough on the cup to make the styrofoam creak.

"Hey, Martin."

He startled. "Anya. Hi. Morning."

"You okay? You look a little..."

"Fine." Too fast. "Just a long week. You know how it is."

*Don't ask. Please don't ask. Not here.*

"Sure," she said. "Get some rest."

Back upstairs she tried, for twelve whole minutes, to make the read mean anything else.

Maybe Kessler was a cutout. Maybe referral meant the first step in some contact protocol. Maybe cash-only plus no paper trail plus timed absences plus the exact right document pulls was enough even if the fear underneath it wasn't about ideology. People did dangerous things for reasons smaller than belief all the time. Debt. Shame. Family. Disease. A private crisis didn't clear him. If anything, private crises made people vulnerable.

That line held until she opened his work lane and saw how neatly the bad facts stacked.

She pulled Dreher's access history, printer releases, and leave calendar into the same window and stared until the pattern stopped looking accidental. Three leak windows matched three unexplained afternoon absences, each between ninety minutes and two hours. Each absence landed after he'd pulled procurement annexes or border-positioning summaries that the Renewal Front had later anticipated almost exactly. Nothing on his calendar explained those gaps. Nothing in the user-facing system accounted for the hard-copy releases. Today's schedule had another blank block beginning at 3:40.

She checked the routing authority on the packets. Wilkinson had signed the broader Baltic tasking, but not the individual print pulls. She checked the printer room logs. Same unresolved section-code release all three days. She checked Dreher's desk assignment notes from the shared project folder. Half the filenames were routine. Half touched exactly the lanes Damian had warned her were still hot. She checked the archive request tray and found his name again in the handwriting box, request submitted for hard-copy review because "screen comparison unreliable." That might have meant aging eyes. It might have meant he didn't want a digital trail.

At 11:40 she went back downstairs under the excuse of a copier jam and walked past his cubicle. Dreher wasn't there. His desk was too neat for somebody in panic except for one open drawer crammed with antacid tablets, a crushed receipts envelope, and a bakery box folded flat for reuse. The family photo sat angled toward his chair. Next to it was a yellow legal pad with budget numbers in one column and three dates circled hard enough to bruise the paper.

Clinic dates, maybe.

Handoff dates, maybe.

The problem was that both stories used the same evidence.

At 12:06 she came back up and opened Falk's personnel file, willing the answer to be there instead because Falk made more sense as a traitor and less sense as a man with a hidden oncologist. Twenty years older than her, senior enough to move paperwork without being noticed, close enough to Loid that betrayal would actually count. But Falk's recent access looked broad in the ordinary way senior analysts looked broad, and the telepathic passes she had managed on him had turned up nothing except weather, budgets, and the kind of tidy administrative annoyance that clung to men who alphabetized their own tea tins.

By 1:30 the choice had narrowed to something simpler and worse. If she waited for cleaner proof on Falk, Dreher's 3:40 blank block would come and go. If Dreher was the leak and she sat on it because cancer was an uglier secret than treason, then the next warning would reach the Renewal Front before anyone inside WISE moved.

The pattern said four o'clock with Kessler, cash only, no paper trail.

If that was a handoff and she spent the afternoon trying to get a cleaner read on Falk, she'd be waiting through another leak to avoid making herself uncomfortable. Falk was still there. Dreher had a clock on him.

By 3:12 she had written the memo.

The wording was careful. Too careful. Nothing in it exposed how she knew. She listed the paper pieces one by one: overlapping access, weird hours, missing afternoons sitting right on top of the old leak windows, hard-copy packets that seemed to vanish, a man looking like he was about to come apart. Then she cut that last one back to "observable strain" because she couldn't write *I heard him thinking about dying in the break room,* deleted "sudden" from the line above, and printed the page.

Kurtz took it in internal security without reaction. Mid-forties, flat voice, office that smelled like stale paper and orange-peel cleaner.

She read the first paragraph while Anya stood there and said, "Why now?"

"Because there's another gap this afternoon."

"And not yesterday?"

Anya kept her face still. "Yesterday it was a pattern. Today it's a clock."

Kurtz looked back down at the paper. Not suspicious. Not reassuring either. Just evaluating. "If you're wrong, this burns a senior analyst for having a private life."

Too late to pretend that wasn't exactly what she was doing. "If I'm right, he gets the rest of the week."

Kurtz set the memo on the in-tray with one finger. "We'll look."

Counterintelligence yanked two analysts off the Ostanian desk the next morning. IT went rooting through Dreher's terminal traffic and comms metadata. A watcher took the desk opposite his in the bullpen with a legal pad and the posture of a man pretending not to watch the exact person he was watching. By lunchtime the whole third floor was walking funny, compensating around holes nobody was allowed to name while Dreher moved through the middle of it under surveillance he hadn't agreed to and couldn't yet feel.

Anya felt it for him anyway.

She felt it in the way the floor had gone fractionally wrong. Conversations stopped when he approached and restarted too fast after he passed. Printer queues backed up because the extra reviewer assigned to cover him didn't know the third-floor machine needed to be kicked low on the left side to catch the second tray. Somebody in logistics kept thinking *which one is it* every time he saw a security officer walk by. Dreher himself seemed to notice only that people were busier than usual. His surface thoughts stayed trapped inside appointments, numbers, and the repeated impossible task of deciding whether to tell Eva before or after the next scan.

At lunch on Wednesday she passed him in the corridor with a paper cup of soup in one hand and a folder tucked under his arm. He nodded like always.

"Afternoon."

"Hey."

*Don't spill. Don't spill on the report. Tell Eva Thursday. No, tonight. No. Tonight and then she looks at you differently forever.*

Anya made it all the way to the stairwell before the guilt hit hard enough to bend her over the railing.

The investigation ate two days.

Late on the second evening, Kurtz's report hit Anya's secure inbox. Three lines in, she got up from her desk and took it into the bathroom.

Martin Dreher was not selling intelligence to the Renewal Front.

Three weeks earlier, Martin Dreher had been diagnosed with Stage III pancreatic cancer. Dr. Kessler was an oncologist at a private clinic across the city. The referrals were medical. The cash was medical. The blank blocks in his afternoons were appointments, imaging, treatment planning, and time parked two streets over because he couldn't yet drive straight home and tell his wife it was happening to him now, not someday. He'd kept it off the WISE plan because a flagged claim would pull him from active work before he was ready to be looked at like a man leaving.

But Kurtz's report didn't stop there. Counterintelligence had tailed him on Tuesday and Wednesday. Both times Dreher left the building alone, took the tram to Zeidler Private Oncology, signed in under his own name, met with Kessler, then sat in his car on a side street with both hands locked on the steering wheel before driving home. No third-party contact. No alternate route. No communication with any known Renewal Front intermediary before, during, or after the appointments.

The document pulls were real and they were legitimate. Wilkinson had assigned Dreher to the Baltic contingency packet after Geller's intercept note, which meant the procurement annexes and border-positioning summaries Anya had circled were exactly what he was supposed to be reading for the weekly readiness brief. The "missing" printer releases had not disappeared into a handoff. They had gone through a third-floor secure batch queue mapped to the section code instead of individual user badges; security recovered the binders from Wilkinson's locked project cabinet with Dreher's tabs and margin notes still in them. Terminal audit clean. Communications audit clean. Personal finance clean except for the clinic withdrawals.

The leak-window alignment had been the cruelest part. Those documents passed through half the desk in the twenty-four hours before every readiness brief, which was exactly when the real mole needed them most. Dreher's absences had overlapped the leaks because his appointments happened to fall on the same afternoons Falk, Wilkinson, Geller, and two duty officers were all moving through the same circulation chain. Anya had mistaken shared timing for a private channel.

The last page listed the remaining names with access to those packets under the same project code. Gerald Falk was on it.

In the stall, the folder sat open on her lap while warm air pushed through the vent overhead. Somebody came in, used the sink, left again. Another person followed, thinking in loud irritated fragments about a train delay and a tampon she'd forgotten to replace. The thoughts crashed against Anya's skull and kept going.

Every piece she'd read had been true: Tuesday, cash, the second referral, the terror. None of it had been about treason. A man trying to keep chemo off an HR system had sounded, at the surface, exactly like a man preparing a covert handoff, and she had handed that mistake to counterintelligence in a neat memo.

Two analysts lost two days on him. IT burned hours on the terminal of a man who had been reading survival rates on his lunch break. Physical surveillance watched him come out of an oncology clinic with his face wrecked from trying not to cry in public. Somewhere in the building, the actual mole had all that room.

His desk photo came back to her, along with the Friday pastries and the low tuneless humming from three floors down. The flattened bakery box. The antacids. The legal pad with those three dates stabbed into it. She had turned the worst private fear of his life into a security referral and then made half the building rearrange itself around the damage.

Cold metal bit into the scar in her left palm while she held the stall latch and waited for the nausea to level out. At the sink, the face in the mirror looked the same. Trusting it felt harder.

She closed the report, then opened it again because hating herself was not the same thing as fixing the hole.

Falk on the last page. Falk still inside the chain.

She stood at the bathroom sink until the fluorescent hum flattened back into one sound she could stand.

---

Gerald Falk was in his usual corner of the fourth-floor bullpen the next morning with tea and a cable digest. Seeing him there felt less like spotting a colleague than realizing a familiar fixture in the room had shifted half an inch overnight. He had once shown up at her Eden graduation with a silver fountain pen that still lived in her desk drawer. Not a sentimental gift, exactly. Useful. He'd said a person should own at least one pen that made ugly paperwork feel expensive.

The shared printer gave her an excuse to stand near him. Pages fed out. She straightened them one by one and stayed inside his radius.

"You look tired," Falk said without lifting his eyes from the digest. "Eat breakfast."

His mind moved under the words in tidy little rows.

*Budget allocation for Q3 is twelve percent high again. Same trick as last year. Sylvia won't fight the escalation numbers because they're useful. Pension committee at noon. Actuarial adjustment still wrong. Might rain.*

"I'm fine," Anya said. "Long week."

"Isn't there always."

*Wilkinson routing note is sloppy. Geller used one intercept and called it a pattern. Move the extra eyes. Fix it later.*

Forty seconds passed. Then a minute. His thoughts kept moving through budget irritation, pension math, weather, soup rotation. Then, warm and unguarded in the middle of it:

*She doesn't eat enough. Loid used to do the same thing.*

That was all. There was no spike, no concealed channel, no buried panic broadcasting through the walls. After Dreher, the quiet was almost worse. Noise had made her wrong. Silence gave her nothing to be wrong about.

Back at her desk she opened Kurtz's attachment again, this time not for the shame. She pulled the circulation-chain names into a separate note and started working through them one by one. Wilkinson had authority but not enough unsupervised time on the live desks. Geller's access was narrow and loud; if he moved a channel, three people complained in writing. The duty officers rotated too often. Falk sat at the crossing point: senior enough to alter coverage, ordinary enough to vanish into procedure, trusted enough that nobody read his tidy annotations as ambition.

She opened the Wilkinson file that Falk's stray thought had tagged. Falk had annotated it at 8:12 that morning and signed off on a further coverage shift starting the next day at 0600. Two more analysts off live review of the Ostanian diplomatic procurement traffic. Temporary on paper. Forty-eight hours. Bureaucratically clean. The exact desk Damian had spent the last week warning her the Renewal Front still used to move material and instructions.

It wasn't the only clean cut.

She dug back through the previous ten days and found three smaller reallocations with Falk's initials on them. A one-shift analyst loan from procurement review to embassy media traffic. Harmless if you believed Geller's note. A delayed archive pull on border customs chatter because the desk was "overcovered." Sensible if you believed Anya's false assessment had bought them room. A request to widen a watchlist on Westalian student groups while narrowing one on Ostanian freight brokers. Perfectly defensible in isolation. Together they formed a pattern of tiny absences around exactly the lanes Grosz and Damian kept naming.

The first of the edits had landed the morning after Sylvia accepted Anya's report on Damian.

She sat back in her chair so fast it squealed.

There it was. Not proof. Worse than proof, in a way. A shape built out of things she'd made easier for somebody else to do.

Her false report had not created Falk. It had created air. He'd been breathing it ever since.

From the cubicle across the aisle, somebody was thinking furiously about a yeast infection and whether business-casual allowed boots with a heel. From the far hall came a burst of laughter, then the stray thought *if he brings tuna again I'm transferring departments.* Headquarters kept being headquarters while the page on Anya's screen quietly accused her.

She opened her desk drawer and found the fountain pen where it always lay under spare batteries and a pack of headache tablets. Silver barrel, black grip, weighty in the hand. Falk had given it to her outside Eden's assembly hall while Loid was trapped in traffic and Yor was trying not to cry onto Anya's shoulder.

"For forms worth signing," he'd said, and then, because Anya had been eighteen and suspicious of all adults on principle, "It isn't a bribe. You're not important enough yet."

She had laughed. He had, too.

Now she rolled the pen between her fingers and thought about all the things administrative men could kill without ever touching a gun. A desk didn't go blind in one dramatic flash. A desk went blind because somebody wrote TEMPORARY RESOURCE REALIGNMENT at the top of a form and signed where trust expected them to sign.

At 11:20 Falk stopped by her cubicle with his mug.

"You staying buried in coverage tables all day?"

Anya locked the Wilkinson file before turning. "Looks like it."

"Take a lunch break before you start seeing patterns in the carpet."

"Maybe the carpet's suspicious."

One corner of his mouth moved.

*Ask if she's going home this weekend. No, don't crowd her. She already looks strung thin. Wilkinson still can't format a note to save his life. Move the six o'clock handoff. No, not handoff. Coverage shift. Christ, get the wording right.*

The stumble was so small she almost missed it. Not a revelation. Not some obvious crack. Just one word appearing where it shouldn't and getting shoved aside fast enough that somebody without telepathy would never have known it had been there.

Her pulse kicked once under her ribs.

Falk tipped his mug toward the break room. "There's still some decent coffee if you move fast."

"That sounds fake, Gerald."

"It is, but it's less fake than the tea."

He kept walking.

Anya sat there with the locked screen and the pen in her hand and tried to decide whether that one slip counted as anything or whether this was how another innocent man got burned. *Handoff* could have meant the shift paperwork itself. People said the wrong operational word all the time. She knew that. She also knew he had corrected it inside his own head before the thought finished forming.

Not proof.

But no longer nothing.

She spent the next hour building the smallest case she could trust. Not betrayal. Not motive. Just motion. Falk's initials on the coverage changes. Falk on the circulation chain for the Dreher packets. Falk catching and using the gap her false report had opened. Falk's brief internal slip over the six o'clock change. By 1:00 the list fit on half a page and still looked thin enough to hate.

She did not take it to Kurtz.

Not yet.

If she handed internal security another elegant wrong answer, she would deserve whatever came after it. Damian was right about the time pressure, but Damian didn't work inside a building full of people whose private disasters could mimic treason down to the minute. This one she needed to watch happen.

At 16:40 she checked the next day's floor assignment board. The procurement desk coverage shift was still there. 0600. Falk's initials in the approval field. No one had challenged it.

Tomorrow's hunt stopped being abstract. If somebody inside WISE wanted that lane dark before the weekend, 0600 was the hour to watch.

The encrypted call came at nine that night, one-time cipher and frequency rotation. Anya sat on her kitchen floor with her back against the cabinet and had to try the key sequence twice before the line cleared through static.

Damian's voice came on stripped of its diplomatic polish.

"The weather turned."

"How bad?"

"Our friend at the garden center moved up the planting schedule. Public event. Meant to look like the people across the street started it."

It was a diplomatic incident, something visible and meant to read as Westalian provocation.

"When?"

"This weekend. Midweek at the latest. Days, not weeks."

The kitchen faucet dripped once into the sink. Then it dripped again.

"I lost two days on the personnel issue," she said.

"What happened?"

"I was sure about one of them. I was wrong. He was hiding that he's sick. Bad sick."

Silence held on the line. Then, quieter, "I'm sorry."

The words hit harder than they should have.

"I flagged him because the paperwork fit," she said before she could stop herself. "Access history, printer pulls, missing afternoons, all lined up with the leaks. It was all real. It just meant oncology instead of treason."

Damian took a breath. Paper shifted on his end, then stopped. "And the real one?"

"Still inside the same circulation chain. I think it's Falk."

"Think, or know?"

"Think. More than I did yesterday. Less than I want."

"What changed?"

She looked at the note she'd already started on the back of the electricity bill, though there was almost nothing on it yet.

"He signed a forty-eight-hour coverage cut on one of the diplomatic procurement desks. Starts at six tomorrow. Then I went backward and found three smaller reroutes in the same lanes, all after my Damian report went into the system. He keeps making the hole look administrative."

The static hissed softly between them.

"Which desk?"

She gave him the channel designation.

Another pause followed. "All right," he said. "Don't watch speeches, then. Watch deliveries. If the event is this weekend, they'll move parts first. Your person is clearing air for that or somebody is using his paperwork to do it."

"That's more useful."

"I know."

Her shoulder eased a fraction against the cabinet. It wasn't enough to matter.

"There's something else," she said. "He almost thought the wrong word."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning I don't know if I caught a tell or if I'm doing the same thing again and building a whole case out of one second of somebody's bad brain."

"Then don't build the case out of that second."

She shut her eyes.

"Right."

"Use what the paper can survive," Damian said. "Use what you can watch happen in real time. If you're right about him, he's going to need that desk dark for a reason. If you're wrong again, you burn another innocent person. So don't guess. Sit on the lane and see who uses it."

That was the first clean instruction anybody had given her all week.

"Okay."

"Can you get eyes on it alone?"

"I can get there before the shift change. After that I'll decide if I need help."

"From someone you trust?"

She let out one dry breath.

"That list is not having a good week."

He was quiet just long enough for her to picture the look he would be making if they were in the same room. Not diplomatic. Not amused. The other one.

"Anya."

"Yeah?"

"Be careful."

"You too."

"If the schedule moves again, call me."

"I will."

The line clicked dead.

For a moment she stayed on the floor listening to the pipes and the neighbor upstairs moving around in shoes that should have been illegal after nine. Then she pulled the electricity bill fully onto her lap, turned it over, and wrote:

0600  
diplomatic procurement desk  
Falk

Underneath that she added, after a second:

watch, don't report

Then, lower:

Yor one more night.

It was ugly and it was still the calculation. Her mother was somewhere in the city with Dominic's photographs and whatever was left of her faith in Garden. Anya was betting Yor would stay quiet until tomorrow because the other clock had reached six in the morning and a desk inside WISE was about to go blind.

The apartment went dark when she hit the switch. Her head still hurt, but now it was the ordinary version, the screen-stare, skipped-meal kind. She set the note beside the phone, got into bed, and stayed awake long enough to count the hours until six between the faucet drips.


# Chapter 12

The Blackbell estate glowed against the October dark. White lanterns lined the drive in a row so straight Anya could picture Becky stopping the car to fix one if it leaned. The topiary garden was lit from every flattering angle. Becky had confirmed photographers from three publications, which meant the effortless look on display had been built out of seating charts, florist threats, and at least one hang-up.

Fourteen hours earlier Anya had been at a WISE listening desk waiting for the 0600 watch on Falk's diplomatic procurement lane to prove she wasn't chasing smoke. It had proved the opposite kind of thing: the coverage cut was real, the desk went blind on schedule, and whatever was supposed to move through it never showed its face. No courier. No pickup. No useful mistake. By noon the route had been salvaged north. A WISE courier was carrying copied procurement papers toward Kreuzfeld on forged customs documents and was supposed to clear the checkpoint and check in from the west side by 2300. Yor had taken the extra night Anya had bought and stayed silent through the day. The watch was over. The damage wasn't.

Anya stepped out of the car and the thoughts hit before the cold air did.

*If the valet scratches my paint, I'm going to lose it. I just had it detailed. I'm literally watching him.*

*She's wearing the Moreau. She's wearing the Moreau. I was told I was the only one with the Moreau.*

*Seven more handshakes. I can do seven. Maybe eight if I count the wife.*

Two hundred minds. She let them blur to a murmur and sorted for signals: security detail, two private guards, one off-duty SSS officer she recognized from the diplomatic reception, exit routes, and the running estimate of how long she could stay before Becky noticed if she slipped out early.

The answer, from a thought she'd caught three weeks ago during a phone call: *If she cancels on me one more time, I'm killing her. I'm not joking. I will actually kill her.* Becky had probably meant socially. The sentiment had been clear.

Damian came around the car. Dark suit, precise posture. The thin scar through his eyebrow was the only thing that didn't match the packaging. He hadn't said much on the drive over. The only useful thing he'd offered was a complaint about road checks north of the city, and the thought under it had translated cleanly: the SSS had pushed one Kreuzfeld inspection roster earlier than expected. If their courier was already on the road, earlier mattered. The mole was still unidentified. The Renewal Front's clock had shrunk to days. Attending a charity gala while all of that pressed at their backs felt obscene, but covers needed maintenance. Anya's social calendar had to look like a real person's social calendar.

"Ready?" she said.

"No."

"Still nothing from six?" he asked quietly.

"Nothing that helps."

His mouth tightened. "That's a terrible answer to get before a party."

*The things I do for East-West cultural exchange. Kirchner is going to corner me and talk about tariffs for forty minutes, and I'm going to smile until my face detaches.*

"It'll be fine," Anya said. "Becky throws a good party."

"You keep saying that."

"The food'll be amazing."

"That's what you said about the reception."

"And was I wrong?"

He paused. "You weren't entirely wrong."

*She wore that color before. The state dinner. Stop it. Trade policy. Think about trade policy.*

Anya walked toward the entrance and tried not to listen to what he was trying not to think.

Becky got to them in the foyer before the door had fully shut.

"You came!" She grabbed Anya by the shoulders, held her at arm's length, and frowned. *Mascara under one eye. Left earring fastened crooked. And she's doing the hair thing again. Something happened.* Becky swiped a thumb gently beneath Anya's eye, then straightened the earring with two quick fingers. "You actually came. I was going to be so mad if you bailed."

"I said I'd come," Anya said.

"You did your face in a moving car," Becky said under her breath. "That's never a good sign."

"Traffic."

"Same category."

"You said that about the gallery opening in August and then you texted me from a parking lot at eleven saying work blew up." Becky released her and turned to Damian with sudden, predatory interest. "And you brought a guest."

"Damian Desmond." She extended her hand. "Eden Academy. You cried at the eighth-grade science fair because your volcano didn't erupt."

*Oh no. She remembers the volcano. Of course she remembers the volcano.*

"There was a structural issue with the display," Damian started.

"It didn't erupt."

"The sodium bicarbonate ratio was off."

"Damian. It didn't erupt. I was there. You cried."

His public smile held, barely. "It was a frustrating afternoon."

*Kill me. Just kill me now.*

Anya bit the inside of her cheek.

Becky smiled, the real one, not the camera version. Her dark curls had been pinned into something that had probably required a second pair of hands and a prayer, and she was wearing a dress that probably cost more than Anya's monthly cover salary, but underneath all that she was still the girl who'd traded her lunch for Anya's dessert every day for a year at Eden and thought she was getting the better deal.

"I'm glad you're here," Becky said. The thought under it matched the words: *I'm glad. I'm glad she came.* "Both of you. Go eat, talk to people, enjoy yourselves. I have to deal with a crisis. They brought Riesling instead of Gruner Veltliner and I'm going to start a war."

She was already moving, heels sharp on the marble, thoughts skipping ahead of her.

*Check the wine table. Find Lila. She's here. Okay. She's actually here. Maybe tonight. Maybe tonight will feel real.*

Anya watched her go and felt the first pull of the evening settle behind her ribs, right where the bruise from the ambush had mostly healed and not quite vanished.

The ballroom had gilt frames, a chandelier heavy enough to go on a threat map, and acoustics that carried every low conversation farther than anyone in the room would admit. Tables with white cloths ran along one wall. The buffet ran along the other. The bar already had the thickest crowd.

Anya worked the room the way she'd been trained: peripheral scanning, threat assessment, social mapping. Tonight was supposed to be maintenance, not operations. She needed to be seen doing ordinary things: making small talk, circling the buffet, existing in public beside a friend who threw galas. Normal. Unremarkable. A cultural attache with a social life.

The problem was that Eden Academy alumni were everywhere.

Becky had been mining the old school network for years. Lawyers, diplomats, media producers, one person Anya was fairly sure had become a professional horse trainer. Every few feet another face clicked into place with a surface thought attached to it. *She still looks like that?* *Don't bring up the debate trophy.* *If Kessler says merger one more time, I'm leaving.* Childhood hadn't loosened its grip on anybody.

A woman in green intercepted Anya near the buffet. "Anya. From Miss Henderson's class. Do you remember me?"

*Please say yes. Please say yes. I rehearsed this in the car.*

"Of course. You sat next to the window. Third row."

"Yes. Oh my God." The relief in her head was immediate. *She remembers.* "I'm Petra. Petra Vogt. I'm in publishing now."

"That's great."

"What about you? Someone said you're with the diplomatic service?"

"Cultural attache. Mostly paperwork and events."

"That sounds amazing."

Petra's thoughts corrected the sentence for her. *Sounds boring, honestly, but she looks incredible. Is that silk?* Anya smiled, said they should catch up properly sometime, and escaped before either of them had to pretend they meant it.

Before she could make it to the terrace doors, Becky reappeared with the expression she wore when an event had slipped half an inch off center and everybody around her had become useless.

"No disappearing yet," Becky said. "I need one respectable photograph with the youth foundation board, because apparently the room only believes in charity if enough attractive alumni stand near the logo."

"You have photographers from three publications," Anya said.

"And not one of them can manufacture legitimacy out of thin air. Move."

She herded Anya and Damian toward a step-and-repeat near the quartet where a photographer and three board members were arranging themselves into something that would read as spontaneous generosity in print. Otto Kirchner, silver-haired and pleased with himself, shook Damian's hand and launched directly into complaint.

"If anyone in Foreign Affairs asks," Kirchner said, "my logistics people lost half a day to customs because Kreuzfeld decided every truck needed to be turned inside out. Children's medicines do not improve in storage."

Damian's expression didn't change. "I'm sure the relevant offices have their reasons."

*Or somebody upstream decided the road mattered tonight.*

The photographer lifted his camera. Becky adjusted Anya by the elbow, adjusted Damian by the sleeve, then stood back and frowned the way she did when people were making her work harder than necessary.

"Closer," she said. "You look like you were assigned alphabetically."

They stepped half an inch toward each other. Flash. Another flash. Kirchner kept talking about manifests, delays, a refrigerated shipment stalled north of the city. Half of it was donor theater. Half wasn't. Kreuzfeld kept coming up, in Kirchner's complaint, in Damian's earlier thought, in the officer by the bar. The word had started following her around the room. WISE's courier was supposed to cross there before the tighter search rotation fully bit, papers hidden inside a customs sleeve that would look ordinary under a lazy inspection. If the inspection was no longer lazy, eleven o'clock would not be a routine signal. It would be the moment she found out whether the whole salvage plan had just died in line.

Becky examined the preview monitor, satisfied enough to stop tormenting them. "Great. You both look expensive and vaguely public-minded. Go circulate."

Near the bar, the off-duty SSS officer from the drive accepted a refill and laughed at a joke he wasn't listening to. Under the laugh his mind ran on a separate track. *Kreuzfeld moved to before dawn. Good. Let them hit wire instead of fog for once.*

The name cut through the room. Kreuzfeld was where WISE had shoved the procurement packet after the dawn desk watch came up empty. A courier should have cleared on forged customs papers, crossed to the west side, and signaled by eleven. Moving a roster on a Thursday night meant somebody expected movement, not paperwork, and "before dawn" meant whoever had planned that route might already be walking into brighter light than promised.

She found the buffet. Becky's money had bought the panic menu: gorgeous, expensive, too much of it. Anya loaded a small plate and drifted toward a table near the terrace doors. Damian had been absorbed into a group near the bar, dark suits and policy talk and a fog of young-diplomat thoughts trying to sound older than their owners. *Regional framework. Don't say tariff twice. If he asks about my posting, smile.* His thoughts surfaced through the crowd noise at intervals. *Bilateral trade framework, that's safe. Say that. Kirchner still parts his hair the same way. And if Kreuzfeld is tightening tonight, the courier with the forged papers is walking into a search line.*

That last line caught like a hook. She didn't look at him. She took a bite of goat cheese on toast and chewed slowly while the operational clock, which had been background pressure all evening, moved up a notch.
