10:17. Forty-three minutes until the check-in that was supposed to tell her the salvage route had worked. Forty-three minutes of donor smiles, alumni chatter, and pretending she wasn't listening for the first sign the night had gone bad.

A waiter offered champagne. She took a glass and didn't drink from it. The glass was a prop, an excuse for her hands. Across the room, a retired diplomat was telling the same customs story he told at every event. Two tables over, an elderly woman was planning the argument she intended to have with her husband when they got home. *If he says one word about my mother, I swear to God...* Anya tuned away before she got the rest.

They were careful with each other in public. They had been careful since the safe house, since the bruised ribs and split eyebrow and the quiet after, since the line they'd crossed and then, by mutual unstated agreement, backed away from without deciding which side they meant to live on. Here, in a room full of people who knew one or both of them, careful was the only option. It also weighed a ton.

Once, near the buffet, their paths crossed. Damian reached for a napkin. Anya reached for a second goat cheese toast.

"The cheese is good," she said.

"I noticed you're eating all of it."

"It's strategically important cheese."

He almost smiled. *Don't smile. You're smiling.* "I'll leave you to your strategy."

He moved away. The almost-smile stayed in her chest longer than it should have, and she hated that, because her brain should have been on the mole, on Kreuzfeld, on the clock, on anything except the exact way his mouth moved when he was trying not to be amused.

Then somebody found Becky's nostalgia trap.

She'd set a side table near the bar with stacked Eden yearbooks, a leather binder of archival photo reprints, and a rented carousel projector clicking school pictures onto the cream wall between the terrace doors and the quartet. Lila, one of Becky's media contacts and an Eden alumna, had apparently decided this counted as public service.

The wall filled with a yearbook class photo first: thirty children in Eden blazers, squinting at the photographer with expressions ranging from dutiful to mutinous.

"Oh my God," Lila said. "Look at us."

Nostalgia spread fast and got less accurate by the minute. People abandoned drinks to crowd around the table and wall. Somebody flipped through yearbooks. Somebody else dug through the binder. The projector clicked from sports day to the end-of-year ceremony to a class picnic where half the boys looked seasick. Adults laughed at the evidence that they had once been tiny and ridiculous.

Anya watched from the edge of the cluster and felt her stomach tighten.

Erich Kessler materialized at her table with his third beer and the comfortable posture of a man who had found his equilibrium several drinks ago. Erich ran logistics for his family's distribution company. His surface thoughts gave her the rest: resentment over his older brother's recent promotion, and a homesick gratitude for the one place where people had talked to him before they learned his last name mattered.

*Good to be here. Good party. I miss this. I miss when we were all in the same building and nobody'd figured out who was going to matter and who wasn't.*

"Anya." He dropped into the chair across from her, grinning. "From Eden, right? Becky's friend?"

"That's me."

"You look exactly the same." He pointed at his own head. "The hair. I'd recognize it anywhere. Nobody else had pink hair. You were like the one bright flag in the whole school."

*Pink hair. Class photo. Second row. She sat behind me in Mr. Kraft's class.*

"I've been told it's distinctive."

"Distinctive? It's a landmark. I used to navigate the hallways by your head."

She laughed. The laugh was real enough. Erich was harmless enough. For half a minute it was just a party.

Then he leaned over the nostalgia table, pawed through the clear sleeves in the binder, and let out a delighted noise. "No. Becky is evil for this. Lila, put this one up."

The projector clacked. The wall filled with an Eden charity bazaar planning committee from the year Becky had decided twelve-year-olds should save a pediatric ward through sheer force of personality. A folding table vanished under ribbon spools, raffle books, paper flowers, and a dented cash box. Becky stood in the middle pointing at somebody outside the frame like a tiny dictator. Damian was at one end with a clipboard. Anya was at the other with scissors in one hand and a face that clearly objected to the concept of volunteer labor.

She remembered glue drying on her fingers. Becky assigning jobs in a voice even the teachers had obeyed. Damian getting handed the tally sheet because Becky said his handwriting looked trustworthy enough for money.

"Oh, this is incredible," Erich said. He slapped a yearbook open beside the projector and pointed his beer at the wall. "Desmond!"

He called it loudly enough to turn heads. Damian looked over from his conversation.

"Erich." Smile. Measured. "Good to see you."

"Come look at this. Charity committee. She had us doing unpaid labor before puberty."

Damian crossed the room. His thoughts shifted as he moved. *Old photos, of course. Smile at the wall. Say we all look young.*

Erich kept talking, beer-warm and easy, one hand on the yearbook, one on the beer. He looked at the projection. He looked at Anya. He looked down at the caption block under his thumb.

"Wait." His whole face lit with the loose clarity alcohol stripped him down to. "This is the funniest possible thing. Becky has seriously been throwing the same event her whole life." He jabbed the page at Damian. "Look. That's you, that's me, that's Becky, and that's Anya Forger pretending not to murder us over raffle tickets. Same face and everything."

The name landed in the room like a glass hitting tile.

Anya Forger.

A man on his third beer had said it out loud at a party while grieving his own vanished importance and having no idea what he'd just done.

And then something in Damian's mind locked hard enough she felt it in her teeth.

*Forger.*

The yearbook page under Erich's hand. The girl in the photo box in his apartment. The woman six feet away in silk with a champagne glass she hadn't touched.

*Anya Forger.*

Not resemblance. Not the same laugh, the same tilt of the head, the same almost-memory. Her.

His expression didn't change. Twenty years of political upbringing and Desmond discipline held the line. His face produced a half-smile. His hand stayed steady around his glass.

"That was a long time ago," he said, voice perfectly even.

*Don't do this here. Not in Becky's house. Not with cameras.*

Erich was still going. "And Becky still has the same staffing model, apparently. Child labor and threats."

"I think you're reading a yearbook with beer in your eyes," Anya said.

She heard her own voice from a distance, bright and easy and built for exactly this kind of deflection. "This is why archivists shouldn't serve alcohol."

"Maybe," Erich said, squinting down at the caption. *Tiny print. Why is the print this small?* He took a drink. "Or maybe Becky is committing typographic crimes. Same difference."

He moved on. The cluster shifted back toward the wall, the binder, the yearbooks, the harmless pleasure of finding out the tallest boy in class had become the shortest man in the room.

Damian did not move on.

He stood where he'd been, glass untouched.

*Not here. Think first.*

His eyes found hers across the room. Half a second. Less.

Then somebody beside him asked a question about wine. He answered without missing a beat.

*Later.*

Anya set her champagne glass down before she dropped it. The quartet slid into a waltz. Erich turned another page in the binder. Around them the party kept moving.

She pressed her palms flat to the tablecloth and breathed.

Her pager vibrated inside her evening bag.

Two short pulses. One long.

Border channel.

She turned just enough to shield the tiny screen with her hand.

`KZF 2300 MISSED. RETURN SECURE ASAP.`

Kreuzfeld.

The cold that went through her had nothing to do with October. The courier should have been across by now, forged papers accepted, procurement copies intact, one secure burst from the west side to say the salvage route had held. MISSED meant no burst. No confirmation. Maybe a delayed checkpoint. Maybe a dead radio. Maybe men with search lamps pulling his coat apart on the roadside while the papers came loose in their hands. And Damian, somewhere behind her in the ballroom, had already clocked that Kreuzfeld was tightening before dawn. She couldn't stay here smiling through alumni small talk and handle a missing courier and a man who had just realized who she was. The room had split in two and both halves wanted her at once.

The crowd noise pressed in. She needed to not be in this room.

She stood, smoothed her mouth into something neutral, and did the stupid math. The Blackbells would have a private study somewhere off the west hall with a landline she might be able to borrow, but asking for it meant explaining why. The powder room gave privacy and nothing else. The terrace gave cold air, a door, and a few seconds before anybody followed. Seconds were not a plan. Seconds were what she could buy without detonating the evening.

Crossing the ballroom took forever. Kirchner had Damian by the cuff now, still talking about customs exemptions with the damp intensity of a man who mistook access for friendship. Becky was laughing at a joke she obviously hated because a camera was pointed at her. Erich had moved on to telling Lila he deserved compensation for surviving adolescence under Becky Blackbell's management style. Everybody else was fully occupied with the ordinary business of a rich party. Damian looked up once as Anya slipped toward the terrace doors. His face stayed perfectly diplomatic. The thought under it did not.

*Not here.*

She kept walking because if she stopped and looked back, that would count as an answer.

* * *

The terrace was colder than she'd expected. The party burned warm behind the glass doors, but out here the October air had finally given up pretending to be kind. Wide flagstones, wrought-iron railing, a view of the grounds that disappeared into black after about twenty feet. In daylight the garden probably looked expensive. Tonight it looked like a place you could vanish into.

She gripped the railing. The cold metal helped. It was an object, a boundary, a fact.

Damian was still inside, at the edge of her range. The careful rhythm of his thoughts came through thinned by glass and distance. They had narrowed into two problems he was refusing to let touch. *Not here. Not tonight. If Kreuzfeld caught the courier, the papers are gone.*

She couldn't read what he'd decide about her. She could read that he was holding the choice shut with both hands, and that was worse. The border alert sat under it like a second blade. The courier had been meant to carry copied procurement documents over the line before the blind desk could be scrubbed and the route rewritten. If he missed check-in, the papers were exposed or lost, and the only place Anya could do anything useful was on a secure line, now. There was no version of the next hour where she could manage that and stay in front of Damian's face at the same time.

The guilt sat in her like spoiled fruit.

"You look like you're about to jump."

Becky appeared in the doorway, shoes in one hand, wine in the other. At some point during the evening she'd taken her hair down. The engineered updo had surrendered to gravity and humidity, and the loose curls made her look less like a magazine spread and more like the girl Anya had known since kindergarten.

"I'm fine."

"Mm-hmm." Becky came out barefoot and leaned on the railing beside her. "You're doing the thing."

"I'm not doing a thing."

"You're doing the thing where you go quiet and stare at nothing and say you're fine in a voice that means the opposite. You've been doing it since we were six. I know the thing, Anya."

*She's not fine. Did someone say anything in there? Did Damian say anything? If he did, I'll throw him into a hedge.*

Anya said nothing.

They stood for a while. The party murmured on the other side of the glass. Below them, somewhere in the garden, an irrigation system clicked on and started spraying arcs of water across the dark lawn.

"Good event," Anya said at last.

"It's fine." Becky sipped her wine. "The Riesling situation has been resolved. The shellfish issue has been contained. The Brahms problem is ongoing."

"The Brahms problem?"

"The quartet keeps playing that one piece, the slow sad one that makes everybody stare at their plates like they regret their lives. I asked for uptempo. I specifically said uptempo."

"Did you give them a playlist?"

"I gave them a mood board."

"A mood board. For a string quartet."

"It had clear visual cues."

Anya almost smiled. The ease of it, the old groove of talking to the person who had known her longest, made the next turn worse.

Becky's thoughts shifted. The surface traffic about caterers and wine and quartets dropped away. Under it was the heavier thing she'd been carrying all evening, tucked under performance and timing and hostess energy, waiting for a moment when she could stop being watched.

*Say it. She's the only one I can say it to. If I can't say it to Anya, then who.*

"Can I tell you one real thing?" Becky asked.

"Yeah."

Becky looked down into her wine. The pause lasted long enough for the irrigation system to complete one cycle and begin again.

"I broke up with Marc."

"I'm sorry."

"Don't." She turned the glass slowly between her palms. "He was — Marc was nice. He was genuinely nice. He came to everything, stood where I put him, smiled for photos." She stopped. "Five months."

*He didn't want me. He wanted the version that gets you into rooms.*

"He asked me about my work once," Becky said. "Early on. I started telling him about this documentary thing I want to do — artists in the eastern districts, people nobody covers — and I was actually excited, I was talking too fast, and he." She stopped. "He said 'That's great, babe.' And then he asked what we were doing Saturday."

She drank. She looked at the dark garden.

"Then on my birthday he sent orchids. Nice ones. The card said he was proud of everything I'm building." She almost laughed. "Building. Like I'm a — " She didn't finish. "We'd been sleeping together for three months and he still wrote about me like he'd read a profile."

The sprinkler clicked through another rotation below. Inside, somebody laughed too loudly. A waiter crossed behind the glass with a tray of tiny pastries.

"And he wasn't even — he wasn't trying to be an asshole. That's what's so — " Becky's voice had lost the performance register. It was quieter now, flatter, the voice she used when nobody was filming and nobody needed to be charmed. She shook her head.

*Everyone. Not just him. Everyone does that.*

"You know what I do on Tuesdays?" Becky said. "When there's nothing on the calendar. I eat cereal for dinner. I watch cooking shows. I talk to my cat." She wasn't looking at Anya. "And I've never told anyone that, because it would — " She waved a hand at the glass doors, the party, the chandelier. "I don't know. Ruin it."

She looked down at her wine. "I'm just—" She trailed off.

She turned toward Anya. Her face was open in a way Anya had seen maybe a dozen times in fourteen years, stripped of charm, performance, and the social armor Becky wore so well most people forgot it was armor at all.

"You're my best friend," Becky said. "You know that?" She stopped. When she started again her voice was different. "You listen when I'm not trying to be fun. You always have."

*She's the only one who sees me. The Tuesday-night me. I don't know what I'd do without her.*

The words hit low and mean. Six-year-old Becky with a red lunchbox: *I like her hair. I want to sit with her every day.* Fifteen-year-old Becky on the phone past midnight: *Don't let me text him back. Please don't let me text him back.* Tuesday-night Becky beside her now, barefoot on cold stone with wine on her breath.

The truth pushed at Anya's teeth. *I've been hearing the rest since kindergarten.*

Her fingers slipped on the railing. Tell Becky now and every year of the friendship would split open at once.

"You're mine too," Anya said. "My best friend. You know that."

The last word caught on the way out.

Relief moved through Becky so plainly Anya could have touched it. Becky leaned her head against Anya's shoulder. Her thoughts quieted, not empty, just settled.

*Thank God for her.*

Anya stared out at the dark garden. The October air stung her eyes. Inside, the quartet had finally found an upbeat piece. Damian still hovered at the far edge of her range, careful and distant and impossible to unread.

Her pager vibrated again inside the bag at her wrist.

Border channel.

Becky didn't move.

In another minute Anya would have to go back inside, make an excuse, and disappear into secure traffic that would run the rest of the night. For now she kept her shoulder where it was and looked out into the dark until the garden lights blurred.


# Chapter 13

Anya was already awake when the phone rang at 6:14 on the first morning of November. She had slept an hour at best. It was probably less. Most of the night had gone to encrypted border traffic on her WISE terminal and the neighbor in 4B thinking, *please please please just sleep, I'm begging you, just one hour.* The baby on the other side of the wall kept crying. The mother's thoughts were louder.

She picked up on the second ring. Sylvia got straight to it.

"At approximately 0340, border control at Kreuzfeld was breached. Crane was moving on forged papers when a three-man team intercepted him. They had his route, his timing, and the cover packet. He died on site."

Anya sat on the edge of the bed with the phone hard against her ear. The baby cried again. The neighbor thought, *please, please, this one time,* and then nothing but blank tired static.

"His body has been recovered," Sylvia said. "The documents have not. I have informed his wife."

Crane. David Crane. Parking space, three months ago. "Chief," every single time. Forty-one. Wife, no kids. He kept border stamps in an Altoids tin in his glove compartment because supply never had the ones he wanted when he wanted them. He had spent the last six weeks running eastern courier lanes Anya had helped make possible.

Three weeks ago she had filed Damian's network as political vanity with a useful pedigree attached. She had written that the chatter hanging off his eastern contacts was posture, not live logistics. She had cut the note about customs clerks and freight schedules. She had downgraded Renewal Front movement on the eastern corridors from active concern to something smaller, duller, safer. WISE moved the Desmond-adjacent traffic off hot watch and into periodic review. Duplicate eyes came off Kreuzfeld. Courier lanes were rebuilt around the thinner coverage picture. Crane drew one of the replacement runs.

That did not mean her report put rifles in anybody's hands. It meant the hole existed. Somebody with routing access had used it.

"There is another development," Sylvia said. "At approximately 0300, an SSS tactical unit executed a search warrant on a residential address in eastern Berlint. The location matches an address from our analytical review last week. The warrant was specific. They knew what they were looking for."

Damian's cache. The apartment. The shelves arranged by region. The false wall behind the kitchen cabinet. The coffee stain near the baseboard that he had left because replacing the carpet meant letting the landlord inside. She had laughed when he said it. He had not.

"The material was seized intact," Sylvia said. "Analysis is pending. The eastern sector is compromised."

Compromised. Broken. Burned open. Gone.

"I have questions about your reporting timeline," Sylvia said. "We will address them at 0800. Attend in person."

"Yes."

Sylvia went quiet for half a second. Not uncertainty. Just the small stop between the work and the next piece of work.

"Crane was a good officer," she said. "We will debrief in the morning."

The line went dead.

Anya stayed where she was. The neighbor in 4B adjusted the baby and thought, *okay, okay, maybe like this,* and the baby screamed harder. The thought blew apart into exhausted misery.

She called Damian.

He picked up at once. "I'm aware," he said. His voice was controlled, too even, and she could hear how hard he was working at it. "My contact in the SSS reached me twenty minutes ago. They took everything."

"How specific?"

"They went to the false wall first. Then the bedroom floorboards. No wasted motion."

Anya shut her eyes.

"Crane is dead," she said.

A pause. "Who?"

"WISE field officer. He was carrying a courier packet through Kreuzfeld."

"I'm sorry."

He meant it. That was the problem. He did not know what he was apologizing for. He did not know she had handed WISE a cleaner version of him than the truth. He did not know Crane's run had existed inside the coverage picture that lie helped build. He did not know the dead space between what she filed and what she knew had finally filled up with a body.

"There's something else," Damian said. "Mertens called an hour ago."

The name hit and brought the rest with it. A ministry official. Careful. Nervous. Two daughters. Saturday football. Photocopies in a gym bag, left in the back seat of his car in the ministry garage because that was the blind spot nobody bothered to search.

"He's out," Damian said. "Told me not to call again. He thinks the SSS got the address through his department's records. He thinks they will come for him next."

"Will they?"

"I don't know. If the files were taken intact, they'll find his name soon enough. I tried to tell him I could arrange something. He hung up before I got there."

Anya stood and went to the kitchen window without deciding to. Morning had turned the glass the color of dishwater. She thought of Crane's parking space. Mertens in a ministry garage with a gym bag. The report she had put in Sylvia's hands. She had drawn a line around Damian and called it strategy. Everything outside that line had paid for it.

*Please just sleep,* the neighbor thought. *Please.*

"Do you think the raid and Kreuzfeld were the same operation?" she asked.

"I think they were two precise hits inside forty minutes," Damian said. "I think both were tied to the same work. The rest is guesswork."

"A good guess."

"Yes." He exhaled. "Still guesswork."

That helped and did not help.

"We need to meet," Anya said. "Not the usual place."

"I know somewhere."

She hung up, stripped off her T-shirt, and stepped into the shower. The water never got fully hot. She let it hit the back of her neck anyway. On the tram west, a man in a wool scarf thought about invoice numbers, a woman in nurse's shoes thought about whether she had time to shave her legs before tonight, and somebody near the back kept replaying *did you lock the cabinet, did you lock the cabinet, did you lock the cabinet* until Anya wanted to turn around and shake him. The city had made room for dawn and deliveries and normal resentment without asking anybody's permission. Crane stayed dead inside it just fine.

The printing shop block was still half asleep. Someone inside was already running a press. The sound came through the walls in steady mechanical shudders.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

The basement was underneath the shop, reached through a service door beside a dumpster that smelled like wet ink and cardboard. Damian had a key. He did not explain how. The stairs were steep concrete. By the halfway mark, the railing had leached enough heat from her palm that she shoved both hands into her coat pockets.

The room below was about fifteen feet by twenty, all concrete except for a steel folding table, four mismatched chairs, and one fluorescent tube on the ceiling. Damp had browned the far wall in vertical streaks. Damian was already there with his elbows on the table and his hands folded loose, coat still on, as if he had been holding the same position for an hour. The burn mark near his left cuff stood out darker than the rest of the wool.

She pulled out the chair and sat. Neither of them spoke.

Above them, someone in the shop dragged something heavy across the floor and let it drop. The thud came down through the ceiling, dull and blunt. Damian kept looking at the table.

*Too static. Stupid. Should've moved it. If they took the little ledger, Mertens is finished.*

There was a pale coffee ring on the table, half rubbed away. Anya put two fingers beside it and kept them there.

"They went to the false wall first," Damian said at last. "Straight there. No search pattern. No hesitation."

"So the warrant wasn't just specific."

"No."

"Mertens still thinks the address came through ministry records?"

"He thinks someone pulled an internal property file connected to his department. He thinks the SSS is going to —" Damian's voice went flat. "He thinks they're coming for him. He's probably right. I don't have anything useful to say to that."

Anya nodded.

"Crane was carrying forged driver papers through Kreuzfeld," she said. "The team that hit him knew the exact lane, the exact time, and the cover packet. That did not come off the street."

Damian's gaze dropped to her hand by the coffee ring.

"Then somebody inside your routing handed them the window."

"Yes."

Another thud upstairs. Someone laughed immediately after, bright and stupid and normal.

Damian rubbed his thumb against the side of his index finger. "They had my apartment. They had your courier window. Both before sunrise, and I keep —" He stopped. "I keep trying to make that two separate things and I can't."

"The coordinated part is still a guess," Anya said.

"It doesn't feel like a guess."

"Still a guess."

He looked up at that. The fluorescent light made the scar over his eyebrow look whiter than the rest of him.

"Yeah," he said. "Still a guess."

There it was. Not agreement. Discipline. The difference mattered and she hated that it mattered when Crane was dead.

"Mertens is going to be okay," she said.

He looked at her.

She heard how bad it sounded too late. "No. I don't know that. I just..." She swallowed. "I know he mattered to you."

A drip hit the floor somewhere behind her. Slow. Then another.

"He has two daughters," Damian said. "That's the part I can't stop thinking about."

Anya nodded because anything else would have been a lie.

"Tell me about Crane."

The question landed hard enough that she almost laughed. Not because it was funny. Because a man was dead and Damian still wanted him to be a person, not a line item. That almost broke something.

"He called everybody chief," she said. "Even Sylvia, once. He used too much powdered creamer in bad coffee and then pretended he liked it. He whistled when he was waiting at checkpoints, but only the same three bars because he couldn't remember how the song went after that. He could spot a bad stamp on forged papers faster than anyone I know." She swallowed. "He was forty-one."

Damian said nothing.

"He kept spare seals in his car because he never trusted supply. He had one story about a checkpoint dog that he told every time somebody new got assigned east. He always got the middle wrong and the ending right."

The room held it. Upstairs, someone cursed about ink coverage. Somewhere in the pipes a radiator knocked once and gave up.

"He was on a replacement run," Anya said.

Damian's face did not change, but the silence tightened.

"Replacement for what?"

She rubbed at the heel of her palm with her thumb, hard enough to feel the old scar pull. "For coverage we cut."

He waited.

She could have stopped there. She did not.

"I filed the east-sector picture wrong," she said. "I made it smaller than it was. I said it was ego and old men talking war after dessert. I pushed the customs piece and the freight piece down the stack. WISE thinned the eastern watch after that. They opened substitute lanes through crossings they thought were quieter." Her throat felt scraped raw. "Crane took one."

Damian's hands stayed folded. That restraint felt worse than if he had stood up and shouted.

"That's what I can prove," Anya said. "I can prove my report changed the coverage picture. I can prove somebody inside had the new window. The rest is me looking at a dead man and wanting the line to point somewhere clean."

He inhaled slowly through his nose.

"Then don't make it cleaner than it is."

She looked at him.

"Your report helped build the lane," he said. "That doesn't mean you briefed the men waiting on it."

"You don't know that."

"No." His voice went flatter. "Neither do you."

She looked back down at the coffee ring. He was right. He was annoyingly, relentlessly right about her in all the places that had nothing to do with telepathy.

*She's going to take the whole thing into herself if I let her.*

That one landed with force and no new information. She still hated it.

"At the reception," she said, "I caught a fragment about the mole and I let it slide because I couldn't place it. Then I burned two days on the wrong analyst."

"Which analyst?"

She shook her head too fast. "Doesn't matter now."

It mattered. He knew it mattered. Her evasion hung there between them like a smell.

"It matters if you're still guessing at the wrong people," he said.

"I know."

"Do you."

It was not a challenge in his public voice. It was quieter than that, and more exhausted.

Anya opened her mouth, closed it, tried again. "I know I missed something."

"That isn't the same sentence."

Above them, somebody dropped a stack of paper. The slap of it came through the ceiling in a flat burst. Anya almost laughed again, this time because the universe apparently intended to keep humiliating her until she went numb.

"My network is burned," Damian said after a moment. "Your eastern routing is burned. The mole is still —" He rubbed his face with one hand. "Mertens has two kids. I can't even tell him what's coming."

"Yes."

"So if we're still doing this, the shape changes."

She looked up.

"No more fixed caches," he said. "No more apartments holding paper. No more couriers either of us hasn't looked in the eye."

"That's not much of a network."

"It's what's left."

"What about your people?"

He almost smiled and failed. "I have fewer people than I did at dawn."

The drip behind her slowed. Stopped. Started again.

"If I have something for you, I bring it," Damian said. "If you have something for me, you bring it. Nothing sits still long enough to be photographed, copied, or sold."

Anya nodded once. "Okay."

"And if Mertens calls again, I answer once. After that, nothing in writing."

"Okay."

He looked at her for a beat too long, not speaking.

*Don't ask now. Not like this.*

He pulled off his coat and held it out across the table. "Take this."

"I'm fine."

"You're shaking."

"I'm cold."

"Yes," he said. "That's why."

She did not take it. After a second he laid it on the table between them, the sleeve falling over part of the coffee ring.

"I have to be at WISE in half an hour," she said.

"Then go."

It should have sounded like dismissal. It did not. It sounded like the thing you said when there was no useful version of stay.

She stood. So did he.

"What do we actually have left?" she asked.

His gaze flicked to the coat, then to the concrete floor, then back to her face.

"Direct contact," he said. "You and me. That's it."

Anya nodded once and moved past him toward the stairs. When the service door banged shut behind her, his coat was still spread across the table.

She made 0800 with three minutes to spare.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

The WISE east annex smelled like wet wool, radiator dust, and coffee that had died sometime before dawn. A security clerk with a razor nick on his chin checked her badge and thought about mustard. Somewhere behind the inner door, two analysts were arguing out loud about a border map while one of them thought, *if you say contour line one more time I will put this pencil through your hand.* Nobody sounded like a building where a man had died an hour ago. The noise of ordinary irritation almost made her sick.

Sylvia was waiting in Briefing Room B with the blinds half closed. A route map had been pinned to the far board. On the table sat a thin gray folder, a stack of copied manifests, and one black-and-white checkpoint photo turned face down. Sylvia did not stand when Anya came in.

"Sit down," she said.

Anya sat.

Sylvia pushed the folder across the table. "Open page three."

It was her assessment from three weeks ago. Clean typescript. Her name at the top. The lie sat there in flat bureaucratic language, smaller than the damage it had done.

Desmond-adjacent freight and customs chatter assessed as aspirational political signaling, not evidence of live operational control over eastern logistics.

Anya did not need to read the rest. She had built the sentence herself.

"Read the next line," Sylvia said.

Anya obeyed.

Associated Renewal Front reporting to remain under periodic review pending independent corroboration. No immediate rerouting recommended on current evidence.

Sylvia waited until Anya looked up again.

"This assessment entered the system as analysis, not source color," she said. "Once it did, eastern desk reclassified the Desmond-adjacent corridor from red watch to amber review. Duplicate surveillance at Kreuzfeld, Seebruck, and Langenau was reduced. Support coverage was reassigned north. Replacement courier windows were generated from the new threat picture." She touched the manifest stack with two fingers. "Crane drew the 0340 Kreuzfeld window."

There it was. Not guilt. Process. Not feeling. Chain.

Anya stared at the paper until the words blurred and came back.

"What did you have when you wrote this?" Sylvia asked.

"Fragments."

"From where."

"I can't give you a clean source line."

"That is not what I asked."

Anya heard the unspoken line under it anyway. *Do not hide behind tradecraft now.*

"Social access," Anya said. "Conversations. Indirect material. Nothing I could cite cleanly."

"Enough to think the chatter was live?"

"Yes."

Sylvia's face did not change. That made it worse.

"Enough to say so at the time?"

Anya looked back at the report.

"Yes."

Silence filled the room. Outside the glass, a junior officer hurried past holding two mugs and thought, *don't drop it, don't drop it,* with the absolute emotional commitment of someone whose whole life briefly was not dropping coffee.

"Why didn't you," Sylvia said.

The question was small. It landed like a punch.

Anya's first answer came out wrong. "I thought if we tagged him active we would blow the only line we had into that circle."

Sylvia kept waiting.

Anya hated that waiting. It left too much room for truth to move.

"I told myself I was preserving access," she said. "I told myself I was buying time."

"By distorting an assessment."

"Yes."

"For how long did you know it was distorted."

There was no version of this that did not sound like rot. "When I filed it."

Sylvia looked at her for a long second.

*You idiot. You absolute, gifted idiot.*

That one did not come through Sylvia's face. It came through clean and bright and vanished so fast Anya almost thought she imagined it.

"Then hear the chain as it currently stands," Sylvia said. Her voice had gone flatter, not colder. "Your report changed the board. Falk's desk signed the downgrade into active routing guidance. Eastern watch thinned the corridor. Replacement windows were created. Someone with access to one of those windows handed Crane's packet to the team waiting at Kreuzfeld. Those are the facts available this morning. I will not let you turn the rest into certainty because certainty is easier to punish."

Anya swallowed.

"And Desmond's apartment."

"Specific warrant. Specific enough to suggest either prior interior knowledge or a records leak on the Ostanian side." Sylvia tapped the face-down checkpoint photo instead of looking at it. "The timing suggests coordination. The channels do not prove it. Ministry records could explain the address. Our reroute explains Kreuzfeld. One operation could have reached both. Two separate leaks could have landed on the same night. Until we know otherwise, treat both possibilities as live."

That was the answer Damian had given in different words. The same ugly shape, twice from two different directions.

"Do you believe Falk signed those changes because he was exploited," Anya asked, "or because he is the mole?"

Sylvia's gaze sharpened.

"I believe Gerald Falk touches half the eastern paperwork because that is his job." The line was clipped enough to count as a warning. "If you are making an accusation, make one with evidence."

"I'm not."

*Not yet,* thought the part of her that Sylvia did not need telepathy to read.

Sylvia leaned back in her chair. "Good. Then be useful instead."

She turned the checkpoint photo over.

Crane stood beside a border kiosk in bad lighting, coat zipped to the throat, one hand lifted halfway to his ear as if whoever took the picture had caught him mid-annoyance. Somebody had circled the truck lane behind him in blue grease pencil. Another photo beneath it showed the same patch of gravel later, darker in one long spill beside the curb.

Anya looked away first.

"By 1700," Sylvia said, "I want every omitted note, every downgraded contact, every crossing affected by this assessment, and every reroute that followed from it. Names if you have them. Desk handoffs if you can trace them. If you touched it, I want the exact shape of the damage."

"Yes."

"Nothing leaves your desk today without me seeing it."

"Yes."

"And Anya."

She looked up.

Sylvia's expression had not softened. That made the next part legible precisely because it did not.

"Do not waste the day performing grief at me. Work."

The route analysis room had twelve terminals and air that never seemed fully used by the people breathing it. Anya took the station nearest the dead radiator because nobody else wanted it. The screen took too long to wake up. While it loaded, the analyst across from her thought about whether a second breakfast counted as a moral failure if the first one had been half a banana eaten standing up. Somebody at the copier worried his wife had stopped loving him because she no longer corrected his tie. The ordinary traffic of private nonsense moved through her skull with the same rights as anything else. She wanted silence badly enough to bite something.

She opened her own assessment first. Then the routing chain behind it.

One lie turned into forms faster than grief turned into sense.

Her report at 09:14.

Eastern desk note, twenty-three minutes later: Desmond-associated freight chatter reclassified amber pending corroboration.

Coverage review that afternoon: duplicate watch at Kreuzfeld no longer operationally justified under current threat picture.

Support transfer the next morning: shadow-car allocation moved north to Nordhafen.

Courier redistribution approved that evening.

Falk's initials sat on two of the three approvals in neat blue script.

Not proof. Just proximity. Too much proximity.

She opened Crane's packet.

Alias: Erwin Baer.

Cover: sugar-delivery subcontractor.

Crossing: Kreuzfeld, lane three.

Window: 03:36 to 03:44.

Support column: none.

Below that sat a field note from two nights earlier, clipped and irritated in exactly the way Crane had been clipped and irritated in person.

Papers serviceable. Guard on lane three still lazy about axle seals. Whoever typed the manifest needs a new ribbon because this one looks like shit.

He had signed it D.C., because everyone knew it was him.

Anya stared at the initials until they stopped meaning anything.

She pulled up her original rough notes from the Desmond file. The sentence she had removed was still there in an earlier draft: contact cluster suggests possible touchpoints with customs scheduling and rail freight timing across east crossings. She had highlighted it once, then deleted it from the final because saying it aloud inside WISE would have brought too much heat too fast. That had been the argument. That had been the shape of the self-justification. Preserve access. Avoid blowing the line. Stay useful longer.

Crane had died inside longer.

A junior analyst stopped at her desk with a stack of memos and thought, *wrong station, wrong station, wrong station,* before realizing she was not the person he wanted. He mumbled an apology and kept moving. Anya nodded without hearing him.

She kept digging.

The downgraded corridor had not only changed courier assignments. It had changed which anomalies triggered immediate review and which ones got rolled into end-of-day summaries. Two customs names she had shoved into a side note rather than the main threat lane never hit urgent follow-up. A flagged fuel invoice sat unanswered for forty-eight hours because the desk handling it was no longer working under red-watch rules. The whole thing was so boring on paper it almost offended her. Nothing in the chain looked like murder. It looked like staffing. It looked like bureaucracy. It looked like a set of adult decisions made in polite language until somebody bled out on gravel.

By noon her coffee had gone cold untouched. By one she had three pages of reroutes, support cancellations, and downgraded anomaly notices. By two she had a list of names she had minimized, a list of crossings affected, and an ugly little cluster in the middle where Falk's desk touched everything without yet touching enough.

She took the printouts to Sylvia's office.

Sylvia read standing up.

"Falk signed these two," Anya said. "The downgrade and the support transfer."

"Yes," Sylvia said.

"That is too close."

"It is close." Sylvia set the pages down. "It is not yet narrow."

Anya hated that she was right.

"Mertens may explain the apartment," Anya said. "If the ministry side leaked the address, and Falk touched the routing, then it could've been two people hitting the same network from different sides."

"Possible."

"Or Falk handed the routing out and somebody else took advantage of the panic on the Ostanian side."

"Also possible."

"That is not very useful."

"Neither is false certainty." Sylvia picked up the pages again. "Keep separating the known from the guessed."

The rebuke landed because it was deserved. Anya stood there anyway.

"If you had to make the call now," she said, "would you pull me off the desk?"

Sylvia took longer than she needed to answer.

"If I wanted to punish you, yes," she said. "If I want the damage mapped before whoever did this moves again, no." She set the pages down for the second time. "So I am not punishing you today. I am using you. Try to deserve the distinction."

That should have felt merciful. It did not.

Anya went back to the terminal and kept working until the words on the screen turned liquid around the edges. At 16:40 she sent Sylvia the first clean reconstruction: the lie she filed, the downgrade it triggered, the coverage cuts that followed, the specific window Crane inherited, the points where uncertainty still started. She left the building after dark with the route map burned behind her eyes and the smell of dead coffee in her coat.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

Damian took the long way home.

He walked east through Berlint's commercial district past shuttered storefronts, delivery trucks, and cafes rolling up awnings for a day that had no idea what had happened. The air bit harder without his coat. He had left it in the basement. He did not go back.

Mertens had daughters. Eight and ten. Damian had never asked their names. That had felt prudent. Now it felt cheap. He knew about the football instead. He knew about the gym bag. He knew Mertens took the long ramp into the ministry garage because the short stairs hurt his knee in winter. Useless information, except it was not. It was the part that kept exposure from staying abstract.

By the time he reached his building, rain had started. He let himself in, locked the door, and stood in the entryway listening to the refrigerator hum and the pipes settle. Wet footprints spread on the tile. The apartment looked half inhabited, as it always did when he was too tired to pretend otherwise. Books on the floor. One lamp on. Cold air leaking out of the bedroom because he had left the window cracked again.

He emptied his pockets on the kitchen counter. House key. Basement key. Two coins. A tram ticket gone soft with rain. Mertens's number on a folded scrap of paper.

He looked at the number for a moment, then tore it into four pieces and dropped them into the sink.

The box was in the closet on the top shelf, behind a stack of diplomatic histories he kept meaning to finish and never did. He had opened it once since moving in, late at night after a dinner where he had smiled through opinions that made him want to break the glass in his own hand. He had looked at the photo then and put it back. This time he took the whole box down and set it on the bed.

It held the usual things. A school blazer he had outgrown at thirteen, folded badly. A note from his mother, the ink almost gone. A runner-up certificate from a math competition. Three pens his father had given him for the entrance exams. Second place had always been the safe result in his house. Not good enough to display. Too good to throw away.

And the class photo.

He sat on the edge of the bed with it in both hands. Standard group portrait. Teacher at the side, already tired. Second row, third from the left. Pink hair. Wide grin. Uniform rumpled. Looking somewhere off camera like whatever was there was more interesting than the photographer. Small enough that somebody had probably told her where to stand twice. She was probably seven.

He was not looking for proof. He already had that. Becky's drunken sentence at the gala had closed the last inch of distance. The photo made the certainty worse, not clearer.

Anya Forger.

The problem now was not whether he was right. The problem was what right meant.

The woman at the state dinner had been Anya Forger. The cultural attache who found him too quickly. The woman who stepped into an ambush at exactly the right second. The woman who listened with her whole body and somehow still always seemed half a beat ahead of the words coming out of his mouth.

Did that mean Westalis had put someone near him that early? No. Start over. That did not hold. She had been a child. He had been a child. She had punched him during dodgeball because he had called her a freak and deserved what came after. Nobody spent years cultivating the second son of a family not yet worth the trouble.

The cover could have come later. The family could have been a cover too. Or neither was true. He could not prove any of that from one photo and a stack of memories that refused to behave like evidence.

The memories themselves were insultingly ordinary. Half a peanut butter sandwich shoved at him after Emile stole his lunch. Anya calling a teacher unfair to his face and then getting detention for it. The way she laughed without checking first whether it was allowed. He remembered her getting bored during assemblies and kicking the back of his chair until he finally turned around to glare at her. He remembered the specific satisfaction of glaring at her when he was eight, as if outrage were a kind of rank.

Childhood had not felt like tradecraft. It had felt like childhood. Messy, mean, brief, real.

He remembered the safe house too, in ugly detail. Antiseptic on the cut above his eyebrow. Her fingers cold from rinsing out a washcloth. The way she said sorry like she meant the rib and not everything else. The kiss. There was no point pretending he had forgotten the kiss. There was even less point pretending he had understood it.

Was any of it real?

The girl in the photo was. The woman in the basement had been too. When she said she had filed him wrong, there had been no performance in it. He knew performance. He had been raised on it. He also knew what a person looked like when she was trying to build a complete theory of disaster out of whatever sharp parts were nearest to hand.

What unsettled him was not only that she had lied. He lived among lies. He produced enough of them himself to have lost the right to be shocked on principle. What unsettled him was the shape of her timing. She moved toward danger before warnings fully landed. She answered questions he had not quite asked. She knew where the pressure was before anyone in the room admitted there was pressure at all. At the state dinner he had called it intuition. At the safe house he had called it field instinct. At the gala he had stopped trying to name it because every name he had sounded insufficient.

That still was not proof of anything except that she remained a problem he could not diagram.

He set the photo down long enough to close the bedroom window. Rain tapped once against the glass and slid away. The room cooled immediately anyway. When he came back to the bed, the photo waited where he had left it, pink hair bright even in bad grayscale.

He had been trained from childhood to treat attachment as exposure. Standing in the kitchen with the basement key in his hand, he tried on the neat answer: cut Anya loose, bury whatever was left of the network, and call it prudence. Crane stayed dead in that version too. Mertens stayed exposed. The mole kept breathing.

He dropped the key into the back of the kitchen drawer under a roll of string and three dead batteries. There would be no more fixed rooms. Nothing on paper would sleep behind walls again. If anything passed between them now, it passed hand to hand or not at all.

Then he went back to the bed, slid the class photo under his mother's note, and shut the lid. He would keep working with her. He would not hand her the photo. Not yet.

He put the box back on the shelf behind the unread histories. Rain tapped once against the shut window. The apartment stayed cold.


# Chapter 14

Three days after David Crane died at the Kreuzfeld border checkpoint, Anya stood outside the apartment door with the key in her hand and thought, not for the first time, that he had no business being in this hallway on a Tuesday night. He was there anyway. The apartment smelled of sesame oil and sugar catching at the bottom of a pan. Sylvia's voice: *We lost Crane at the checkpoint.* The checkpoint lights. The gap in the coverage pattern Anya had helped cut open with a lie.

Through the door she heard Bond's nails on hardwood and the low murmur of the radio Loid always kept on in the evenings. Yor moved in the kitchen. Water ran at the sink. Home sounded normal enough to make her throat close.

She should've called. There was a protocol for this. When an operation went bad and you needed a handler outside the chain, you filed a contact request and waited for a secure window. You did not disappear for three days and then show up at your parents' apartment at nine on a Tuesday with your hands shaking and a dead man's name lodged behind your teeth.

She let herself in.

Loid was at the sink in an apron, sleeves pushed to the elbows. Yor had the refrigerator door open and a bottle of sauce in one hand. Bond thumped once from the living room. For a second nobody had turned yet, and Anya saw the evening as it had been before she came in: dinner half finished, radio low, the kind of ordinary hour Crane would never get back.

"Anya!" Yor shut the refrigerator too fast. "You should've called. I would've made more rice. Have you eaten? Sit down. Wait, no, you look cold. Sit down first and then tell me if you've eaten."

Bond lumbered in from the living room and shoved his head against Anya's hip hard enough to make her stumble. The thought from him knocked into her in three blunt beats. *Home. Her. Good.* She bent to scratch behind his ears because it gave her one extra second before she had to speak.

Loid turned. His face stayed easy. The quick check behind it did not.

*She came alone. No call. Her hands. How bad.*

His gaze dropped to the white grip of her fingers around the strap of her bag.

"How's work?" he asked.

It was the kind of question a father asked at a kitchen table. It was also cover for *tell me what room we're in*.

"Papa." Her voice came out rough. She cleared it. "I need to talk to you."

The reading glasses came off his head. That was the only large movement. The rest happened in smaller places: the change in his stance, the way his attention locked, the quiet that settled over him without touching his face.

Yor set the sauce bottle down. "Anya?"

"They killed Crane," Anya said.

The kitchen went still.

Loid crossed to the radio and switched it off. The weather report died in the middle of a sentence. Yor pulled out a chair without taking her eyes off Anya.

"Sit," Loid said.

Anya sat because her knees had already started to shake. Bond pressed against her leg. Yor filled a glass from the sink and put it in front of her. Anya wrapped both hands around it and did not drink.

"When?" Loid asked.

"Three mornings ago. Before dawn. Kreuzfeld checkpoint."

"You were there?"

"No." The word scraped. "I got the call after."

Yor sat too, hands folded tight in her lap. "Anya, what happened?"

She had spent the train ride over telling herself she would say it cleanly. Just facts. Just what they needed. The facts came out anyway, but not cleanly.

"Crane was working a courier line tied to summit prep. Not official prep. Off-book movement, procurement chatter, access schedules that shouldn't have been on the same route. Somebody burned the checkpoint before first light. He didn't make it through. The line went dark after."

Loid's eyes did not leave her face. "You think the checkpoint hit and the summit are one operation."

"I know they touch." She swallowed. The water in the glass trembled against her fingers. "Renewal Front isn't waiting anymore. They've got help inside both governments and they're tightening the timetable. The summit's not a pressure tactic now. It's the stage."

"What kind of hit?" he asked.

"Public. Fast. Something that reads Westalian before anybody can get in front of it."

Yor made a small sound and looked down at the table as if she could see the conference center there in the wood grain.

Loid said, "How much of this does WISE know?"

"Pieces. Border breach. Dead courier. Noise in the procurement channels." She forced herself to meet his eyes. "Not the whole shape. Not from me."

He waited a beat.

"Why not from you?"

Because she had lied once already. Because there were forms with her name on them and one false line still spreading rot through rooms she could not see. Because Sylvia had trusted her and Crane was dead and every hour since the call had sounded like blame.

What came out was smaller and truer in a different direction.

"Because there's a mole in WISE and I still can't name them. If I push the wrong packet through the wrong desk, we lose whatever's left."

Loid did not nod. He did not reassure her either. He just held the line of the question.

"And that is the only reason?"

Anya looked at the water.

"No."

Yor leaned forward a little. "Honey."

"I kept thinking I could get one more clean piece," Anya said. "One thing nobody could argue with. One thing that would make it look less like panic and more like a case file. I kept thinking if I came to you with enough in my hand I could do this right, and every time I had something new it still wasn't enough."

The kitchen stayed quiet.

Loid's thoughts came through in jagged, disciplined fragments.

*No sleep. Guilt first, then tradecraft. Not useful. Fix that. Summit schedule. Venue ingress. Who else has seen her working this line.*

"You should've come sooner," he said.

"I know."

It did not feel like absolution. It was not meant to.

Yor reached across the table and touched Anya's wrist, just once. "Did you eat anything today?"

Anya shook her head.

"That wasn't the question," Loid said, still looking at her. "What else are you not saying?"

Her stomach dropped hard enough to hurt.

"Some of the sourcing."

"Whose?"

"Mine."

His expression did not change, but he heard the dodge for what it was. Anybody would have. Anya could feel the lie-shaped absence sitting on the table between the glasses and the soy sauce and the half-made dinner.

"Back channel?" he asked.

"Yes."

"Is it compromised?"

"Not yet."

"Can you prove that?"

"No."

Yor's fingers tightened on her wrist, then loosened. "Is that the part that's dangerous to say here?"

Anya let out a breath that shook on the way out. "Partly. Partly it's just..." She looked away. "If I start on every wrong thing in the right order, we're going to be here all night and the summit's tomorrow."

Loid watched her for one more second.

"Is what you're holding back the difference between stopping this and not stopping it?"

"No."

"Is it the difference between whether I can trust the summit threat?"

"No."

"Then I can leave it alone for tonight."

He said it flat, as a temporary operational choice and nothing kinder than that. The mercy in it made her throat burn worse.

"Tell me the solid part," he said. "Just the part you can stand behind."

So she did.

She told them about the opening session being the obvious window because the cameras would already be live and the delegations trapped in place. She told them there had to be an internal relay point inside the building because no exterior team could keep a line clean through the first minutes of panic without help from the floor. She told them the target was still missing, that she could not yet tie the shooters to one named unit, that the operation depended on making the blame legible before the facts were.

She did not tell them about telepathy. She did not tell them about the false report sitting in the WISE system like a planted fault line. She did not tell them that every useful thread she'd pulled in the last three days had started with a thought she'd stolen off somebody else's mind.

Loid interrupted only to narrow things down.

"Opening session, not lunch."

"Yes."

"Gunfire or explosive?"

"Gunfire first, I think. Something directional. Something camera-readable."

"Do you know who the inside relay serves?"

"No. Just that they're feeding somebody on the floor."

"Do you know the route from relay to shooters?"

"No."

"Do you know where Voss will position himself?"

"Not yet."

That got his eyes up.

"You think Voss is close enough to the action to matter."

"I think he wouldn't build this and then miss the first five minutes."

Yor had gone pale in a very steady way. Not shock anymore. Recognition moving into place.

*Dominic's office. The manifests. The watermark.*

She stood up so abruptly her chair legs scraped the floor.

"I have something," she said.

Loid turned to her. "Yor?"

She was already moving into the hall. Anya heard the closet door, the quick shift of boxes, paper against paper. Yor came back carrying a thick manila envelope and set it on the table between the soy sauce and the stack of plates.

"I should've shown you sooner too," she said. "I kept waiting until I understood exactly what I was looking at, and I don't. Not all of it. But I know enough."

She opened the envelope. Grainy photographs slid across the wood. Shipping manifests. Procurement records. A photographed purchase order with the Garden watermark at the top and three completed eliminations listed below it.

Loid picked up the manifests first. His eyes moved fast.

*Voss. Civilian intermediaries. Defense procurement. This is the supply chain.*

"Where did you get these?" he asked.

"A job last month. Karl Dominic. The broker who handled some of the off-books movement." Yor kept her voice even by effort, not ease. "He had them in his office safe and on his desk. Not locked separately. Like he'd gotten sloppy or like he thought nobody around him knew what they were."

Loid's hand stopped on the Garden sheet. He looked at her. She met his eyes before he could ask.

"The Garden took contracts for them," she said. "Not officially. Through cutouts. They marked people as liabilities in the chain and sent me after them."

Behind the words came the sentence she was not saying aloud.

*I killed them and never asked who was paying.*

Anya looked at the list again. Three dates. Three names. Three neat entries sitting on the kitchen table while Crane's death still burned at the edge of every thought she had.

Yor touched the page with one finger.

"I know this watermark. Not from city hall paper, not from anything ordinary. This is ours. Internal. The same mark that's on assignment sheets when they think nobody outside the shop will ever see them." Her mouth tightened. "I copied it before I left Dominic's office. He was on the floor bleeding through the carpet and I stood there with his camera in my hand taking photographs because I knew if I came home with nothing but a feeling I'd talk myself out of it by morning."

Loid did not move.

"How many?" he asked.

"Three on this sheet. More in the manifests. I don't know the full count."

"How long have you known?"

"Since Dominic. Since I saw the records." She folded her hands together to stop them from shaking. "Long enough to know I'm not going back."

He did not rush her.

"Say that again."

Yor blinked once. Not because she had not heard him. Because she understood exactly why he was making her do it.

"I'm not going back," she said. "If they send a handler, I won't answer. If they leave instructions, I won't take them. I'll help with the summit, but not as Thorn Princess. Not as their asset." She looked at Anya first, then at Loid. "As Yor Forger. As your family."

That was all. She didn't make it a speech, and she didn't apologize. The break sat in the room with its full weight.

Loid reached across the table and covered her hand with his.

"All right," he said.

Yor turned her hand under his and held on.

Anya looked down at Bond's head against her knee. His fur smelled like dust and soap and the apartment itself. Safe things. Crane had had safe things too. Everybody did, right up until the moment they did not.

Loid let the silence sit for a breath, then another, then shifted back to the table because that was what he did when the wall was already on top of them.

"Your documents corroborate the procurement chain," he said to Yor. Then to Anya: "Your summit read gives it a clock. We can work with that."

He pulled one photograph free and flattened it with his palm. "We still need floor placement, leadership seating, and whatever Voss thinks his deniability looks like tomorrow."

Anya lifted her head. "Damian can get that."

The kitchen changed again. Not as sharply as it had on Crane's name, but enough.

Yor looked between them. "Damian."

"The ministry diplomat?" Loid asked.

"Yes."

He leaned back a fraction. "Why him."

Not who is he. Not what do you feel. Just the operational knife straight through the middle.

"Because he was hit too," Anya said. "The same morning Crane died, somebody raided one of his caches. One of his people went to ground. He has his own line into this and whoever's building the summit attack wants him blind almost as badly as they want us blind."

Loid said nothing.

She kept going because stopping now would sound weaker than the truth was.

"He can get close to Voss. He can get party communications and interior movement faster than I can from my side. He already knows enough about the operation to matter and shutting him out doesn't make him safer. It just means he does tomorrow alone and we do it without half the floor."

Yor asked quietly, "Do you trust him?"

Anya stared at the edge of the Garden sheet.

"Not cleanly," she said.

That got Loid's attention in a way a prettier answer would not have.

"But enough?"

"Enough that when he had chances to burn me, he didn't. Enough that if he says Voss is in one gallery instead of another, I believe him." She looked up. "He doesn't know about you two. He knows me and the summit. That's all."

Loid's face gave away nothing. His thoughts did not.

*Useful. Risky. Young. Attached to her in some fashion. Possibly vice versa. Separate problem. Later.*

Anya wanted the floor to open.

"If he comes here," Loid said, "he comes because we need what he knows, not because you're asking me to take him on faith."

"That's what I'm saying."

Yor looked at the family photos on the hall wall and then back at Anya. "If he helps stop this tomorrow, I don't need faith. I need to know what we're asking him to walk into."

Anya looked at her phone.

"A summit attack," she said. "And probably the end of whatever cover he's still got left."

Yor nodded once, accepting the shape of it.

Loid picked up the Garden sheet again, then set it down. Decision made.

"Text him," he said. "If he's coming into this room, I want him here before we lock assignments."

Anya sent the message under the table because it made her feel less exposed even though both of them obviously knew what she was doing.

`Need you at my parents'. Tonight. Summit.`

The reply came back before she could lower the phone.

`On my way.`

Loid saw the light in her hand. "Fast."

"He was awake."

"Of course he was," Loid said.

Yor stood and went automatically toward the stove. "Everybody should eat before we start moving maps around. Thinking is worse on an empty stomach. I know that's not the biggest problem in the room, but it's still true."

Nobody argued hard enough to stop her.

She spooned rice into bowls with the same steady hands she had used to lay out proof that she'd been murdering the wrong men for the wrong people. Loid moved aside the papers just long enough to make room. Bond followed the smell and parked himself under the table with the solemn patience of a dog who believed all crises ended in dropped food.

Anya got three bites down because Yor was watching and because Loid said, "Two more," in the exact tone that meant he had already decided this counted as medicine.

The rice tasted like sesame and old metal.

No one filled the silence with anything fake. Yor apologized once for overcooking the vegetables, realized what room she was in, and stopped. Loid ate standing up, reading the manifest photographs between bites. Anya watched steam go thin over the bowls and tried not to listen to the room harder than the room was speaking.

It did not work.

*Whiteboard. Venue plan. No outside calls yet. Need clean paper.*

*Knife case in the hall closet. Flats, not heels. If they call from the shop, don't answer. Don't answer.*

*Eat. Keep chewing. Don't think about the checkpoint. Don't think about Sylvia saying his name. Don't think about Crane in the cold.*

She put the chopsticks down before she snapped them.

Loid noticed at once. "Enough?"

She nodded.

He took the bowls to the sink, ran water over them, and transformed the kitchen by stages. Plates stacked. Sauce bottle moved. Whiteboard lifted off the side wall and leaned beside the refrigerator. Venue map up with two magnets shaped like oranges and one shaped like a pear. Yor's photographs aligned on the table. Pens uncapped. The ordinary planning board Bond occasionally stared at while waiting for dinner turned into a war map in under a minute.

By the time the rice went cold on the counter, the kitchen looked less like dinner and more like evidence.

"Opening session is still the obvious window," Loid said. He marked the south terrace doors, then the east service corridor, then the west leadership row. "They need visibility and clean attribution. If the first footage reads Westalian, the correction loses the first hour and maybe the day after."

"The south side is theatrical," Yor said. She had not touched the tea in front of her. "Good sight lines. Room for panic. Bad if you want to keep the shooters alive."

Loid gave her a look.

She returned it, unoffended. "I'm only saying the plan values drama."

He tapped the map with the red pen. "Relay point is here or near here. Interior enough to push coordinates, close enough to the floor to watch timing, not obvious enough for venue staff to notice the same face twice."

Anya did not mean to read ahead. She just caught it because it was there, because Loid thought in route lines and risk blocks, because the room kept dropping usable things into her lap like knives.

*If Yor starts on the terrace and the team comes through the corridor first, she loses the angle.*

"Foyer junction," Anya said. "If Mama starts there, she can cover the west approach and still get south."

Both of them looked at her.

Too fast. Again.

Loid asked, "Why."

"Because the terrace is too open from the start."

"That isn't an answer."

She gripped the back of the chair. "Because from the junction she gets both lines until contact."

Loid stared at the map. "And loses the terrace for the first stretch of panic."

"Not all of it."

"Enough of it."

Yor leaned forward and measured distance with two fingers. *Forty seconds if the heels are wrong. Less without them.* "If I start inside and move on the first break, I can make it. Thirty-five, maybe forty."

Anya heard the unspoken part and said it before she could stop herself.

"Go in flats first."

Yor's head came up.

Anya wished very sincerely to stop doing this in front of other people forever.

"I was only thinking," she said too quickly. "Because running."

Yor still looked at her for one beat too long, then back at the map. "Yes. Flats first."

Loid's pen hovered.

*How is she getting there before I say it. Pattern. Later.*

He tapped the south side again. "Even with flats, forty seconds is too long if the first burst comes from outside."

The room tightened. Anya had solved nothing. She had just dragged an unspoken route into the open and proven it had a hole in it.

Good. Great. Excellent use of telepathy. Steal the thought, say it too early, still be wrong.

They reworked the lines once. Twice. Loid moved Yor back toward the terrace. Yor pushed herself toward the junction again. The map gathered arrows without getting clearer. The more useful Anya tried to be, the more she heard how much she was taking.

Bond had been asleep through most of it. Then he stood so abruptly the nails of his front paws scraped the floor.

Yor half rose. "Bond?"

He crossed the living room fast, faster than an old dog with stiff hips had any right to move, and planted himself in front of the refrigerator. He barked once and shoved his nose against the lower edge of the map.

Anya was already reading him. She could not help it.

Low wall. Wet stone. Grass crushed under hard shoes. Men in dark clothes coming fast along the edge. Light flashing on glass. Heat. Noise. Not words, never words, but a future with teeth.

Bond barked again, sharper this time.

Loid followed the line of his muzzle. "South perimeter."

The place Bond had pinned was outside the south terrace, where the landscaped grounds narrowed near the vehicle approach.

"That's not random," Anya said.

Loid looked at her, then at Bond, then back to the map. A different memory moved through his thoughts.

*Government event. Weeks ago. She pushed me away from it without telling me why.*

He did not ask the question. He only circled the south perimeter in red.

"Then the exterior route is real," he said. "Staging or extraction. Either way it matters."

Yor stood and came to the refrigerator. She measured from the foyer junction to the south terrace with two fingers again, then from the junction to the west side.

"If they're coming along the low wall, I shouldn't start on the terrace," she said. "I start inside, stay out of sight, and move south on contact. I can take the first man at the bend before the terrace line sees me."

Loid nodded once. "Better."

It had taken a failed suggestion, Bond's panic, and a future full of wet stone to get there. The map looked cleaner now, but not because Anya had plucked the perfect answer whole out of someone's head and handed it back. They had missed, adjusted, and paid for the adjustment with time and nerves. The knot under her ribs loosened by almost nothing.

Bond pressed closer to the refrigerator, the vision fading now into the simpler distress of a dog who knew only that the place on the map was bad.

Anya crouched and put her arms around his neck. "Good boy," she said into his fur. "I know."

Loid set the red marker down and recapped it with a click.

"You're not on the south side," he said without looking at her.

Anya straightened. "I know that."

"You're not on first contact at all."

"Papa."

Now he looked at her.

"No," he said. "We've got enough unknowns. I need one variable in this apartment that doesn't get shot at."

Yor looked from one to the other and did not intervene yet.

Anya felt the heat come up her neck.

"You need somebody on the floor who can tell when it turns," she said. "Not after. Before."

"Somebody," Loid said, "does not have to be you."

The sentence landed harder because he said it like a handler and a father at the same time and did not bother separating the two.

"I know the players better than anybody you're going to place in a corridor," she said.

"That still doesn't make you expendable."

Yor set her mug down very carefully. "She's not saying expendable."

Loid's jaw tightened.

"Then what is she saying."

Yor answered before Anya could. "That if the room shifts before the shooting starts, she'll see it faster than I will from a junction and faster than you will from a relay."

He looked at Yor.

She met it calmly. Not social-flustered Yor, not Thorn Princess exactly either. The third voice. The one that came out only when family and danger sat in the same chair.

"I don't like it," she said. "I still think she's right."

Loid's thoughts flickered in hard, precise lines.

*No open floor. Not after Crane. Not with the line already burned. Find another way.*

The doorbell had not rung yet, but Anya could already feel the shape of the answer coming up the stairwell in a damp coat with its thoughts pushed into neat stacks.

She texted Damian a second line.

`Need seating and Voss placement. Also press if possible.`

His reply took longer this time.

`Understood. Five minutes.`

At twenty past ten, his thoughts reached the stairwell before the bell did.

*You can walk into a kitchen. You can brief a family. Crane is dead, Reinhardt is in hiding, and you can still say hello like a person. Do not look at the photographs. Do not think Eden. West Gallery. South perimeter. Keep it on the summit.*

The bell rang.

Yor got there first. "Hello. You must be Damian. Come in. It's awful out there. Do you want tea? We have tea. And food. Please ignore the maps."

"Thank you, Mrs. Forger."

"Yor," she said at once. "Mrs. Forger sounds like somebody who sends complaint letters."

That got a surprised breath of laughter out of him. Not much, but enough for Anya to hear how tired he really was.

He stepped into the kitchen damp from the rain, coat dark at the shoulders, hair flattened at the temples. He looked like he had been sleeping badly in clean shirts. When his eyes found Anya, the next thought came too fast and too polished.

*Tea. Maps. Hallway photos. Don't. West Gallery. Keep it on the summit.*

There it was again. It wasn't a second layer or a hidden room, just a clean, deliberate choice to put one thought in front of another and lock the door behind it.

"Hi," she said.

"Hi."

Loid came out of the hall wiping his hands on the apron. "Damian. I'm Loid."

The handshake lasted the exact right length. Loid's smile stayed easy. Behind it, Anya caught the fast inventory click into place.

*Good suit. No aide. Diplomat's manners, no sleep. He looked at her first. Personal, then.*

Anya wanted to die on the spot.

"Thank you for having me, Mr. Forger," Damian said.

"Loid is fine."

Yor pressed a mug into Damian's hand before anybody else could choose a seat. "Anywhere except that chair. Bond claims it."

Bond, who apparently claimed everyone now, approached Damian with solemn attention, inspected his shoes, inspected his coat hem, then turned once and settled on Damian's feet with a noise of total surrender.

Damian went still.

*The dog has me pinned. I have negotiated with ministers under less pressure than this.*

It hit Anya wrong and almost made her laugh. Crane was dead. The kitchen table was covered in conspiracy paperwork. Her mother had just quit an assassination organization. And still there was Bond, flattening a diplomat by the ankles.

"He likes you," Yor said warmly.

Damian looked down at the dog and then around the kitchen. He left the top layer sitting right out in the open.

*This is real. The radio. The dog hair. Fruit magnets on a war map. She comes from this. No wonder she fights like someone protecting a house, not a flag.*

Loid did not move back to the refrigerator yet.

"Before we go any further," he said, "tell me why you're here."

Damian looked at him over the rim of the tea mug.

"Because the same people who killed Crane hit my side before lunch," he said. No diplomatic padding. None of his usual preamble. "One of my caches is gone. Reinhardt's underground. If I keep pretending tomorrow is still a political event with a security problem, more people die."

Loid said nothing.

Damian set the mug down.

"And because Anya asked."

There. Terrible. Mortifying. Fully on the table.

Loid's thoughts sharpened.

*Too honest on the second point. Good. Or dangerous. Same skillset.*

"If I think you're protecting your own position at her expense," Loid said, "this conversation gets short."

Damian nodded once. "Fair."

Yor, apparently satisfied that the men had done whatever strange animal test that counted for them, set a plate of reheated vegetables on the table.

"Please eat something," she said. "Operations improve when people chew. I don't know if that's an official intelligence principle, but it should be."

"Thank you," Damian said, and because he was either too polite or too well trained to refuse her, he sat down and took the plate.

Then Yor, trying to be kind, said, "Anya barely touched dinner when she was small unless I told her there was dessert waiting. She used to bargain like a union organizer."

Damian's gaze flicked once toward the hallway where framed family photos hung. Then the next four thoughts came up in a hard, neat stack.

*West Gallery. Voss. South perimeter. Ask about seating.*

He smiled at Yor with perfect politeness. "Can we go over the summit first? I brought the delegation update."

The swerve was so clean Anya almost missed it. But she saw the glance at the hall. She heard the swap. Harmless thoughts lifted into place where a riskier one had almost gone.

She could read the dodge. She could not read what he had dodged. He knew a piece of her life he was refusing to hold still long enough for her to catch. That was all she had. The rest was inference.

Loid was already back at the refrigerator. Father mode had retreated without disappearing. "Walk us through it."

Damian set the fork down untouched and stepped closer to the map. The whole room shifted with him. He stopped being the wet young man in the doorway and became what he had always actually been underneath the diplomatic polish: a trained political operator with nowhere safe to put the truth except work.
